Generalized myoclonic (3.3.1)Brief (<100 ms) bilateral symmetric jerks; usually upper limbs/face; consciousness preserved; "lightning jerks"Polyspike or polyspike-wave; time-locked to jerksJME, PME, Dravet, MAEPHT/CBZ/OXC may worsen; VPA/LEV/ZNS preferred
Generalized tonic (3.3.3)Sudden bilateral sustained muscle contraction; axial/proximal; brief (5–20 s); falls common; nocturnal in LGSFast 10–25 Hz activity ("tonic seizure pattern") or generalized paroxysmal fast activity (GPFA)Lennox-Gastaut syndrome, DravetAvoid AEDs that may precipitate tonic SE (CBZ, OXC, vigabatrin)
Generalized negative myoclonus (3.3.4)Brief interruption of tonic posture (>200 ms EMG silence); looks like mini-atonic drops; consciousness usually preservedGeneralized polyspike-wave burst; EMG silence 200–400 ms after dischargeLGS atonic variants, epileptic encephalopathiesNEW 2025; distinguish from atonic seizure by EMG timing
Generalized atonic (3.3.6)Sudden loss of postural tone; "drop attacks"; head drop or falls; very brief (1–2 s); high injury risk; NO loss of consciousness typicallySlow spike-wave or paroxysmal fast activity; brief high-amplitude discharge followed by EMG silenceLGS (atonic = "astatic"), Doose (myoclonic-atonic)VPA/LTG/CLB/RFN; ketogenic diet for refractory
Epileptic spasms (3.3.5)Brief (1–2 s) axial flexion ± extension; clusters on awakening; infantile spasms = West syndrome; developmental regressionHypsarrhythmia (interictal); electrodecremental response (ictal)West syndrome; structural, genetic, metabolic causesACTH/vigabatrin 1st line; consider TSC-specific (everolimus)
Typical Absence vs. FIC (FIA) — Critical Clinical Trap
FeatureTypical Absence (GE)Focal Impaired Consciousness (FIC/FIA)
Duration3–20 seconds30 seconds – 3 minutes
Onset/offsetAbrupt (like a switch)Gradual onset; post-ictal confusion
Post-ictal stateNONE — immediate returnConfusion, fatigue, often minutes–hours
AutomatismsSimple (eyelid flutter, lip-smacking, minor hand)Complex (hand fumbling, oroalimentary, gestural)
PrecipitantsHyperventilation (87%); photic in someNone specific; may be sleep-related
EEG (ictal)3 Hz bilateral symmetric GSW; background normalRegional theta/slow; temporal or frontal focus
MRINormal (genetic)Often abnormal (hippocampal sclerosis, FCD, tumor)
AgeTypically children 4–14 (CAE); older (JAE)Any age; MTLE commonly adults
ASM choiceETX, VPA, LTGLTG, LEV, OXC, LCM, CBZ
DANGERMisdiagnosing FIC as typical absence → prescribing CBZ/OXC (will not harm but miss focal treatment) OR diagnosing absence as FIC → giving CBZ → absence SE precipitated!
2.5 Differential Diagnosis of Paroxysmal Events

Seizure Mimics — Key Differential Diagnoses

ConditionFeatures Favoring This DiagnosisKey Distinguishing Tests
Convulsive syncopePreceded by lightheadedness, diaphoresis, pallor; myoclonus AFTER loss of consciousness; upward eye deviation; arrhythmic jerks (multifocal, brief 1–15 s); NO post-ictal confusion; NO tongue biting; quick recoveryTilt-table test; ECG/Holter; serum lactate (low in syncope); EEG (normal)
Psychogenic non-epileptic seizure (PNES)Asynchronous movements; pelvic thrusting; eye closure during event; prolonged with fluctuating course; multiple events in short period; stress trigger; no post-ictal; normal EEG during event; no elevation of prolactinVideo-EEG monitoring (gold standard); serum prolactin (elevated 15–20 min post-ictal GTC, normal in PNES)
TIANegative symptoms (loss of function); no spread; older patients; vascular risk factors; usually no jerkingMRI DWI; carotid duplex; cardiac echo; ECG
Migraine with auraVisual aura: gradual spread (march) over minutes, positive (scintillating scotoma) then negative; followed by headache; no LOC; longer duration (20–60 min)Clinical; MRI (normal or white matter); EEG (normal or non-specific)
HypoglycemiaDiaphoresis, tremulousness; can cause focal neurological deficits; responds to glucose; check blood glucose immediatelyGlucose <55 mg/dL; rapid response to IV dextrose; EEG may show diffuse slowing
Sleep disorders (parasomnia)REM sleep behavior disorder: acting out dreams; NREM: sleepwalking (no memory); sleep terrors; occur from sleep; variable EEGPolysomnography (PSG) + video; clinical history
Movement disordersParoxysmal dyskinesias (PKD, PNKD): involuntary movements triggered by movement/caffeine/stress; consciousness preserved; no EEG correlate; duration minutesEEG (normal during event); genetics (PRRT2 for PKD); response to CBZ in PKD

Convulsive Syncope vs. Epileptic Seizure

FeatureConvulsive SyncopeEpileptic Seizure
Onset of myoclonusFollows loss of consciousness (after falling)Immediate (before or with LOC)
Eye deviationUpwardLateral (contralateral to focus)
Myoclonus rhythmArrhythmic jerksRhythmic jerks (clonic)
Myoclonus patternMultifocal; proximal and distal; bilateralUnilateral/asymmetric or bilateral symmetric with evolution
Duration of jerks1–15 seconds30 seconds – 2 minutes
Urinary incontinenceMay occurCommon (especially GTC)
Post-ictal confusionNO — rapid return to normalYES — post-ictal confusion common
Tongue bitingRare, tip of tongueLateral tongue biting (highly specific for GTC)
Serum lactate/CKLow/normalElevated lactate, elevated CK after GTC
Prolactin (15–20 min post)NormalElevated (×2 baseline) after GTC or complex focal; NOT reliable for focal SZ; NOT elevated in absence
Clinical Pearl — PNES Diagnosis: 12% of syncope has "myoclonic jerks" (convulsive syncope) — arrhythmic, multifocal, brief, NO post-ictal. PNES: video-EEG is gold standard; asynchronous movements, eye closure, pelvic thrusting, long duration with waxing-waning, normal ictal EEG during event, normal prolactin. Importantly, PNES and epilepsy COEXIST in 10–30% of patients with confirmed PNES.
2.6 Epilepsy Diagnosis Framework & Investigation
ILAE 2017 Four-Level Diagnostic Framework
LevelCategoriesExamplesClinical Action
1. Seizure type (ILAE 2025)Focal (FPC/FIC/FBTC); Generalized (absence/tonic/clonic/myoclonic/atonic/GTC); Unknown; UnclassifiedFIC with automatisms; Typical absence; GTC with myoclonic onsetAcute management; initial ASM selection
2. Epilepsy typeFocal; Generalized; Combined generalized+focal; UnknownFocal epilepsy (MTLE); Generalized epilepsy (JME); LGS (combined)ASM class: avoid Na+ channel blockers in generalized epilepsy
3. Epilepsy syndromeSpecific defined syndrome (ILAE 2022)CAE, JME, Dravet, LGS, MTLE, West, BECTSyndrome-specific ASM; genetic counseling; prognosis
4. EtiologyStructural, Genetic, Infectious, Metabolic, Immune, UnknownHippocampal sclerosis (structural); SCN1A (genetic); Anti-LGI1 (immune)Disease-specific therapy; surgical candidacy; inheritance risk

Etiology + comorbidities assessed at ALL levels simultaneously. Comorbidities (depression, anxiety, cognitive, sleep) impact quality of life and treatment decisions.

Investigation of New-Onset Seizures

InvestigationIndicationKey Points
EEGAll patients with first seizure or suspected epilepsySensitivity 50% single; up to 80–90% after ≥3 serial EEGs. Epileptiform pattern increases recurrence risk: 30% → 70% in 1st year. Best within 24–48 h of seizure (higher yield). Sleep-deprived EEG or video-EEG for unclear cases.
MRI brainAll patients with new seizure EXCEPT confirmed genetic generalized epilepsy syndrome (e.g., CAE with classic 3 Hz GSW, no focal features)Normal CT misses 12% of abnormalities detected on MRI. Epilepsy protocol: 3T, coronal FLAIR, T1 volumetric, T2 hippocampal sequences. Look for hippocampal sclerosis, FCD, tumors, vascular malformations, heterotopia.
Blood glucose, electrolytes, calcium, MgAll emergency seizure presentationsExclude metabolic causes: hyponatremia, hypoglycemia, hypocalcemia, hypomagnesemia, uremia
Serum prolactinSelected cases: GTC vs. PNESElevated ×2 baseline 15–20 min after GTC or FIC; not useful for absence; not reliable for focal alone; not gold standard
Genetic testingEarly-onset epilepsy, drug-resistant, family history, suspected genetic syndromeChromosomal microarray, gene panel, WES/WGS for epileptic encephalopathies. SCN1A (Dravet), KCNQ2, CDKL5, ARX, MECP2, TSC1/2
Autoimmune panelNew-onset seizures with encephalopathy, rapid progression, CSF pleocytosis, failure to respond to ASMNMDAR, LGI1, CASPR2, GABA-B, AMPAR antibodies (serum + CSF). LGI1: faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS) pathognomonic — treat immunotherapy before widespread epilepsy develops
LP / CSF analysisFever + seizure, altered consciousness, suspected encephalitis or meningitisOpening pressure, cell count, protein, glucose, culture, PCR (HSV, VZV, enterovirus, TB), VDRL, India ink, cytology if malignancy suspected
CT head (acute)Acute setting: rule out hemorrhage, large structural lesion, herniation before LPEmergency use; MRI preferred for epilepsy workup. CT misses small lesions, FCD, HS, vascular malformations
Clinical Pearl — LGI1 Antibody Epilepsy: Faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS) = brief unilateral arm ± face tonic/dystonic posturing, <3 sec, very frequent (>100/day), consciousness preserved. This is PATHOGNOMONIC for LGI1 autoimmune encephalitis. Treat with immunotherapy (steroids ± IVIG) BEFORE widespread seizures and memory loss develop — ASMs alone are largely ineffective for FBDS. Anti-LGI1 is also associated with hyponatremia and SIADH.
2.7 First Unprovoked Seizure — Management
Reference: Krumholz A, et al. "Evidence-based guideline: Management of an unprovoked first seizure in adults." Neurology 2015;84:1705–13. AAN/AES guideline.
First Seizure Management Algorithm
StepActionKey Points
1. Is it a seizure?Distinguish epileptic seizure from mimics (syncope, PNES, TIA, parasomnia)Obtain detailed history from patient AND witness; ask about: prodrome, onset, evolution, post-ictal state, duration
2. Is it the first seizure?Ask about prior events: myoclonic jerks on awakening, nocturnal tongue biting, brief staring spellsOften the "first" GTC is actually not the first seizure — prior absence/myoclonic/focal SZ may have been missed
3. Provoked or unprovoked?Assess for metabolic, toxic, structural (acute), or infectious precipitantsProvoked = treat underlying cause; does NOT meet epilepsy criteria; recurrence risk not increased by prior SZ count within 24h
4. Assess recurrence riskAAN 2015 factors that INCREASE recurrence riskPrior brain insult (Level A); EEG epileptiform (Level A); Significant MRI abnormality (Level B); Nocturnal seizure (Level B)
5. Treat or observe?Immediate AED reduces 2-year recurrence risk; does NOT change long-term prognosis (>3 years)AED treatment: side effects 7–31% (mild/reversible); shared decision-making; consider driving, work, social factors

Recurrence Risk after First Seizure

Scenario2-Year Recurrence Risk
1st unprovoked seizure (overall)21–45% (greatest within first 2 years)
With prior brain insult (remote symptomatic)~65%
With epileptiform EEG~65% (recurrence risk doubles from 30% → 70%)
With both EEG abnormality + prior insult~85%
2nd unprovoked seizure76% chance of 3rd seizure
3rd unprovoked seizure86% chance of next seizure (72% in children)
Provoked seizure onlyDoes NOT increase long-term recurrence risk
Clinical Pearl — Immediate AED after First Seizure: Immediate treatment REDUCES 2-year recurrence risk but does NOT improve long-term (≥3 year) seizure-free rates or prognosis. Therefore, the decision to treat must weigh: (1) risk of seizure recurrence and its consequences (driving, occupation, injury), (2) side effects (7–31%, usually mild/reversible), (3) patient preference. A 60-year-old with post-stroke first seizure + MRI abnormality + epileptiform EEG → strong indication to treat. A healthy 25-year-old with first idiopathic GTC, normal EEG and MRI → may observe with shared decision-making.
2.8 Epilepsy Syndromes — ILAE 2022 Classification
Reference: ILAE 2022 Syndrome Papers. Epilepsia 2022 (multiple papers): Zuberi SR, et al. (Neonatal/Infant); Specchio N, et al. (Childhood); Hirsch E, et al. (Variable-age). ILAE methodology: Specchio N, et al. Epilepsia 2022.

Idiopathic Generalized Epilepsies (IGE) — Comparison

JME — Juvenile Myoclonic Epilepsy (Most Common IGE in Adults)

JME (Janz syndrome): onset 10–24 years; classic triad: (1) myoclonic jerks on awakening → (2) GTC (often awakening, may follow missed sleep) → (3) absence seizures (33%). Precipitants: sleep deprivation, alcohol, stress, menses, photic stimulation. EEG: 4–6 Hz polyspike-wave; normal background. ILAE 2022: "self-limited" syndrome — high responder to VPA (85%); however, VPA avoided in women → use LTG/LEV/ZNS. Lifelong ASM required — 78% relapse after withdrawal. NEVER use CBZ/OXC/PHT/GBP → worsen myoclonus, may precipitate myoclonic SE.

Self-Limited Focal Epilepsies

SyndromeAge OnsetSemiologyEEGPrognosis
SeLECTS (formerly BECT / Rolandic epilepsy)3–13 yr (peak 7–8); M > FFocal oro-facial: unilateral face/lip tingling, dysarthria, hypersalivation; may evolve to GTC; predominantly nocturnal; infrequentHigh-amplitude centrotemporal spikes (rolandic discharge); horizontal dipole; activated by sleep; normal backgroundExcellent — remission by puberty (age 16); ASM not always required; avoid in mild cases
Panayiotopoulos syndrome2–8 yr (peak 4–5)Autonomic seizures: ictal vomiting, pallor, sweating + eye deviation + altered consciousness; prolonged (30+ min); rare GTCS; may mimic encephalopathyOccipital and multifocal spike-waves; shifts between regions; normal backgroundExcellent — self-limited; most remit within 2 years; low seizure frequency; ASM only for frequent/severe
Gastaut syndrome (late-onset childhood occipital)3–16 yrVisual symptoms: amaurosis, phosphenes, visual hallucinations; ictal headache; consciousness preserved; post-ictal migraine-like headacheOccipital sharp-slow waves; blocked by eye openingVariable — may persist; ASM often needed; differentiate from migraine with visual aura

Mesial Temporal Lobe Epilepsy (MTLE)

FeatureDetails
DefinitionMost common adult focal epilepsy; arises from hippocampus/amygdala/entorhinal cortex; most common cause of drug-resistant epilepsy
EtiologyHippocampal sclerosis (most common structural cause); febrile status epilepticus in childhood → hippocampal sclerosis → MTLE in adolescence/adulthood ("dual pathology")
SemiologyEpigastric rising aura (most common aura) → behavioral arrest → oroalimentary automatisms (lip-smacking, chewing) + ipsilateral hand fumbling → post-ictal confusion; déjà vu/jamais vu; fear; olfactory aura if uncal involvement
MRI (epilepsy protocol)Unilateral hippocampal atrophy + T2/FLAIR hyperintensity = hippocampal sclerosis; loss of internal architecture on T1; reduced NAA on MRS
EEGInterictal: unilateral/bilateral anterior temporal spikes (F7/F8, T7/T8). Ictal: rhythmic 5–7 Hz temporal theta
Natural history~60–70% drug-resistant; progressive memory impairment; depression ~50%; significant quality of life impact
SurgeryAnterior temporal lobectomy (ATL): 70% seizure-free at 1 year (NEJM 2001 Wiebe trial); superior to continued medical therapy in drug-resistant MTLE; earlier surgery = better outcomes

Epileptic Encephalopathies

SyndromeAgeSeizure TypesEEGTreatmentPrognosis
West syndrome3–12 mo (peak 4–7 mo)Epileptic spasms (clusters); flexion ± extension; on awakening; developmental regressionHypsarrhythmia (chaotic high-amp, multifocal spike-wave); electrodecremental response ictallyACTH (short-term, 2–4 weeks); Vigabatrin (1st-line if TSC); corticosteroids. Vigabatrin 1st choice in TSC (50% spasm-free)Poor overall (structural causes); 40–60% develop LGS; TSC: better with vigabatrin
Lennox-Gastaut syndrome (LGS)1–8 yr (peak 3–5 yr)Tonic (nocturnal, hallmark), atonic (drop attacks, hallmark), atypical absence; myoclonic; high injury riskSlow spike-wave <2.5 Hz; generalized paroxysmal fast activity (GPFA) 10–25 Hz during tonic SZ; slow/disorganized backgroundVPA, LTG, CLB (rufinamide for drop attacks); felbamate (limited by hepatotoxicity/aplastic anemia); cannabidiol (FDA 2018); dietary (ketogenic); VNS; corpus callosotomy for drop attacksPoor — most drug-resistant; persistent cognitive disability; 5–10% mortality in childhood
Dravet syndromeFirst year of life (peak 5–8 mo)Febrile/afebrile clonic/GTC → myoclonic → focal dyscognitive → atypical absence; fever-sensitive; vaccine-proximateNormal initially → generalized polyspike-wave + multifocal spikes; photosensitive; normal background earlyVPA + clobazam (1st-line); stiripentol + VPA + CLB (EU approved); cannabidiol; fenfluramine (recent). AVOID: CBZ, OXC, PHT, LTG, GBP, VGB (worsen, potential SE)Drug-resistant (~80%); intellectual disability common; SUDEP risk elevated (15–20× normal); ~10–20% mortality by 20 yr
Progressive Myoclonic Epilepsy (PME)Variable (childhood–adult)Action myoclonus, GTC, neurological deteriorationGeneralized spike-wave; giant somatosensory evoked potentials (SEPs)VPA, LEV, ZNS (symptomatic); avoid PHT/CBZ. Specific: N-acetylcysteine (Unverricht-Lundborg); enzyme replacement (Gaucher). Causes: Unverricht-Lundborg, Lafora (PAS+ inclusions), Myoclonic Epilepsy with Ragged Red Fibers (MERRF), NCL, SialidosisProgressive neurological decline; wheelchair-dependent eventually; MERRF: maternal inheritance; Lafora: fatal by 3rd decade
Clinical Pearl — Dravet Syndrome Diagnosis Trap: First seizure in Dravet is often febrile hemiclonic status epilepticus in a previously normal infant, followed by vaccine-proximate seizures (not caused by vaccine — caused by fever from vaccination). Do not give CBZ, OXC, LTG, PHT — these worsen seizures and can precipitate SE. SCN1A mutation identified in ~80% (loss-of-function, de novo). Genetic testing is essential. Stiripentol (only drug specifically for Dravet) + VPA + clobazam triad is standard.
2.9 Anti-Seizure Medications (ASMs) — Pharmacology & Drug Profiles

Mechanisms of Action

TargetMechanismASMs
Na+ channel block (fast inactivation)Stabilize fast-inactivated state; reduce high-frequency firingPHT, CBZ, LTG, OXC, ESL, ZNS, RFN
Na+ channel (slow inactivation)Unique mechanism — enhance slow inactivated state; reduces persistent Na+ currentLCS (lacosamide)
Ca2+ channel (T-type)Block low-voltage activated Ca2+ channels; reduce thalamo-cortical oscillations in absenceETX, ZNS, VPA (partial)
Ca2+ channel (α2δ subunit)Reduce Ca2+ influx at presynaptic terminal; reduce excitatory NT release; NOT for absenceGBP, PGB
GABA-A enhancementBZD: ↑ Cl- channel opening frequency; Barbiturates: ↑ Cl- channel opening durationBZD (LZP, DZP, MDZ, CLB), PHB
GABA reuptake inhibitionBlock GAT-1 transporter → ↑ synaptic GABATGB (tiagabine)
GABA transaminase inhibitionIrreversible GABA-T inhibition → ↑ GABA levelsVGB (vigabatrin)
SV2A modulationSynaptic vesicle protein 2A; modulates vesicle exocytosis; reduces NT release during high-frequency firingLEV, BRV (10× higher SV2A affinity)
AMPA receptor antagonismBlock glutamate AMPA receptors post-synapticallyPER (perampanel), TPM (partial), ZNS (partial)
Multiple targetsNa+, Ca2+, GABA↑, HDAC inhibition (VPA); Na+, Ca2+, GABA↑, CA inhibition, AMPA block (TPM)VPA, TPM, ZNS, LTG

Key ASM Profiles — Board-Level Pharmacology

DrugMechanismDosingPKKey Side EffectsContraindications / AvoidDrug Interactions
Phenobarbital (PHB)GABA-A: ↑ Cl- channel opening durationAdult: 60–240 mg/d OD; Serum: 15–40 mg/Lt½ 72–144 h; bioavail 95%; protein binding 50%; hepatic + 25% renalSedation, memory impairment, fatigue, hyperactivity (children), depression; Dupuytren's contracture, frozen shoulder, plantar fibromatosis; pregnancy cat DNOT effective for absence; sedation limits useStrong CYP inducer (2B6, 2C, 3A4) → ↓ many drugs; renders other AEDs less effective
Phenytoin (PHT)Na+ channel (fast inactivation state)Adult: 200–400 mg/d BID-TID; Serum: 10–20 mg/L (free: 1–2 mg/L); IV loading: 15–20 mg/kg at ≤50 mg/mint½ 9–40 h; zero-order kinetics at therapeutic doses; 90% protein bound; CYP2C9/2C19Gingival hyperplasia, hirsutism, coarsening of facial features, acne, diplopia, ataxia (dose-related), nystagmus, SJS/TEN (HLA-B*1502 Asian population), peripheral neuropathy (chronic), osteoporosis, IV: hypotension + arrhythmia if too fastNOT for absence, myoclonus, JME; avoid in IGE; avoid in DravetCYP inducer + substrate; many interactions; amiodarone/fluoxetine/INH/azoles ↑ PHT toxicity
Carbamazepine (CBZ)Na+ channel (fast inactivation)Adult: 400–1800 mg/d BID-TID; Serum: 4–12 mg/Lt½ 12–17 h (after autoinduction from 36 h); 75–85% bioavail; 75% protein bound; CYP3A4; autoinduction (4–6 weeks)Hyponatremia (SIADH, especially elderly), diplopia, ataxia, drowsiness (dose-related), nausea, SJS/TEN (HLA-B*1502; screen Asian patients), aplastic anemia/agranulocytosis (rare), weight gain, teratogenicityAVOID in IGE (absence, JME, Dravet) — worsens generalized SZ; AVOID in cardiac conduction disordersStrong CYP3A4 inducer; ↓ OCP, warfarin, other AEDs; inhibits own metabolism initially then induces
Valproate/Valproic acid (VPA)Multiple: Na+ channel, ↑GABA (GABA-T inhibit), T-type Ca2+ (partial), HDAC inhibitionAdult: 500–3000 mg/d BID-TID; Serum: 50–100 mg/L; SE: 20–40 mg/kg IVt½ 9–16 h; ~100% bioavail; 90% protein bound; hepatic (β-oxidation, glucuronidation); enzyme INHIBITORWeight gain, tremor (dose-related), hair loss (alopecia, reversible), PCOS, menstrual irregularities, sedation, teratogenicity (NTD risk highest of all AEDs: spina bifida 1–2%, major congenital malformations 10%), hepatotoxicity (especially <2 yr), pancreatitis, thrombocytopenia, hyperammonemic encephalopathyAVOID in women of childbearing age (teratogenicity); avoid in hepatic disease; use with caution in urea cycle disordersEnzyme INHIBITOR: ↑ LTG levels (doubles LTG t½); ↑ PHB; ↑ carbapenems ↓ VPA significantly; drug-drug interactions extensive
Lamotrigine (LTG)Na+ channel + ↓ glutamate/aspartate releaseAdult: 200–400 mg/d BID; SLOW titration essential (risk SJS); Serum: 3–15 mg/Lt½ 24–35 h (alone); 7–10 h (+CBZ); 50–70 h (+VPA); 55% protein bound; glucuronidation (UGT1A4)SJS/TEN (rapid titration, especially with VPA or in children); dizziness, headache, diplopia, insomnia; rash (10%); in JME: may worsen myoclonus at higher dosesCaution in JME (may exacerbate myoclonus at high doses); pregnancy: levels decrease significantly (↑ clearance → dose adjustment required)VPA doubles LTG t½ → start at 25 mg QOD, titrate very slowly; CBZ halves LTG t½; OCP ↓ LTG 50%
Levetiracetam (LEV)SV2A modulationAdult: 1000–3000 mg/d BID; Serum: 12–46 mg/Lt½ 6–8 h; ~100% bioavail; <10% protein bound; 66% renal excretion unchanged; minimal hepatic metabolismIrritability, aggression, mood changes, psychiatric disorders (~15%); somnolence, dizziness; safe in hepatic impairment; dose adjust in renal failure (GFR-based)Psychiatric history: use with caution; add pyridoxine (B6) if behavioral SE in some protocolsMinimal DDI; no enzyme induction/inhibition; safe in complex polypharmacy
Oxcarbazepine (OXC)Na+ channel (MHD active metabolite)Adult: 900–2400 mg/d BID; Serum (MHD): 3–35 mg/Lt½ of MHD: 8–10 h; ~100% bioavail; 40% protein bound (MHD); renal excretion of glucuronideHyponatremia (SIADH, more common than CBZ; 25–30%); diplopia, ataxia, dizziness, headache; SJS (HLA-B*1502 cross-reactivity); less cognitive effects than CBZAVOID in IGE (same as CBZ); monitor Na+ especially elderly; cardiac conductionModerate CYP3A4 inducer; less interactions than CBZ; no autoinduction
Topiramate (TPM)Multiple: Na+ channel, ↑GABA-A, AMPA/kainate block, CA inhibition, Ca2+ channelAdult: 200–400 mg/d BID; Serum: 5–20 mg/L; cognitive effects dose-dependentt½ 19–23 h; 80% bioavail; 15% protein bound; 70% renal unchanged; some hepatic"Dopamax" cognitive impairment (word-finding, mental slowing — dose-related), kidney stones (CA inhibition → ↑ urine pH + ↓ citrate), weight loss, metabolic acidosis, hypohidrosis (children), glaucoma (acute closed-angle, rare), paresthesiasCognitive burden limits use; kidney stone history (relative); avoid in metabolic acidosisModerate enzyme inducer; ↓ OCP efficacy (use barrier method); ↑ PHT at high doses; VPA combination → hyperammonemia
Lacosamide (LCS/LCM)Na+ channel (slow inactivation — unique, complementary to fast-inact blockers)Adult: 200–400 mg/d BID; Serum: 10–20 mg/L; IV: 200–400 mg over 15 mint½ 13 h; ~100% bioavail; <15% protein bound; 40% renal; CYP2C19 minorDizziness, diplopia, nausea, headache; cardiac: PR interval prolongation (avoid in 2nd/3rd degree AV block, SSS); atrial fibrillation/flutter (rare)Cardiac conduction disorders; caution with other PR-prolonging drugsMinimal DDI; no enzyme induction/inhibition; excellent tolerability; IV formulation useful in SE
Ethosuximide (ETX)T-type (low-voltage activated) Ca2+ channel block in thalamusAdult/child: 500–1500 mg/d BID-TID; Serum: 40–100 mg/Lt½ 40–60 h (adults), 30 h (children); ~100% bioavail; low protein binding; hepatic CYP3A4GI (nausea, vomiting, anorexia — start low/slow); headache, hiccups, lethargy; rare: blood dyscrasias, SLE-like syndromeAbsence seizures ONLY; no efficacy for GTC or focal; if patient also has GTC → add VPA or LTGModerate DDI; VPA ↑ ETX levels; PHT ↓ ETX
Zonisamide (ZNS)Na+ channel + T-type Ca2+ + carbonic anhydrase inhibitionAdult: 200–600 mg/d OD; Serum: 10–40 mg/Lt½ 50–68 h; 40–60% bioavail; 50% protein bound; hepatic CYP3A4 (significant inducer effect)Cognitive effects (less than TPM), anorexia/weight loss, kidney stones (CA), somnolence, word-finding difficulty, metabolic acidosis, oligohidrosis (children), rash; sulfonamide allergy (caution)Sulfonamide allergy; renal stones historyCYP3A4 inducers ↓ ZNS significantly; moderate interactions
Clobazam (CLB)1,5-benzodiazepine; GABA-A; less sedating than 1,4-BZDAdult: 20–40 mg/d OD-BID; Child: 0.1–1 mg/kg/dt½ of active metabolite (N-desmethylclobazam): 71–82 h; hepatic CYP2C19/3A4Sedation, behavioral issues; tolerance develops with chronic use; withdrawal syndrome; teratogenicity riskCaution in COPD/sleep apnea; tolerance limits long-term use as sole therapyCYP2C19 inhibitors ↑ CLB; CBD (cannabidiol) ↑ CLB metabolite levels significantly
Brivaracetam (BRV)SV2A (10× higher affinity than LEV); also Na+ channel at higher dosesAdult: 100–200 mg/d BID; IV formulation availablet½ 9 h; ~100% bioavail; ≤20% protein bound; hepatic CYP2C19Fewer psychiatric side effects than LEV (irritability/aggression less common); somnolence, dizziness; nasopharyngitisMay be used when LEV psychiatric SE are problematicCYP2C19 interaction; rifampin ↓ BRV; carbapenems may ↓
Perampanel (PER)AMPA receptor antagonist (competitive, selective)Adult: 8–12 mg/d QHS (titrate weekly by 2 mg); Serum: 180–990 ng/mLt½ 70–110 h; 95–96% protein bound; hepatic CYP3A4Dizziness, somnolence, headache; psychiatric: aggression/hostility/irritability (dose-dependent, BBW); fallsPsychiatric history; caution driving; aggression riskStrong CYP3A4 inducers (CBZ, PHT, PHB) ↓ PER 50–67% → may need higher dose; ↓ OCP efficacy at ≥12 mg/d

ILAE Evidence-Based Efficacy Review (Marson 2013, Epilepsia)
IndicationLevel ALevel BLevel C
Adult partial-onset (focal) SZCBZ, LEV, PHT, ZNSVPAGBP, LTG, OXC, PHB, TPM
Elderly adults — partial-onset SZGBP, LTG—CBZ
Adults — GTC——CBZ, LTG, OXC, PHB, PHT, TPM, VPA
Children — absence SZVPA—LTG
BECT (Rolandic)——CBZ, VPA
Juvenile myoclonic epilepsy———
Clinical Pearl — VPA + LTG Interaction (Critical DDI): Valproate is a strong UGT1A4 inhibitor → doubles LTG half-life (from 24 h to 50–70 h). When adding LTG to a patient ON VPA: start at 25 mg every other day × 2 weeks, then 25 mg daily × 2 weeks (extremely slow titration). Failure to do so → toxic LTG levels → SJS/TEN. Conversely, if VPA is stopped in a patient stabilized on LTG+VPA → LTG levels will DOUBLE → toxicity; reduce LTG dose by ~50%.
2.10 Treatment Selection, Stopping ASMs & Polytherapy

When to Start ASM — Decision Framework

ScenarioRecommendationRationale
2nd unprovoked seizure (epilepsy confirmed)Start ASM76% risk of 3rd seizure; treating established epilepsy reduces harm
1st unprovoked SZ + high-risk features (epileptiform EEG, MRI abnormality, prior brain injury, nocturnal SZ)Consider starting ASM (shared decision)≥60% recurrence risk → meets practical epilepsy definition
1st provoked seizure onlyTreat underlying cause; ASM usually NOT needed long-termProvoked SZ does not indicate epilepsy; metabolic correction
Epilepsy syndrome diagnosedStart syndrome-appropriate ASMSyndrome-specific therapy optimizes efficacy; avoids harm (e.g., CBZ in JME)

When to Stop ASM — Criteria

CriterionConsideration
Seizure-free ≥2 years (most guidelines)Can consider withdrawal in adults; risk of recurrence ~30–40% within 2 years of stopping
Seizure-free ≥1 year + favorable factorsChildren: normal EEG, normal MRI, single SZ type, good response; some syndromes (BECT) can stop earlier
Factors that INCREASE recurrence risk after stoppingAbnormal EEG (epileptiform), structural etiology, multiple SZ types, focal epilepsy, longer history before control, IGE (especially JME) — 78% relapse after stopping
JME specificLifelong ASM almost always required; 78% relapse even after years seizure-free
Driving considerationsWithdraw ASM only during non-driving period; counsel patient about risk

Rational Polytherapy

PrincipleRationaleExamples
Complementary mechanisms (synergistic)Two different MOA → potentially additive/synergistic; avoid same-class duplicationNa+ blocker + SV2A (CBZ + LEV); Na+ blocker + GABA-ergic (LTG + VPA); VPA + LTG (broad spectrum combo)
Avoid pharmacokinetic antagonismTwo enzyme inducers together may severely ↓ levels of bothAvoid CBZ + PHT together (competing induction); avoid adding inducer to stable regimen without level monitoring
Rational combinations for focal epilepsy1st: CBZ/LTG/LEV/OXC → failure → add different MOALTG + LEV (different MOA: Na+ + SV2A); LTG + LCS (fast + slow Na inactivation); VPA + LTG (but ↑LTG toxicity risk)
Rational combinations for IGEVPA + LTG (most studied broad-spectrum combo); LEV + LTG; LEV + VPAVPA + LTG: excellent for JME, JAE; VPA + LEV: good evidence; ETX + VPA: for absence + GTC
Combinations to AVOIDPharmacodynamic or PK incompatibility; worseningCBZ + PHT (↑ neurotoxicity); PHT + PHB (sedation); VPA + TPM (↑ hyperammonemia); CBZ in IGE with any AED
2.11 Special Populations

Women of Childbearing Age & Pregnancy

Reference: Tomson T, et al. ILAE Task Force. "Management of epilepsy in pregnancy." Epileptic Disorders 2019 (ILAE). EURAP International Registry data.
IssueKey PointsRecommendation
Teratogenicity riskVPA highest risk: major congenital malformations (MCM) 10–11%, NTD (spina bifida) 1–2%, neurodevelopmental effects (cognitive impairment, autism); dose-dependent. LTG: MCM ~2% (similar to background); LEV: MCM ~2%; PHT, PHB: MCM ~5–6%; TPM: oral clefts ↑; OXC: MCM ~3%AVOID VPA in women who may become pregnant if alternatives exist. Preferred: LTG, LEV (lowest risk). Folic acid 5 mg/day periconceptionally for ALL women on ASM
Hormonal contraception interactionsEnzyme inducers (CBZ, PHT, PHB, OXC, TPM at high dose, PER at ≥12 mg) ↓ OCP efficacy → breakthrough pregnancy. LTG: OCP ↓ LTG 50% (use higher dose or IUCD)Use non-hormonal contraception (IUCD, barrier) with enzyme-inducing AEDs; OR switch to non-inducing AED. If using OCP: use ≥35 µg ethinylestradiol; monitor LTG levels
Seizure frequency during pregnancyMost women (50–80%) remain stable; 15–30% worsen (sleep deprivation, medication changes, levels drop); levels of LTG, LEV, OXC/MHD decrease significantly during pregnancy due to ↑ renal clearance/volume of distributionMonitor drug levels every trimester; increase dose if breakthrough seizures; postpartum: reduce dose to prevent toxicity (levels rebound)
Vitamin K / folic acidEnzyme-inducing AEDs → vitamin K deficiency → neonatal hemorrhage; VPA + some others → folate deficiencyFolic acid 5 mg/day from pre-conception to 12 weeks; Vitamin K 10–20 mg/day in last month for women on enzyme inducers
BreastfeedingGenerally safe for most ASMs; LTG, LEV, VPA low transfer in breast milk; PHB/PHT/BZD: sedation in infant possible; ETX: high transfer → monitor infantBreastfeeding encouraged; observe infant for sedation; bottle-feed if concern

Elderly Patients with Epilepsy

IssueKey Points
Most common new-onset epilepsy causeCerebrovascular disease (#1); tumors; Alzheimer's/dementia; trauma; cryptogenic
Preferred ASMs (ILAE Level A)GBP and LTG for partial-onset SZ in elderly; LTG better tolerated; LEV also well-tolerated with good tolerability profile
AvoidPHT (osteoporosis, cardiac conduction, drug interactions); PHB (sedation, falls, cognitive impairment, Dupuytren's); CBZ (hyponatremia/SIADH, interactions with warfarin)
Pharmacokinetic considerations↓ renal clearance → adjust LEV, GBP, OXC, LCS doses; ↓ albumin → ↑ free PHT fraction (interpret total levels cautiously); CYP interactions more dangerous with polypharmacy
Falls riskAll CNS-active AEDs increase fall risk; minimize sedating AEDs; single agent preferred; treat comorbid osteoporosis
2.12 Drug-Resistant Epilepsy & Surgical Evaluation
Key Trial: Wiebe S, et al. "A randomized, controlled trial of surgery for temporal-lobe epilepsy." N Engl J Med 2001;345:311–318. — Surgery (ATL) vs. continued medical therapy in drug-resistant MTLE: 58% seizure-free post-surgery vs. 8% medical; significant QoL improvement.
Definition & Approach to Drug-Resistant Epilepsy

Drug-resistant epilepsy (ILAE 2010): Failure of adequate trials of ≥2 tolerated, appropriately chosen and used AED schedules (monotherapy or in combination) to achieve sustained seizure freedom.

~30% of patients with epilepsy are drug-resistant. Most patients who will become drug-resistant do so within the first 2 years of treatment; if 2 AEDs fail → probability of seizure freedom with further drugs falls to <5%.

Presurgical Evaluation

StepEvaluationPurpose
1. Phase I (Non-invasive)Video-EEG monitoring (seizure capture); 3T MRI epilepsy protocol; neuropsychological testingDefine seizure semiology, localize EEG onset, identify structural lesion, baseline cognitive mapping
2. Functional MRI (fMRI)Language and memory lateralizationMap eloquent cortex; Wada test (IAP) if fMRI inconclusive
3. FDG-PETInterictal hypometabolism; concordant with EEG and MRI zoneIncreases confidence when MRI negative; localize epileptogenic zone
4. SPECT (Ictal/Interictal)Ictal SPECT: SISCOM analysis (subtraction ictal SPECT co-registered to MRI)Localize seizure onset when MRI negative or discordant
5. MEGMagnetoencephalography; dipole source analysisLocalize interictal epileptiform discharges; eloquent cortex mapping
6. Phase II (Invasive EEG)Stereo-EEG (SEEG) or subdural grid/strip electrodesWhen Phase I concordance insufficient; map epileptogenic network; test resection safety

Surgical Options for Epilepsy

ProcedureIndicationSeizure-Free Rate
Anterior temporal lobectomy (ATL)Drug-resistant MTLE with hippocampal sclerosis~70% seizure-free at 1 year; ~60–65% at 5 years
Lesionectomy / focal cortical resectionFocal lesion (FCD, cavernoma, low-grade tumor) concordant with seizure onset~60–80% (lesional > non-lesional)
Corpus callosotomyDrop attacks (atonic/tonic SZ) in LGS; bilateral synchrony interruption~50–75% reduction in drop attacks; rarely seizure-free
Hemispherectomy / hemispherotomyUnilateral hemispheric pathology (Rasmussen, hemimegalencephaly, large porencephaly)~60–80% seizure-free; requires contralateral hemispheric compensation
Vagus nerve stimulation (VNS)Drug-resistant focal epilepsy not amenable to resection; adjunctive~50% responders (≥50% reduction); 5–10% seizure-free; benefit increases over time
Responsive neurostimulation (RNS)Drug-resistant focal epilepsy; seizure detection + on-demand stimulation~50% responders; 16% seizure-free at 9 years
Deep brain stimulation (DBS)ANT-DBS (thalamic nucleus anterior): drug-resistant focal; SANTE trial~40% reduction; 17% seizure-free at 5 years; progressive benefit
Ketogenic dietDrug-resistant pediatric epilepsy; LGS, Dravet, GLUT-1 deficiency (1st line)~50% with ≥50% reduction; ~10–15% seizure-free in refractory epilepsy
Clinical Pearl — Refer Early for Surgical Evaluation: The major mistake in drug-resistant epilepsy management is waiting too long before surgical referral. After 2 AED failures → refer for presurgical evaluation (ILAE recommendation). Only 3–5% chance of a 3rd, 4th, or 5th drug working. The Wiebe NEJM 2001 trial showed 58% seizure-free with surgery vs. 8% with medications in drug-resistant MTLE — this transforms patients' lives. Drug-resistant MTLE with unilateral hippocampal sclerosis = the best surgical candidate.
2.13 Status Epilepticus — Definition, Classification & Staged Treatment
Key References:
  • Trinka E, et al. "A definition and classification of status epilepticus." Epilepsia 2015;56(10):1515–1523. (ILAE Task Force)
  • ESETT Trial — Kapur J, et al. "Randomized Trial of Three Anticonvulsant Medications for Status Epilepticus." N Engl J Med 2019;381:2103–2113.
  • RAMPART Trial — Silbergleit R, et al. "Intramuscular versus Intravenous Therapy for Prehospital Status Epilepticus." N Engl J Med 2012;366:591–600.
SE Definition — ILAE 2015 (Trinka)

"SE is a condition resulting either from the failure of the mechanisms responsible for seizure termination, or from the initiation of mechanisms which lead to abnormally prolonged seizures (after time point t₁). It is a condition that can have long-term consequences (after time point t₂), including neuronal death, neuronal injury, and alteration of neuronal networks."

SE Typet₁ (treat)t₂ (long-term consequences)
Generalized convulsive SE (GCSE)5 minutes30 minutes
Focal SE with impaired consciousness10 minutes>60 minutes
Absence SE10–15 minutesUnknown

SE stages: Early/Impending (0–30 min) → Established (30–60 min) → Refractory SE (RSE, >60 min) → Super-refractory SE (SRSE, >24 h despite anesthetics, or recurrence on anesthetic weaning)

Classification of SE (Trinka 2015)

CategorySubtypesKey FeaturesMortality
SE with prominent motor phenomena (SE-PM)Convulsive (GCSE, focal onset → bilateral); Myoclonic (with/without coma); Focal motor (Jacksonian, EPC, adversive); Tonic SE; Hyperkinetic SEClinically obvious; most common; GCSE = neurological emergency; EPC (epilepsia partialis continua): continuous focal motor activity, may be very prolongedSE-PM overall: ~10%; convulsive only → 0% (if treated); convulsive evolving to nonconvulsive: 23–40%
Non-convulsive SE (NCSE)NCSE with coma (subtle SE); NCSE without coma: generalized (typical/atypical/myoclonic absence status); focal (aura continua, aphasic, with/without LOC); unknown (autonomic SE)Clinically silent (coma or subtle behavior change); EEG essential for diagnosis; often missed; higher morbidity than convulsive in some forms; absence SE: treated aggressively with IV BZD; twilight state in elderly = absence SE until EEG disprovesNCSE overall: ~27.6%; NCSE coma: 43–60%

Etiology of SE — Prognostic Impact

Etiology% of Adult SE CasesMortality
Low/subtherapeutic AED levels34% (most common in known epilepsy)4%
Remote (prior brain injury)25%14%
Cerebrovascular accident (CVA)22%33%
Anoxia/Hypoxia5–7%53–71% (worst prognosis)
CNS infection3%~0–10%
Tumor (CNS)7%30%
Metabolic/toxic15%30%
Alcohol-related13%20%
Cryptogenic/Unknown~15%~5%
Clinical Pearl — Etiology is the Most Important Prognostic Factor in SE: Anoxic SE has 53–71% mortality regardless of treatment. Low AED levels causing SE has only 4% mortality. Adult SE pooled mortality ~15.9% (Neligan 2019, JAMA Neurol); mortality has NOT improved significantly over 30 years despite better treatment protocols. Etiology MUST be identified and treated simultaneously with seizure control.

Staged Treatment Protocol for Generalized Convulsive SE

StageTimeTreatmentDrugs & DosesSuccess Rate
Stabilization0–5 minABC + supportiveAirway, O₂, IV access ×2; ECG monitoring; fingerstick glucose; if glucose <60 mg/dL → thiamine 100 mg IV then D50W 50 mL. Collect: electrolytes, Ca, Mg, BUN/Cr, LFT, CBC, toxicology screen, AED levels. Time seizure onset.—
Stage I (Initial therapy)5–20 minBenzodiazepine — Level A evidence 1st choice (any one):
• IM midazolam: 10 mg (>40 kg); 5 mg (13–40 kg) — RAMPART: IM = IV LZP
• IV lorazepam: 0.1 mg/kg (max 4 mg/dose), may repeat once
• IV diazepam: 0.15–0.2 mg/kg (max 10 mg), may repeat once
2nd BZD choices:
• IV phenobarbital 15 mg/kg (if no IV access)
• Rectal diazepam 0.2–0.5 mg/kg (max 20 mg)
• Intranasal/buccal midazolam 		60–70% respond (30–40% fail)
Stage II (Second therapy / Established SE)20–40 minSecond-line ASM — Level U (no clear preferred agent) Choose ONE of (give as single loading dose):
• IV Fosphenytoin: 20 mgPE/kg (max 1500 mgPE) IV ≤150 mgPE/min
• IV Valproate: 40 mg/kg (max 3000 mg) IV over 10 min
• IV Levetiracetam: 60 mg/kg (max 4500 mg) IV over 10 min
• IV Phenobarbital: 15–20 mg/kg (if not given in Stage I)
• IV Lacosamide: 400 mg IV over 15 min (adjunctive option)
ESETT 2019 (NEJM): LEV 47% vs FOS 45% vs VPA 46% success — NO SIGNIFICANT DIFFERENCE 		40–60% additional responders
Stage III (Refractory SE)40–60+ minAnesthetic agents — ICU; continuous EEG monitoring Choose ONE (titrate to burst suppression pattern on EEG):
• Midazolam infusion: 0.2 mg/kg bolus, then 0.05–2 mg/kg/h
• Propofol infusion: 1–2 mg/kg bolus, then 2–10 mg/kg/h (PRIS risk with high dose/long use)
• Thiopental: 100–250 mg bolus, then 1–5 mg/kg/h
• Pentobarbital: 5–15 mg/kg loading, then 0.5–10 mg/kg/h 		Variable; high morbidity
Stage IV (Super-refractory SE >24h)>24 hSRSE — experimental options + immune therapy • Ketamine: 1–2 mg/kg bolus, then 0.6–10 mg/kg/h (NMDA antagonist; benefit in prolonged SE)
• Magnesium sulfate: 4 g bolus then 2–6 g/h
• Immunotherapy: methylprednisolone 1 g/day ×7 days; IVIG 30 g ×3–5 days; plasmapheresis
• Hypothermia: 32–35°C
• Ketogenic diet (via NG tube)
• Repeat second-line ASMs
Consider NORSE/FIRES in young patients with unexplained super-refractory SE 		Poor; mortality 30–50%
ESETT Trial — Key Results (NEJM 2019)

Kapur J, et al. NEJM 2019 — n=384 patients with benzodiazepine-refractory SE; randomized to LEV 60 mg/kg (max 4500 mg) vs FOS 20 mgPE/kg (max 1500 mgPE) vs VPA 40 mg/kg (max 3000 mg). Primary outcome: seizure control + improved alertness at 60 min.

OutcomeLEVFOS (Phenytoin)VPA
Seizure control at 60 min47%45%46%
ICU admission60%59%59%
Severe hypotension0.7%3.2%1.6%
Cardiac arrhythmia0.7%0%0%
Intubation20%26.4%16.8%
Death4.7%2.4%1.6%

Conclusion: No significant difference in efficacy between LEV, FOS, and VPA for established (BDZ-refractory) SE. All three are equally valid 2nd-line choices. Choose based on clinical context, availability, and safety profile (LEV: fewest cardiovascular SE; VPA: caution in hepatic disease/pregnancy; FOS: cardiac monitoring needed).

RAMPART Trial — IM vs IV BZD (NEJM 2012)

Silbergleit R, et al. NEJM 2012 — n=893 patients with prehospital SE; IM midazolam (10 mg) vs IV lorazepam (4 mg). Primary: seizure cessation before ED arrival.

  • IM midazolam: 73.4% seizure stopped | IV lorazepam: 63.4% seizure stopped (p<0.001)
  • Median time to treatment: IM 1.2 min vs IV 4.8 min (faster due to no IV placement)
  • Conclusion: IM midazolam is non-inferior (actually superior) to IV lorazepam for prehospital SE
  • Preferred when IV access unavailable; comparable safety

NORSE / FIRES

TermDefinitionKey FeaturesTreatment
NORSE (New Onset Refractory Status Epilepticus)Super-refractory SE in a patient without a prior history of epilepsy and without clear acute or active structural/toxic/metabolic cause at onset (Hirsch 2018)Usually young previously healthy adults; prodrome: fever/headache; rapidly deteriorates to RSE; ~50% autoimmune etiology (especially VGKC/LGI1/GABA-B/NMDAR); cryptogenic 50%Aggressive anesthesia + immunotherapy (steroids → IVIG → PE); ketamine; ketogenic diet; early empiric immunotherapy if clinical suspicion; mortality 16–27%; persistent cognitive deficits common
FIRES (Febrile Infection-Related Epilepsy Syndrome)NORSE with febrile illness 2 weeks to 24 hours before SE onset; seen in school-age children (peak 6–16 yr)School-age children; febrile infection precedes by days–weeks; refractory focal SE; EEG: continuous bitemporal activity; MRI may be normal initially then shows mesial temporal changes; autoimmune etiology uncertain; IL-1 pathway implicatedStandard SE treatment ± immunotherapy; ketogenic diet shows promising results in FIRES; anakinra (IL-1 receptor antagonist) — emerging evidence; mortality 12%; majority have drug-resistant epilepsy afterwards; severe cognitive outcome
2.14 SUDEP, Autoimmune Epilepsy & Key Comorbidities

Sudden Unexpected Death in Epilepsy (SUDEP)

FeatureDetails
DefinitionSudden, unexpected, witnessed or unwitnessed, non-traumatic, non-drowning death in a patient with epilepsy, with or without evidence of a seizure, excluding documented SE, with postmortem examination showing no toxicological or anatomical cause of death
Incidence~1.16/1000 patient-years overall; 3.3–9.3/1000 in drug-resistant epilepsy; 15–20× higher risk in Dravet syndrome; 0.09–0.35/1000 in well-controlled epilepsy
Risk factorsGTC seizures (especially nocturnal) — single most important risk factor; drug-resistant epilepsy; young adults (20–40 yr); male sex; prone sleeping position; alcohol use; polypharmacy; subtherapeutic AED levels; nocturnal unmonitored seizures
PathophysiologyPost-ictal cardiorespiratory failure; postictal generalized EEG suppression (PGES) → brainstem dysfunction → apnea, cardiac arrhythmia; serotonergic pathway disruption; autonomic dysregulation
PreventionOptimize seizure control (most important); avoid prone sleeping; nighttime monitoring/supervision; seizure detection devices; address modifiable risk factors; seizure alert dogs

Autoimmune Epilepsy

Reference: Smith G, et al. "Autoimmune seizure disorders." Continuum 2024; Hirsch LJ, et al. "Autoimmune epilepsy." Review 2022.
AntibodySyndromeSeizure TypeKey Clinical FeaturesTreatment
NMDAR-AbAnti-NMDAR encephalitisFocal, multifocal, GTC; SE commonYoung women; psychiatric prodrome → seizures → movement disorders → catatonia; ovarian teratoma (~40% women); CSF pleocytosis1st: steroids + IVIG; 2nd: rituximab/cyclophosphamide; tumor removal if teratoma
LGI1-AbLGI1 encephalitis (limbic encephalitis)FBDS (faciobrachial dystonic seizures — pathognomonic); temporal lobe SEMiddle-aged men; hyponatremia; amnesia; frontotemporal dementia phenotype; FBDS precede encephalitisSteroids ± IVIG; EARLY treatment of FBDS prevents limbic encephalitis; ASMs have limited effect on FBDS
CASPR2-AbCASPR2 encephalitis / Morvan syndromeTemporal and frontal focal SEOlder men; Morvan: peripheral nerve hyperexcitability + insomnia + CNS; thymoma associationImmunotherapy; thymoma resection
GABA-B-AbGABA-B encephalitisTemporal lobe SE; often refractoryMiddle-aged/elderly; severe seizures early; amnesia; SCLC association (~50%)Immunotherapy; treat underlying tumor
GAD65-AbLimbic encephalitis / Stiff-person spectrumTemporal lobe focal SE; drug-resistantWomen; intracellular antigen → poor immunotherapy response; DM-1 associated; stiff-person spectrumImmunotherapy often insufficient; symptomatic; GABAergic ASMs (diazepam, baclofen for stiffness)
Clinical Pearl — FBDS and LGI1: Faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS) are the single most clinically distinctive feature of LGI1 autoimmune encephalitis. They are brief (<3 s) unilateral arm ± face dystonic/tonic posturing, occurring >100×/day, with preservation of consciousness. ASMs are largely ineffective. Treat with immunotherapy (steroids ± IVIG) as soon as FBDS diagnosed — this prevents the subsequent development of limbic encephalitis and cognitive decline. Also associated with hyponatremia (SIADH). LGI1-Ab positive in serum (most sensitive) and CSF.

Common Comorbidities in Epilepsy

ComorbidityPrevalenceClinical Notes
Depression~30–35% (highest in MTLE)Bidirectional relationship; depression → ↑ seizure risk; shared serotonergic/GABA pathways; SSRIs safe (avoid bupropion which lowers seizure threshold); screen all epilepsy patients
Anxiety~25%Peri-ictal anxiety common; distinguish seizure aura of fear from generalized anxiety; treat with SSRI/SNRI
Cognitive impairment~20–40% (especially TLE, LGS)Seizure burden, ASM effects (PHT, PHB worst; LTG/LEV best tolerated); memory loss in MTLE; progressive in PME; baseline neuropsychological testing important
Sleep disorders~40–50%Obstructive sleep apnea worsens seizure control; treat OSA; nocturnal seizures disturb sleep architecture; LEV/LTG may worsen insomnia
Osteoporosis↑ with enzyme-inducing AEDsCBZ, PHT, PHB → ↑ CYP metabolism of vitamin D → ↓ Ca absorption → ↓ BMD; supplement Ca + Vit D; DEXA scan for long-term users of enzyme inducers

3. Movement Disorders

Movement disorders encompass hypokinetic syndromes (Parkinson's disease, atypical parkinsonism) and hyperkinetic syndromes (tremor, dystonia, chorea, myoclonus, tics). This section covers diagnosis, pharmacotherapy, motor complications, advanced therapies, and movement disorder emergencies at subspecialist depth, integrating MDS 2015 diagnostic criteria and 2024 treatment guidelines.

3.1 Movement Classification & Phenomenology
Basal Ganglia Anatomy & Circuits (Poewe, Nat Rev Dis Primers 2017)

Input nuclei: Striatum (caudate + putamen) and subthalamic nucleus (STN)

Output nuclei: Globus pallidus interna (GPi) and substantia nigra pars reticulata (SNr) → inhibitory (GABA) → thalamus

Intrinsic: Globus pallidus externa (GPe), STN

Modulatory: Substantia nigra pars compacta (SNpc) — dopaminergic; Raphe — serotonergic; Locus ceruleus — noradrenergic

PathwayNeuronsEffect on GPi/SNrNet motor effect
Direct (Go)D1-MSN → GABA/SP → GPi/SNr↓ Inhibition↑ Movement facilitation
Indirect (NoGo)D2-MSN → GABA/ENK → GPe → STN → GPi/SNr↑ Inhibition↓ Movement suppression
HyperdirectMotor cortex → STN directly → GPi↑↑ Inhibition (fast)Emergency brake

Dopamine balance: SNpc DA → ↑D1 (direct, facilitatory) and ↓D2 (indirect, inhibitory) → net movement promotion. Loss of SNpc DA → hypokinesia (Parkinsonism)

Abnormal Movement Classification (Fahn 2011)
CategorySubtypesKey features
HypokinesiaParkinsonismBradykinesia, rigidity, rest tremor, postural instability
Hyperkinesia — RhythmicTremorRegular oscillatory; see tremor classification
MyoclonusSudden brief muscle jerk; rhythmic or arrhythmic
Hyperkinesia — Non-rhythmic: Shock-likeTics (suppressible + urge)Semi-voluntary; waxing/waning; premonitory urge
Myoclonus (non-suppressible)No urge; not suppressible
Hyperkinesia — Non-rhythmic: RandomChorea / Ballism / AthetosisFlow-like → violent flinging → writhing; distal→proximal
Hyperkinesia — Non-rhythmic: Sustained repetitiveDystoniaSustained/intermittent contraction; patterned twisting; alleviating maneuver

3.1.1 Tremor

Tremor Classification (Bhatia, Mov Disord 2018)

Rest tremor: Present at rest, suppressed by action. Classic: Parkinson's disease 4–6 Hz

Active tremors:

  • Postural: Maintained against gravity (arms outstretched). ET predominantly postural (but kinetic-dominant)
  • Kinetic: During movement
    • Simple kinetic: occurs throughout movement
    • Intention/terminal (cerebellar): worsens as target approached
    • Task-specific: writing tremor, embouchure tremor
  • Isometric: During muscle contraction against fixed object

Wing-beating tremor: Arms abducted 90° at shoulder — pathognomonic of Wilson's disease (Holmes/rubral tremor at rest+postural+kinetic)

Tremor Frequency & Clinical Correlations
Tremor typeFrequency (Hz)Key characteristicsDiagnosis clue
Essential Tremor (ET)4–12Kinetic >> postural; hands > head/voiceImproved by alcohol; FH+
Parkinsonian4–6Rest++; re-emergent postural; "pill-rolling"Pill-rolling; unilateral onset
Cerebellar2–4Intention/terminal; dysmetria; truncalAtaxia, nystagmus, scanning speech
Holmes/Rubral1–4Rest + postural++ + kinetic++ (all 3)Midbrain/red nucleus lesion
Orthostatic12–16Legs only; worse standing; feels like unsteadinessHigh freq EMG; resolves with walking
Dystonic1–6Irregular; associated abnormal postureGeste antagoniste; task-specific
Physiologic8–12Exaggerated by anxiety, caffeine, thyroidNo functional disability; bilateral
Re-emergent tremor: Parkinsonian tremor appears after 5–10 sec in outstretched position (same frequency as rest tremor 4–6 Hz), distinguishing it from ET which appears immediately (postural tremor). This re-emergence is due to dopaminergic deficiency — critical for board exams.

3.1.2 Myoclonus (Fahn, 2011)

Definition: Involuntary sudden rhythmic or arrhythmic brief muscle movement (positive) or sudden inhibition (negative = asterixis = "flapping tremor").

Activation: Rest, posture, movement → positive or negative myoclonus

Stimulus-sensitivity: Tactile, auditory, visual (cortical myoclonus)

Myoclonus Classification by Origin
TypeOriginFeaturesExamples
CorticalMotor cortexFocal/multifocal; stimulus-sensitive; EEG: cortical correlatePost-anoxic (Lance-Adams), progressive myoclonic epilepsies
SubcorticalThalamus/BGGeneralized; less stimulus-sensitiveEssential myoclonus, Huntington's
BrainstemBrainstemGeneralized or reticular; exaggerated startleHyperekplexia
SpinalSpinal cordSegmental or propriospinalSpinal cord injury, myelitis
PeripheralNerve/muscleHemifacial spasm, hemimasticationFacial nerve compression
Negative myoclonus (asterixis): Sudden lapse of sustained posture (not a tremor despite "flapping" appearance). Seen in metabolic encephalopathy (hepatic, renal, CO2 retention), drug toxicity (anticonvulsants, lithium). Bilateral asterixis → metabolic; Unilateral → contralateral structural (thalamus/cortex).

3.1.3 Tics & Tourette Syndrome (Jankovic, NEJM 2001)

Definition: Semi-voluntary/involuntary brief waxing and waning stereotyping movements (motor) or sounds (phonic); characterized by premonitory urge and suppressibility (with rebound — "tic flurries").

Tic Classification
TypeSimpleComplex
MotorBlinking, nose twitching, head jerkingHead shaking, trunk bending, jumping, copropraxia, echopraxia
PhonicSniffing, throat clearing, coughing, blowing, screamingVerbalization, coprolalia, echolalia, palilalia

Tourette syndrome: ≥2 motor tics + ≥1 phonic tics (not necessarily simultaneous); onset <18 years; duration >1 year. Comorbidities: ADHD (50–60%), OCD (40–60%), behavioral/impulse control disorders.

Adult-onset tics MUST be worked up (secondary causes): Infections (encephalitis, CJD, neurosyphilis, Sydenham's chorea); Drugs/Tardive tics (amphetamine, cocaine, phenytoin, CBZ, phenobarbital, lamotrigine, antipsychotics, dopamine receptor blockers); Toxin (CO); Developmental (autism, static encephalopathy); Chromosomal (Down, Klinefelter, Fragile X); Others: head trauma, stroke, neurodegenerative diseases. (Joseph J, NEJM 2001)

3.1.4 Chorea, Ballism & Athetosis

Chorea-Ballism-Athetosis Spectrum Comparison
FeatureAthetosisChoreaBallism
QualitySlow writhing (piano-like fingers)Flow-like, random, dancingViolent flinging
DistributionDistal (fingers, toes)Distal > proximalProximal (shoulder, hip)
AmplitudeSmallMediumLarge
SpeedSlowModerateFast
SuppressibilityParakinesia (incorporated)Partial (parakinesia)None

Chorea key features: Motor impersistence (tongue "jack-in-the-box," milkmaid grip); parakinesia (involuntary movements incorporated into purposeful acts). Causes: Huntington's, Sydenham's, SLE/antiphospholipid, hyperglycemia (nonketotic → hemiballism), drugs (levodopa, OCP, anticonvulsants, cocaine), toxoplasmosis (involving BG).

Hemiballism: Most common cause = contralateral STN lesion (lacunar infarct in hypertensive patient). Also: hyperglycemia (hemiballism/hemichorea from putaminal T1 hyperintense lesion). Usually self-limiting (weeks–months).

Athetosis: Classic in children with cerebral palsy (double athetosis). In adults: rule out pseudoathetosis (proprioceptive loss — fingers move when eyes closed due to sensory deafferentation).

3.1.5 Dystonia

Definition (Albanese 2013): Involuntary sustained or intermittent muscle contractions causing abnormal, often repetitive movements, postures, or both. Movements are typically patterned and twisting, and may be tremulous. Dystonia is often initiated or worsened by voluntary action and associated with overflow muscle activation.

Dystonia Classification (Albanese, Mov Disord 2013)

Axis I — Clinical Characteristics:

FeatureOptions
Age of onsetInfancy (<2y), childhood (2–12y), adolescence (13–20y), early adult (21–40y), late adult (>40y)
Body distributionFocal, segmental, multifocal, generalized, hemidystonia
Temporal patternDisease course (stable/progressive), variability (persistent/action/diurnal/paroxysmal)
Associated featuresIsolated vs combined (+ other movement disorders); neurological/systemic features

Axis II — Etiology: Inherited (DYT-TOR1A, DYT-THAP1, DYT-GCH1, etc.) vs Acquired (structural, medication-induced, etc.) vs Idiopathic

Key dystonia phenomena:

  • Geste antagoniste (sensory trick): Touch/pressure to an adjacent body area reduces dystonia. Classic in cervical dystonia (90%).
  • Action dystonia: Triggered or worsened by specific voluntary movement
  • Task-specific dystonia: Writer's cramp, musician's focal dystonia
  • Overflow: Dystonia spreads to adjacent muscles not intended
  • Mirror dystonia: Contralateral limb develops dystonia when ipsilateral limb performs task
Dystonia diagnostic pearl: When evaluating new dystonia, especially young-onset, always check Wilson's disease — serum ceruloplasmin (low), slit-lamp for Kayser-Fleischer rings, 24-hour urine copper. "DYT" genes: TOR1A (DYT1) = childhood-onset generalized; GCH1 (DYT5) = Dopa-responsive dystonia (diurnal fluctuation, dramatic response to levodopa, mimics CP).
Other Hyperkinetic Movement Disorders (Fahn 2011)

Akathisia: Feeling of inner restlessness, need to move; caused by dopamine receptor blockers. Not truly a movement disorder per se but often misdiagnosed.

Stereotypy: Repetitive, coordinated, purposeless movement; seen in autism, tardive stereotypy, Rett syndrome.

Restless Legs Syndrome (RLS/WED): Urge to move legs, worse at rest/night, relieved by movement; 5–15% prevalence; treat with DA agonists (pramipexole, ropinirole, rotigotine), iron supplementation (ferritin <75 μg/L).

Periodic limb movements in sleep (PLMS): Stereotyped limb movements during sleep; associated with RLS.

Peripheral hyperexcitability: Fasciculations (motor neuron disease, benign), myokymia (radiation, MS, GBS), cramps (dehydration, electrolyte, motor neuron disease), myotonia (chloride/sodium channelopathies).

References: Fahn S. Principles and Practice of Movement Disorders, 2nd ed, 2011. Bhatia KP et al. Consensus statement on the classification of tremors. Mov Disord 2018;33:75–87. Albanese A et al. Phenomenology and classification of dystonia. Mov Disord 2013;28:863–73. Jankovic J. Tourette's syndrome. NEJM 2001;345:1184–92.

3.1.6 Tone Assessment — Key Distinctions

Tone Classification for Boards
Tone typeVelocity-dependenceCharacterLesion
SpasticityVelocity-dependent ↑Clasp-knife (high velocity resistance then give)Pyramidal/corticospinal tract
RigidityVelocity-independent ↑Lead-pipe (equal throughout ROM)Extrapyramidal/BG
CogwheelingVelocity-independentRatchet sensation = rigidity + tremorParkinsonism
Paratonia (Gegenhalten)Variable/velocity-dependentResistance proportional to examiner forceFrontal lobe disease
HypotoniaDecreased throughoutFlaccid, reduced deep tendon reflexesCerebellar, LMN, or infant
Q: What distinguishes re-emergent tremor from essential tremor on examination?
Re-emergent tremor (Parkinsonian) appears 5–10 seconds after assuming an outstretched posture, at the same frequency as the rest tremor (4–6 Hz). Essential tremor appears immediately upon assuming posture and has a higher frequency (5–12 Hz). Re-emergence is pathognomonic of dopaminergic deficiency and is suppressed by levodopa.
Q: A 35-year-old develops sudden onset of violent flinging movements of the right arm and leg. MRI shows a small left subthalamic nucleus infarct. What is the pathophysiology?
Hemiballism from left STN infarct. The STN normally excites (glutamate) the GPi → increased inhibition of thalamus. Loss of STN → decreased GPi activity → decreased inhibition of thalamus → excessive thalamo-cortical activation → violent proximal movements contralateral to lesion. Most cases from hypertensive lacunar infarction; self-limiting in weeks-months; treat with dopamine receptor blockers (haloperidol, clonazepam) if severe.
3.2 Parkinson's Disease — Diagnosis & Classification
PD Epidemiology (Dorsey, Lancet Neurol 2018)
ParameterData
Proportion of parkinsonism~70% of all parkinsonism
Prevalence0.3% general population; 1% over age 60; 3% over age 80
Mean age of diagnosis55–60 years
Young-onset PD (YOPD)<40 years; consider genetic (PARK2/Parkin, PINK1, DJ-1)
Juvenile-onset PD<21 years; almost always genetic
Familial PD10–15% of cases; LRRK2 most common AD (G2019S); GBA most common risk allele
Global burden projection12.2M (2015) → 25.2M (2050) — "Parkinson Pandemic"

3.2.1 PD Pathophysiology

Core neuropathology: Loss of dopaminergic neurons in substantia nigra pars compacta (SNpc) + Lewy body accumulation (α-synuclein aggregates). Clinical symptoms appear when 50–60% of SNc dopaminergic cells are lost — explains long prodromal phase.

Braak staging of PD (Braak 2003):

  • Stage 1–2: Dorsal motor nucleus of vagus, olfactory bulb → hyposmia, autonomic dysfunction
  • Stage 3–4: Locus ceruleus, SNc → motor symptoms, RBD
  • Stage 5–6: Neocortex → dementia, hallucinations

Dopamine-BG imbalance: ↓SNpc DA → ↓D1 activation (less direct pathway) + ↓D2 inhibition (more indirect pathway) → net increase of GPi inhibitory output → thalamus suppressed → akinesia/bradykinesia.

Neurotransmitter deficiencies in PD: Dopamine (motor, NMS), Norepinephrine (orthostatic hypotension, depression), Serotonin (depression, anxiety), Acetylcholine (cognitive decline, hallucinations — targeted by rivastigmine). This multi-transmitter deficiency explains why levodopa alone has "unpredictable results" for tremor, postural instability, gait, and NMS.

3.2.2 Prodromal PD & Disease Course

PD Disease Trajectory (Kalia & Lang, Lancet 2015)
PhaseDurationKey features
Prodromal (pre-motor)2–5 years before DxHyposmia (90%), RBD, constipation, depression, fatigue, sleep disturbance; autonomic dysfunction
Early motorYears 1–3Fatigue, pain, diplopia, asymmetric tremor/bradykinesia; honeymoon period — excellent levodopa response
Early-midYears 4–12Anxiety, hypophonia, dysphagia, sleep fragmentation, falls begin; motor complications develop (3–5 years after starting levodopa)
LateYear 8+Dementia (PDD), cognitive dysfunction, hallucinations (dopaminergic therapy), incontinence, orthostatic hypotension; nursing home-level care
RBD as a prodromal biomarker: REM Sleep Behavior Disorder (RBD) — acting out dreams (violent behavior during REM sleep due to loss of REM atonia) — predicts PD/DLB/MSA in 80–90% over 10–15 years. This is the single strongest prodromal biomarker. Screen all RBD patients with DAT-SPECT. PSG confirmation: absent REM atonia.

3.2.3 PD Diagnosis Criteria

UK Parkinson's Disease Society Brain Bank (UKPDS) Criteria — Classic Board Criteria

Step 1 — Diagnosis of Parkinsonism: Bradykinesia PLUS at least one of:

  • Muscular rigidity
  • 4–6 Hz rest tremor
  • Postural instability (not caused by primary visual, vestibular, cerebellar, or proprioceptive dysfunction)

Step 2 — Exclusion criteria (any = NOT PD): History of repeated strokes with stepwise progression; history of repeated head injury; definite encephalitis; oculogyric crises; MPTP exposure; neuroleptic treatment at onset; >1 affected relative; sustained remission; strictly unilateral >3 years; supranuclear gaze palsy; cerebellar signs; early severe autonomic involvement; early severe dementia; Babinski sign; CT evidence of tumor or communicating hydrocephalus; negative levodopa response

Step 3 — Supportive criteria (≥3 required for definite PD):

  • Unilateral onset
  • Rest tremor present
  • Progressive disorder
  • Persistent asymmetry affecting side of onset most
  • Excellent response to levodopa (70–100%)
  • Severe levodopa-induced dyskinesia
  • Levodopa response >5 years
  • Clinical course >10 years
MDS Clinical Diagnostic Criteria for PD (Postuma et al., Mov Disord 2015)

Essential criterion: Parkinsonism = bradykinesia + rest tremor OR rigidity

Absolute exclusion criteria (any one = NOT PD): Unequivocal cerebellar signs; downward supranuclear gaze palsy; behavioral variant FTD or primary progressive aphasia within first 5 years; parkinsonian features only in lower limbs >3 years; treatment with dopamine receptor blocker; absence of observable response to high-dose levodopa; unequivocal cortical sensory loss/apraxia/progressive aphasia; normal functional neuroimaging of dopaminergic system; documentation of alternative condition

Red flags (each reduces likelihood of PD):

  • Rapid progression requiring wheelchair within 5 years
  • Complete absence of progression over ≥5 years
  • Early bulbar dysfunction
  • Inspiratory respiratory dysfunction
  • Severe autonomic failure within first 5 years
  • Recurrent falls within 3 years
  • Disproportionate anterocollis or contractures within 10 years
  • Absence of any non-motor symptoms despite 5 years
  • Unexplained pyramidal tract signs
  • Bilateral symmetric parkinsonism

Supportive criteria: Clear and dramatic beneficial response to dopaminergic therapy; levodopa-induced dyskinesia; rest tremor of a limb; positive olfactory loss test OR cardiac MIBG scintigraphy showing denervation

Clinically established PD: No absolute exclusion criteria + no red flags + ≥2 supportive criteria OR only 1 red flag but ≥2 supportive criteria

Clinically probable PD: No absolute exclusion criteria + ≤2 red flags + ≥2 supportive criteria (one for each red flag)

3.2.4 Parkinsonism Classification

Parkinsonism Classification
CategoryDiagnosesKey distinguishing features
Idiopathic PDSporadic, Familial (LRRK2, SNCA, Parkin, PINK1, DJ-1, GBA)Classic features, excellent levodopa response
SecondaryVascular, Drug-induced, Infectious, Metabolic, Structural, Toxin (MPTP, CO, Mn)History/exposure; drug-induced symmetric, no rest tremor
Atypical ParkinsonismPSP, MSA, CBS, DLBRed flags; poor/limited levodopa response
HeredodegenerativeWilson's disease, Huntington's, SCA (SCA2, SCA3), NBIAYoung-onset; genetic testing; Wilson's always check in <50y
Red flags for atypical parkinsonism (McFarland, Continuum 2016):
  • Rapid progression (wheelchair in <3–5 years)
  • Early gait instability and falls within first year
  • Absence of tremor
  • Poor levodopa response (<30% improvement)
  • PSP-specific: Vertical supranuclear gaze palsy (downward), "surprised" facies, retrocollis, applause sign
  • MSA-specific: Severe early autonomic dysfunction (neurogenic OH ≥20/10 mmHg, urinary incontinence), cerebellar signs, inspiratory stridor
  • CBS-specific: Asymmetric cortical features — limb apraxia, alien limb phenomenon, cortical sensory loss, myoclonus
  • DLB-specific: Early dementia (within 1 year), fluctuating cognition, visual hallucinations, RBD (concurrent with or before parkinsonism)

3.2.5 Non-Motor Symptoms (NMS) of PD

PD Non-Motor Symptom Spectrum
DomainSymptomsTreatment
AutonomicOrthostatic hypotension, sialorrhea, constipation, urinary urgency/retention, sweating abnormalities, seborrheaFludrocortisone/midodrine for OH; glycopyrrolate/botulinum for sialorrhea; macrogol for constipation
NeuropsychiatricDepression (40%), anxiety (30%), psychosis/hallucinations (20–40%), apathy, impulse control disorder (DA agonists)SSRIs (depression); quetiapine/clozapine (psychosis — avoid typical antipsychotics); rivastigmine (PDD)
CognitiveMCI (25–40%), PDD (occurs in majority by 20 years), executive dysfunction, visuospatialRivastigmine (PDD, FDA-approved); memantine (mild benefit)
SleepRBD (60%), EDS (50%), insomnia, RLS/PLMS, sleep apneaClonazepam/melatonin (RBD); modafinil (EDS)
SensoryAnosmia (90%, early), pain/cramping (40%), fatigue, akathisia, restless legsOptimize dopaminergic therapy; gabapentin/pregabalin for pain
OtherWeight loss, dysphagia, drooling, seborrhoeic dermatitisSpeech therapy; BoNT for drooling
References: Hughes AJ et al. Accuracy of clinical diagnosis of idiopathic Parkinson's disease. JNNP 1992;55:181–4. Postuma RB et al. MDS clinical diagnostic criteria for Parkinson's disease. Mov Disord 2015;30:1591–1601. Kalia LV, Lang AE. Parkinson's disease. Lancet 2015;386:896–912. Braak H et al. Staging of brain pathology related to sporadic Parkinson's disease. Neurobiol Aging 2003;24:197–211.
Q: A 65-year-old man presents with 2-year history of right hand rest tremor, bradykinesia, and rigidity. He has had hyposmia for 5 years and constipation. He responds dramatically to levodopa. Which set of diagnostic criteria is best applied here, and what level of certainty is achieved?
Apply MDS 2015 criteria: Essential criterion met (bradykinesia + rigidity + rest tremor). No absolute exclusion criteria. No red flags. Supportive criteria: (1) rest tremor, (2) dramatic levodopa response, (3) hyposmia (olfactory loss). With ≥2 supportive criteria and no red flags = Clinically Established PD. Under UKPDS: Step 1 met; no exclusions; unilateral onset + rest tremor + progressive + levodopa response ≥70% + course >5 years = meets 5 supportive criteria = Definite PD by UKPDS.
Q: A 58-year-old woman is referred with 3-year history of parkinsonism. She has falls and needs a walker already. You note she cannot look downward voluntarily but can follow a moving target down. What is the diagnosis and what is this eye sign called?
Progressive Supranuclear Palsy (PSP) — Richardson syndrome. The eye sign is vertical supranuclear gaze palsy — voluntary downward gaze is lost but oculocephalic reflex (doll's eye) is preserved (because the supranuclear pathway, not the nucleus/brainstem itself, is affected). Hallmark of PSP. This plus early falls (<3 years), vertical gaze abnormality, bradykinesia, and axial rigidity = PSP. Poor levodopa response. MRI: "hummingbird sign" (midbrain atrophy on sagittal).
3.3 Pharmacotherapy of Parkinson's Disease

3.3.1 Levodopa — Gold Standard

Levodopa Pharmacology

Mechanism: Precursor to dopamine; crosses BBB via large neutral amino acid transporter (LNAAT). Amino acids from dietary protein compete for transport — hence food-drug interaction.

Must combine with peripheral DOPA decarboxylase inhibitor (DDCI): Carbidopa (in Sinemet) or benserazide (in Madopar/Vopar) — inhibits peripheral conversion of levodopa to dopamine, triples oral bioavailability, reduces peripheral dopaminergic side effects (N/V, OH).

Pharmacokinetics:

  • Fasting Tmax: 15–60 minutes; T½: 1–3 hours (short → motor fluctuations)
  • Food delays Tmax 2–3-fold; decreases Cmax by ~30%
  • Take 30–60 min BEFORE meals (or 2 hr after) on empty stomach for optimal absorption
  • HBS (Hydrodynamically Balanced System/Madopar HBS): bioavailability 50–70%, Tmax 2–4h, T½ 6–8h → smoother levels, less peak-dose dyskinesia

Most effective for: Bradykinesia & rigidity. Unpredictable/variable effect on: Postural instability, tremor, gait freezing, NMS.

Levodopa Formulations (Pitakpatapee, CMU/Siriraj)
ProductFormulationStrengthBioavail.TmaxT½Notes
SinemetImmediate release (IR)10/100, 25/100, 25/250 mg (carbidopa/levodopa)80–98%0.5–2h1–3hStandard; take on empty stomach
Madopar / VoparIR tablet12.5/50, 25/100, 50/200 mg (benserazide/levodopa)98%0.5–1h1–3hThai brand: Vopar 250mg
Madopar DispersibleDispersible/ODT25/100 mg98%<1h1–3hFor morning akinesia, delayed ON; dissolve in water
Madopar HBSExtended-release (CR)25/100 mg50–70%2–4h6–8hNocturnal wearing-off; 20–30% dose increase needed vs IR
StalevoTriple combination25/100/200, 37.5/150/200 mg (carbidopa/levodopa/entacapone)———For wearing-off; see COMT inhibitor section
Duopa/LCIGIntraduodenal infusionVariable~continuousContinuous—Advanced PD; requires PEG-J; ~70,000 THB/month
Levodopa clinical tips (Pitakpatapee):
  • Start at lowest effective dose; titrate slowly
  • Abrupt withdrawal risks: depression, anxiety, pain, and Neuroleptic Malignant Syndrome-like reaction — always taper gradually, never stop cold-turkey
  • When adding entacapone, reduce levodopa dose by ~25% if >800mg/day or prior dyskinesia
  • Minimum carbidopa dose for adequate DDCI effect: ~70–75 mg/day (peripheral DDC saturation)

3.3.2 Dopamine Agonists (DA)

Dopamine Agonists — Dosing & Receptor Profiles
DrugReceptorStarting doseTarget dose rangeTitration
Pramipexole (non-ergot)D3>D20.375 mg/day (IR: TID or ER: OD)0.375–4.5 mg/day+0.75 mg/week
Ropinirole (non-ergot)D3>D22 mg/day2–24 mg/day+2 mg/week
Rotigotine (non-ergot, transdermal)D3>D2>D12 mg/24h patch2–16 mg/24h+2 mg/week every 1–2 weeks
Piribedil (non-ergot)D3>D2; α2 antagonist50 mg/day150–250 mg/day+50 mg every 3 days
Bromocriptine (ergot)D1(-), D2(+)1.25 mg OD7.5–40 mg/day+1.25 mg/week → BID/TID
Apomorphine (SC injection)D1=D21 mg SC test2–6 mg/injectionRescue doses; continuous infusion
Clinical Benefits of DA Agonists (Anthonini et al., Lancet Neurol 2009)
  • Delay need for levodopa (when used as monotherapy in early PD)
  • Fewer peak-dose dyskinesias vs levodopa (long T½ → more stable DA receptor stimulation)
  • Helpful as adjunct for motor fluctuations
  • Positive effects on NMS: sleep (D3 effects), pain, mood
  • Fewer tablets needed (once or twice daily for ER formulations)

DA agonists have HIGHER risk than levodopa for: Peripheral edema, somnolence, sudden onset of sleep, impulse control disorders (ICD), hallucinations, nausea, dopamine dysregulation syndrome, fibrosis (ergot-derived)

Levodopa has HIGHER risk than DA agonists for: Peak-dose dyskinesia, motor fluctuations, dopamine dysregulation syndrome

Impulse Control Disorder (ICD) with DA agonists: Pathological gambling, compulsive shopping, hypersexuality, binge eating — seen in ~14% of DA agonist-treated PD patients. Higher risk with younger patients, male sex, prior history of alcohol/drug use, depression. Management: dose reduction/withdrawal of DA agonist (warn patient of withdrawal symptoms). NOT unique to ergot agents — seen with all DA agonists.

Ergot DA agonists (bromocriptine): Risk of retroperitoneal, pleuropulmonary, pericardial fibrosis + valvular heart disease (echocardiographic monitoring recommended). Now largely avoided in PD — only used for other indications (hyperprolactinemia).
Sudden onset of sleep ("sleep attacks") with DA agonists: Rare but potentially dangerous; patients may fall asleep without warning while driving. Counsel all patients about this risk. Discontinue DA agonist if occurs; avoid driving.

3.3.3 MAO-B Inhibitors

MAO-B Inhibitors — Pharmacology & Dosing
DrugMechanismDoseMetabolismKey AEs
SelegilineIrreversible MAO-B inhibitor5 mg QAM or 5 mg QAM + noon (avoid insomnia); max 10 mg/dayCYP2B6/2C19 → desmethyl-selegiline → l-amphetamine; take before 14:00 to avoid insomniaInsomnia (amphetamine metabolite), confusion, dizziness, exacerbation of levodopa dyskinesias
Rasagiline (Azilect)Irreversible MAO-B inhibitorMonotherapy: 1 mg OD; Adjunct: 0.5→1 mg ODCYP1A2 → aminoindan (non-amphetamine metabolite)Headache, arthralgia, dyspepsia, constipation, depression, flu-like; levodopa dyskinesia exacerbation
SafinamideReversible MAO-B inhibitor + glutamate modulation50→100 mg/day (add-on to levodopa)Multiple pathwaysInsomnia, nausea, dyskinesia, fall risk; retinal safety monitoring

Clinical uses of MAO-B inhibitors:

  • Monotherapy in early PD (efficacious — MDS EBM 2018)
  • Adjunct to levodopa (reduces off-time ~0.5–1 hour/day)
  • May slow disease progression (debated — ADAGIO trial rasagiline 1 mg showed possible neuroprotection but not confirmed)
Selegiline vs Rasagiline — key difference: Selegiline metabolizes to l-amphetamine → stimulant side effects (insomnia, confusion, especially in elderly). Rasagiline metabolizes to aminoindan (no amphetamine activity) → generally better tolerated. Both are irreversible MAO-B inhibitors. Do not use >10 mg/day selegiline (loses MAO-B selectivity → cheese reaction with tyramine-containing foods).

Drug interactions: MAO-B inhibitors + SSRIs → serotonin syndrome (rare at therapeutic doses); + meperidine (pethidine) → hyperpyrexia, rigidity, agitation (selegiline more concerning due to residual MAO-A activity at higher doses).

3.3.4 COMT Inhibitors

Entacapone (Comtan) — Pharmacology

Mechanism: Selective, reversible inhibitor of peripheral catechol-O-methyltransferase (COMT). Acts in gut; does NOT cross BBB. Inhibits peripheral conversion of levodopa to 3-O-methyldopa (3-OMD) → increases levodopa bioavailability and half-life → more stable plasma levels → reduces wearing-off.

Dosing: 200 mg with EACH dose of levodopa/DDCI (IR or CR); maximum 8–10 times daily; can be taken with or without food.

Key adverse effects:

  • Dyskinesia (most common — reduce levodopa 25% if >800 mg/day or prior dyskinesia)
  • Diarrhea (5–10%)
  • Harmless orange/brown urine discoloration
  • Worsening of levodopa side effects (N/V, OH, hallucinations) due to increased levodopa exposure
Tolcapone vs Entacapone: Tolcapone is a central+peripheral COMT inhibitor — MORE effective than entacapone but requires liver function monitoring (3 fatal cases of hepatotoxicity). Entacapone is peripheral only — no hepatotoxicity but less potent. Opicapone (50 mg OD) is newer, once-daily peripheral COMT inhibitor with favorable efficacy profile.

3.3.5 Anticholinergics

Anticholinergic Agents
DrugDoseIndicationKey warnings
Trihexyphenidyl (Benzhexol, Artane)0.5–1 mg BID → 2 mg TID (max 6–10 mg/day)Young patients (<70y) with prominent tremor; drug-induced parkinsonismAvoid in elderly, dementia, BPH, closed-angle glaucoma
Benztropine (Cogentin)0.5–2 mg BIDAcute dystonic reaction (IM/IV), drug-induced parkinsonismSame contraindications; IV for acute reactions

Adverse effects: Hallucinations, cognitive impairment (may precipitate dementia), sedation, dry mouth, blurred vision, urinary retention, constipation, tachycardia.

CRITICAL: Abrupt discontinuation causes ACUTE exacerbation of Parkinsonism — always taper slowly. Never abruptly stop in hospitalized PD patients (surgical patients, NPO orders).

3.3.6 Amantadine

Amantadine

Mechanisms: (1) Promotes presynaptic dopamine release; (2) Non-selective NMDA receptor antagonist (glutamate antagonism → anti-dyskinesia effect)

Doses: 100 mg OD → 100 mg BID; max 200–300 mg/day (renal dose adjustment — clearance is 100% renal)

Uses:

  • Early mild PD (monotherapy or adjunct)
  • Levodopa-induced dyskinesia (LID) — only oral drug with level A evidence for LID (NMDA antagonism)
  • Drug-induced parkinsonism (short-term)

Adverse effects: Livedo reticularis (mottled skin, common, benign), ankle edema, insomnia, confusion/hallucinations (especially elderly), nausea.

3.3.7 Evidence-Based Medicine (MDS EBM, Fox et al. 2018)

MDS EBM for Symptomatic PD Treatment (Fox et al., Mov Disord 2018)
InterventionEvidence LevelClinical Utility
Levodopa (monotherapy)Efficacious (A)Clinically useful +++
DA agonists (monotherapy)Efficacious (A)Clinically useful
MAO-B inhibitors (monotherapy)Efficacious (A)Clinically useful (less than levodopa)
Istradefylline (adenosine A2A antagonist)Nonefficacious for monotherapyNot recommended as monotherapy; useful as adjunct for OFF
