3.3.8 Thai PD Treatment Algorithm

Thai Parkinson's Treatment Algorithm
Patient groupFirst-line recommendationRationale
Age <60, mild symptomsNon-ergot DA agonist OR MAO-B inhibitorDelay levodopa; fewer motor complications long-term
Age ≥60, functional impairmentLevodopa firstBetter efficacy; cognitive tolerance of DA agonists lower in elderly
Significant symptoms, any ageLevodopa regardless of ageFunctional need takes priority
Motor complications developAdd COMT inhibitor (entacapone) or MAO-B inhibitor; consider DBS if eligibleAddress wearing-off

Available medications in Thailand (as of 2024):

  • Levodopa: Vopar 250 mg (levodopa/benserazide), Madopar HBS, Madopar Dispersible
  • DA agonists: Ropinirole (Requip), Pramipexole, Rotigotine patch, Piribedil, Bromocriptine, Apomorphine
  • MAO-B: Rasagiline (Azilect), Selegiline
  • COMT: Entacapone (Comtan), Stalevo
  • Anticholinergics: Trihexyphenidyl (Benzhexol/Artane), Benztropine
  • Amantadine
References: Fox SH et al. International Parkinson and movement disorder society evidence-based medicine review: Update on treatments for the motor symptoms of Parkinson's disease. Mov Disord 2018;33:1248–1266. Anthonini A et al. Dopamine agonists and agonist/levodopa combination therapy in PD. Lancet Neurol 2009;8:929–37. Youdim MBH, Bakhle YS. Monoamine oxidase: isoforms and inhibitors in PD. Nat Rev Neurosci 2006;7:295–309.
Q: A 55-year-old patient with PD on levodopa 300 mg/day develops wearing-off, with symptoms returning 2–3 hours after each dose. What are the strategies to address wearing-off?
Wearing-off management options:
  1. Increase levodopa dose frequency (smaller, more frequent doses — TID → QID)
  2. Add COMT inhibitor (entacapone 200 mg with each levodopa) — extends levodopa half-life, reduces OFF time by ~1–1.5 hours/day
  3. Add MAO-B inhibitor (rasagiline 0.5–1 mg OD) — reduces off-time ~45 min/day
  4. Add DA agonist (pramipexole, ropinirole) — smoother DA stimulation; reduces OFF time
  5. Switch to Stalevo (carbidopa/levodopa/entacapone triple combination)
  6. Controlled-release (HBS) formulation at bedtime for nocturnal/early morning akinesia
  7. Dispersible levodopa for rescue (faster Tmax <1h)
  8. Advanced therapies if uncontrolled: LCIG (duodenal infusion), apomorphine SC, DBS
Drug interactions note: levodopa competes with dietary protein for absorption — protein redistribution diet (low protein meals during day, high protein at night) can improve daytime motor fluctuations.
Q: A 70-year-old man with PD develops vivid visual hallucinations (sees small animals). He is on levodopa 750 mg/day, pramipexole 3 mg/day, and trihexyphenidyl 6 mg/day. How do you approach this?
PD psychosis/hallucinations management — stepwise drug withdrawal:
  1. Step 1: Eliminate precipitants — infection, dehydration, metabolic disturbance
  2. Step 2: Reduce/eliminate anticholinergics first (trihexyphenidyl — most likely offender) — taper over weeks
  3. Step 3: Reduce/eliminate DA agonist (pramipexole — more pro-psychotic than levodopa)
  4. Step 4: Reduce MAO-B inhibitor if present
  5. Step 5: Reduce amantadine if present
  6. Step 6: Reduce levodopa (last resort — preserves motor benefit)
  7. Step 7: Add antipsychotic if psychosis persists:
    • Quetiapine (off-label; low D2 affinity; does not worsen motor symptoms) — most used in Thailand
    • Clozapine (gold standard; requires weekly CBC for agranulocytosis) — most evidence
    • Pimavanserin (5-HT2A inverse agonist; FDA-approved PD psychosis 2016; not available in Thailand)
    • AVOID: Haloperidol, chlorpromazine, risperidone, olanzapine — worsen PD significantly
  8. Step 8: Rivastigmine if dementia component (PDD)
3.4 Motor Complications & Advanced Therapies

3.4.1 Motor Complications of Levodopa

Motor complications develop in ~50% of patients after 5 years of levodopa and nearly all patients by 10 years. They are caused by pulsatile dopaminergic stimulation from short-acting levodopa in the context of progressive presynaptic neuron loss.

Classification of Motor Complications
TypeSubtypeTimingDescriptionManagement
Motor Fluctuations Wearing-off Predictable, before next dose Return of PD symptoms before next dose; most common Add COMT, MAO-B, DA agonist; increase frequency; CR formulation
Delayed ON After dose Levodopa takes longer to work (>60 min); often from food competition Take on empty stomach; dispersible formulation; prokinetics (domperidone)
No ON (dose failure) After dose Dose does not work at all; gastroparesis or GI absorption problem Domperidone; apomorphine rescue; check GI motility
ON/OFF fluctuations Unpredictable Sudden, random transitions; cannot be predicted from dose timing Advanced therapies (DBS, LCIG, apomorphine); continuous DA stimulation
Freezing of gait (FOG) Variable Sudden inability to initiate/continue gait; "start hesitation," narrow spaces Optimize levodopa; cueing (auditory, visual, rhythmic); physical therapy; STN-DBS
Dyskinesias Peak-dose dyskinesia During maximum drug effect (ON) Choreiform/dystonic involuntary movements; most common type (70%) Reduce individual levodopa dose (increase frequency); amantadine; consider surgery
Diphasic dyskinesia Rising and falling plasma levels Ballistic/dystonic movements at beginning and end of dose; difficult to manage Continuous DA stimulation (apomorphine, LCIG); DBS
Off-period dystonia During OFF state Painful foot/leg dystonia; typically early morning; toe curling Long-acting CR formulation at bedtime; bedtime DA agonist; botulinum toxin

3.4.2 Referral Criteria to Movement Disorder Specialist

When to refer to specialist (Thai guidelines): Dyskinesia; wearing-off symptoms; morning akinesia; delayed ON; sudden freezing or unpredictable OFF; diphasic dyskinesia; levodopa >800 mg/day still inadequate control; uncontrolled side effects (hallucinations, ICD, sleep disturbance). Early referral improves outcomes.

3.4.3 Deep Brain Stimulation (DBS)

DBS Eligibility Criteria

Indications:

  • Confirmed PD diagnosis (UKPDS/MDS criteria)
  • Demonstrated dopamine responsiveness (levodopa test: ≥30–40% UPDRS-III improvement)
  • Motor fluctuations and/or dyskinesia refractory to optimal medical therapy
  • Functionally limiting tremor refractory to medical therapy

Relative exclusions:

  • Dementia (MCI acceptable; Hoehn-Yahr ≤4 in ON state)
  • Active untreated mood disorder or psychosis
  • Unrealistic patient expectations
  • Significant structural brain pathology, uncontrolled hypertension
  • Significant surgical risk (anticoagulation, cardiopulmonary)

DBS targets:

  • STN (subthalamic nucleus): Best overall effect; allows levodopa dose reduction (~50%); addresses tremor, rigidity, bradykinesia, dyskinesia; EARLYSTIM trial (Deuschl 2006, NEJM): DBS superior to best medical therapy for quality of life even in early motor complications
  • GPi (globus pallidus interna): Better for dyskinesia-dominant; no levodopa reduction needed; better for patients with psychiatric comorbidities
  • VIM (ventral intermediate nucleus of thalamus): Tremor only; does not address other PD features; used primarily for ET

DBS cost (Thailand, 2024): ~854,530 THB (3–5 year battery life) + replacement battery ~605,420 THB

What DBS does NOT help well: Axial symptoms (gait freezing, postural instability, dysarthria, dysphagia) — especially if present in OFF state; cognitive decline; NMS; apraxia of eyelid opening. These features may worsen after STN-DBS. GPi-DBS preserves speech better than STN-DBS.

3.4.4 Advanced Non-oral Therapies

Advanced PD Therapy Comparison
TherapyRouteMechanismIndicationCost (Thailand)Key issues

LCIG (Duodopa) Intraduodenal infusion Levodopa/carbidopa gel Advanced PD with severe motor fluctuations; on ≥4 doses/day with ≥3 hours OFF/day ~70,000 THB/month Requires PEG-J tube; weight loss; PN, polyneuropathy; site complications; 24-hr nurse for initiation

References: Deuschl G et al. A randomized trial of deep-brain stimulation for Parkinson's disease. NEJM 2006;355:896–908. Williams A et al. Q: A 61-year-old male with PD on levodopa 100 mg TID for 4 years develops involuntary dancing movements of his arms that occur 45–60 minutes after taking levodopa and last about 90 minutes. He is otherwise well during these movements. What type of complication is this, and what is first-line management?
Peak-dose dyskinesia — choreiform involuntary movements occurring at maximum levodopa plasma level (peak ON state). Pathophysiology: pulsatile dopamine receptor stimulation + striatal sensitization from progressive presynaptic loss. Management options: (1) Reduce each individual levodopa dose and increase frequency (e.g., 100mg TID → 75mg QID — same total dose, smoother levels); (2) Add amantadine 100 mg BID — only oral drug with Level A evidence for dyskinesia (NMDA antagonism); (3) Extended-release preparation can smooth peaks; (4) If severe/uncontrolled: refer for DBS (GPi target particularly good for dyskinesia) or LCIG infusion.
3.5 Atypical Parkinsonism (Parkinson-Plus Syndromes)

3.5.1 Progressive Supranuclear Palsy (PSP)

MDS PSP Criteria 2017 (Höglinger et al., Mov Disord 2017)

Pathology: 4-repeat tauopathy; neuronal loss + tau neurofibrillary tangles in STN, SN, pallidum, brainstem

Core functional domains:

  • O: Oculomotor dysfunction (vertical supranuclear gaze palsy → downward VGP pathognomonic; slow vertical saccades)
  • P: Postural instability (falls, retropulsion)
  • A: Akinesia (bradykinesia, freezing, micrographia)
  • C: Cognitive dysfunction (frontal executive, apathy)

Clinical variants:

  • PSP-RS (Richardson Syndrome): Classic; early falls/retropulsion + vertical gaze palsy; "hummingbird sign" on MRI; "morning glory sign" (midbrain atrophy)
  • PSP-P (PSP-Parkinsonism): Resembles PD initially; slower progression; some levodopa response early
  • PSP-CBS: Asymmetric, limb apraxia, cortical features
  • PSP-F: Frontal predominant; early cognitive/behavioral changes

Clinical pearls:

  • "Applause sign": asked to clap 3 times, patients continue (>5 claps = frontal disinhibition)
  • Retrocollis (head extension backward) — contrast with PD (antecollis) and MSA (antecollis)
  • "Surprised facies" — wide-eyed expression from levator overactivity
  • "Square wave jerks" on eye movement examination

3.5.2 Multiple System Atrophy (MSA)

MDS MSA Criteria 2022 (Wenning et al., Lancet Neurol 2022)

Definition: Sporadic, progressive, adult-onset (>30 years) neurodegenerative disorder with glial cytoplasmic inclusions (GCIs) of α-synuclein

Mandatory criterion: Acquired autonomic dysfunction + motor features (parkinsonism or cerebellar)

Autonomic criteria:

  • Neurogenic orthostatic hypotension (OH): ≥20 mmHg systolic / ≥10 mmHg diastolic within 3 minutes of standing
  • Urinary incontinence/retention (neurogenic bladder dysfunction)
  • Erectile dysfunction in males

Subtypes:

  • MSA-P (Parkinsonian variant, Striatonigral Degeneration): Predominant parkinsonism; poor levodopa response; putaminal changes on MRI (putaminal slit sign — T2 hypointensity lateral putamen)
  • MSA-C (Cerebellar variant, OPCA): Predominant cerebellar ataxia; "hot cross bun sign" on MRI (T2 hyperintensity pons)

Additional features: Inspiratory stridor (laryngeal dystonia — emergency if acute); antecollis (exaggerated forward head tilt); cold blue-purple hands/feet (Raynaud-like); Pisa syndrome

Prognosis: Median survival 9–10 years; death from aspiration pneumonia, respiratory failure (stridor)

3.5.3 Corticobasal Syndrome (CBS) / Corticobasal Degeneration (CBD)

Pathology: 4-repeat tauopathy (like PSP); astrocytic plaques in cortex and BG. Note: CBS is a clinical syndrome; CBD is the neuropathological diagnosis (requires autopsy). CBS can result from CBD, PSP, Alzheimer disease, and others.

Clinical features: Asymmetric akinetic-rigid syndrome + cortical features:

  • Limb apraxia: Cannot perform skilled movements despite intact strength/sensation
  • Alien limb phenomenon: Limb moves involuntarily, as if acting on its own will
  • Cortical sensory loss: Astereognosis, agraphesthesia
  • Cortical myoclonus: Stimulus-sensitive; jerky tremor
  • Aphasia (left-sided cortical involvement)

MRI: Asymmetric cortical atrophy (parietal dominant), contralateral to more affected side

3.5.4 Dementia with Lewy Bodies (DLB)

DLB Consensus Criteria 2017 (McKeith et al., Neurology 2017)

Essential feature: Dementia (cognitive decline interfering with function)

Core clinical features (2 sufficient for probable DLB; 1 for possible):

  • Fluctuating cognition — variable attention/alertness (ask family: "does he zone out or seem confused for minutes to hours?")
  • Recurrent visual hallucinations — well-formed, detailed (people, animals); present in ~80%
  • RBD — acting out dreams; often precedes dementia by years
  • Parkinsonism — one or more of bradykinesia, rest tremor, rigidity

Indicative biomarkers:

  • Reduced DAT uptake on DAT-SPECT/PET (same as PD)
  • Reduced cardiac MIBG uptake (postganglionic sympathetic denervation)
  • Polysomnographic confirmation of REM sleep without atonia

DLB vs PDD: "1-year rule" — dementia appearing >1 year after motor symptoms = PDD; Dementia appearing before or within 1 year of parkinsonism = DLB. Pathologically similar; clinically distinction helps management.

Critical management warning: DLB patients are exquisitely sensitive to neuroleptics (even atypical) — neuroleptic sensitivity reaction: acute severe Parkinsonism, autonomic instability, impaired consciousness, potentially fatal. Use only quetiapine or clozapine if antipsychotic absolutely needed. AVOID: haloperidol, olanzapine, risperidone

References: Höglinger GU et al. Clinical diagnosis of progressive supranuclear palsy. Mov Disord 2017;32:853–864. Wenning GK et al. The Movement Disorder Society criteria for the diagnosis of multiple system atrophy. Lancet Neurol 2022;21:430–442. McKeith IG et al. Diagnosis and management of dementia with Lewy bodies. Neurology 2017;89:88–100.
Q: A 66-year-old woman has 2-year history of progressively worsening balance, falling backward, and inability to look down. She has mild bradykinesia but no resting tremor. Levodopa 600 mg/day provides only ~20% improvement. What is the diagnosis, and what is the characteristic MRI finding?
Progressive Supranuclear Palsy — Richardson Syndrome (PSP-RS). Key features: vertical supranuclear gaze palsy (downward) + early falls + retrocollis + axial > appendicular rigidity + poor levodopa response. MRI finding: "Hummingbird sign" (also "penguin sign") — atrophy of the midbrain on midsagittal MRI with relative preservation of pons, creating a silhouette resembling a hummingbird. Also: "morning glory sign" — midbrain atrophy seen on axial view. Midbrain AP diameter <17mm and MRPI (MR Parkinsonism Index = pons area/midbrain area × SCP width/MCP width) >13.55 supports PSP. Treatment: No disease-modifying therapy. Symptomatic: levodopa trial (if any benefit); donepezil for cognition; amantadine for some; fall prevention; PEG for dysphagia; botulinum for blepharospasm/eyelid apraxia.
3.6 Essential Tremor
Essential Tremor — Key Facts

Definition (MDS criteria 2018): Bilateral postural and/or kinetic tremor of the hands and forearms; duration ≥3 years; possibly additional tremor at other locations (head, voice, chin). No other neurological signs (isolated ET).

Epidemiology: Most common movement disorder; prevalence ~5% (age >65: 15–20%); autosomal dominant tendency (~50% familial); LINGO1 and ETM2 gene loci.

FeatureEssential TremorParkinsonian Tremor
TypeKinetic >> postural; no rest tremorRest >> re-emergent postural; no kinetic
Frequency4–12 Hz4–6 Hz
Onset at restNoYes (classic)
Body distributionBilateral hands + head + voiceUnilateral onset; hands, chin, legs; head rare
Response to alcoholYes (~50–70%)No (or minimal)
Family historyCommon (~50%)Less common (10–15%)
DAT-SPECTNormalAbnormal (reduced uptake)
Other PD featuresAbsent (rigidity, bradykinesia, postural instability)Present
ET Treatment (AAN Practice Guideline 2011, Zesiewicz et al.)
AgentEvidenceDoseNotes
PropranololLevel A (established)60–320 mg/day in divided doses; ER 60–80 mg/dayNon-selective β-blocker; CI: asthma, bradycardia, DM insulin-dependent; reduces handwriting tremor ~50%
PrimidoneLevel A (established)25 mg QHS → 250 mg TID; start very low (acute side effects at initiation)Metabolized to phenobarbital; sedation, dizziness at start; hepatic induction; effective for distal > proximal
TopiramateLevel B (probably effective)25–400 mg/dayWord-finding difficulty, renal stones, weight loss; useful if propranolol/primidone fail
GabapentinLevel B1200–3600 mg/day TIDUseful for head tremor; sedation; useful if CNS not tolerated
AlprazolamLevel B0.125–3 mg/dayBenzodiazepine; dependence risk; short-term use
Atenolol, sotalol, nadololLevel C (possibly effective)Atenolol 50–150 mg/dayLess CNS penetration than propranolol; option if pulmonary concerns
ClonazepamLevel C0.5–6 mg/dayEspecially for orthostatic tremor; sedation; dependence
BoNT-A injectionLevel B (hand tremor); Level A (head tremor)50–100U per hand (wrist flexors/extensors)Useful for disabling hand/head tremor; weakness is main SE
ET Surgical Options
  • Thalamic DBS (VIM): Level A (established efficacy); bilateral DBS for bilateral tremor; side effects: dysarthria, paresthesia, disequilibrium; battery-dependent
  • Thalamotomy (radiofrequency): Unilateral VIM; no hardware; irreversible; speech/balance risks
  • MR-guided Focused Ultrasound (MRgFUS) thalamotomy: FDA-approved 2016; non-invasive; unilateral only; safe; no craniotomy; irreversible
  • Gamma Knife radiosurgery: Delayed onset (months); unilateral
Head tremor in ET: Unlike PD where head tremor is very rare (almost never), ET commonly involves head (15–37%) — "no-no" (horizontal rotation) or "yes-yes" (vertical). Cervical dystonia also causes head tremor but with abnormal posture + geste antagoniste. Differentiation: ET head tremor = head moves but neck posture normal; Dystonic head tremor = head deviates + sensory trick helps + irregular frequency. BoNT-A for head tremor: Level A evidence.
Enhanced Physiological Tremor (EPT): 8–12 Hz, bilateral; exacerbated by anxiety, caffeine, thyroid disease, medications (β-agonists, valproate, lithium, SSRIs, steroids, cyclosporine, theophylline). Treat underlying cause; propranolol for symptomatic relief.
Q: A 72-year-old retired engineer presents with 10-year history of bilateral hand tremor affecting his daily activities, specifically his handwriting and tea-drinking. His son also has tremor. Examination shows bilateral postural/kinetic hand tremor; no rest tremor, no rigidity or bradykinesia. He tried propranolol 80 mg/day with 40% improvement but has side effects. What are the next steps?
Essential Tremor (familial). Positive family history, bilateral kinetic > postural, no PD features, propranolol partial response. Management options:
  1. Add primidone (start 25 mg QHS — avoid high initial doses → acute toxicity) — combination propranolol + primidone more effective than either alone
  2. Alternatively: If propranolol SE limiting, switch to atenolol 50–150 mg (less CNS penetration, less bradycardia symptoms)
  3. If still refractory: Add topiramate 25–400 mg (Level B); gabapentin 1200–3600 mg
  4. If disabling and refractory to medications: Refer for DBS (VIM thalamus — bilateral) or MRgFUS (unilateral, right thalamus for dominant right hand) — excellent tremor reduction (>80%)
  5. BoNT injection wrist flexors/extensors: useful for writer's tremor; 50–100 U per hand; caveat: wrist weakness
Head/voice tremor if present: BoNT-A for head (Level A); propranolol for voice tremor.
3.7 Dystonia & Cervical Dystonia

3.7.1 Dystonia Overview

Pathophysiology: Loss of cortical/subcortical inhibition (GABA, dopamine, acetylcholine); dysfunction of BG-brainstem-cerebellar-cortical network; abnormal sensorimotor integration → loss of surround inhibition → overflow.

Dystonia Classification by Etiology (Albanese 2013)
CategorySubtypesExamplesManagement
Isolated (Idiopathic)Focal, segmental, multifocal, generalized, hemidystoniaCervical dystonia, writer's cramp, blepharospasmBoNT, oral medications, DBS
GeneticDYT-TOR1A (DYT1), DYT-THAP1 (DYT6), DYT-GCH1 (DYT5), DYT-ATP1A3, etc.TOR1A: early-onset generalized; GCH1: dopa-responsiveSpecific treatments per gene
AcquiredDrug-induced, structural, vascular, infectious, metabolicTardive dystonia (antipsychotics), Wilson's disease, kernicterusRemove offending agent; treat cause
Dopa-Responsive DystoniaGCH1 mutation (AD) or TH gene (AR)Segawa syndrome; childhood dystonia + diurnal fluctuation; dramatic levodopa responseLevodopa (small doses lifelong)
Dopa-Responsive Dystonia (DRD) — "Segawa Disease": GCH1 mutation → reduced tetrahydrobiopterin (BH4) → reduced tyrosine hydroxylase activity → dopamine deficiency. Features: childhood-onset foot dystonia (worse in afternoon — diurnal fluctuation), gait abnormality, hyperreflexia in lower limbs, often misdiagnosed as cerebral palsy. Treatment: levodopa 1–2 mg/kg/day (minimal dose) = complete and sustained response for decades. ALL patients with childhood-onset dystonia should receive levodopa trial.

3.7.2 Isolated Cervical Dystonia (iCD)

Cervical Dystonia — Epidemiology & Diagnosis (Phokaewvarangkul, 2024; Albanese, Mov Disord 2023)

Definition (old term: Spasmodic torticollis): Most common adult-onset focal dystonia; involuntary sustained/intermittent contractions of neck muscles causing abnormal head postures and/or movements.

Epidemiology: Incidence 1.18/100,000 person-years; Prevalence ≥30/million; Peak onset: 5th–6th decade; F:M = 1.5:1

Two pathognomonic features:

  • Sensory trick (geste antagoniste): Touching adjacent area of head/face/neck reduces dystonia — seen in ~90% of CD patients; unique to dystonia
  • Pain: Cervical pain in 67–75%; NRS average 4.8/10; significantly impacts QoL
CD Subtype Classification (Col-Cap Concept)
Movement typeColumn subtypeCap subtypeDescription
Lateral bendLaterocollisLaterocaputEar toward shoulder (column = neck tilt; cap = head tilt)
Horizontal rotationTorticollisTorticaputChin toward shoulder
Anterior tiltAntecollisAntecaputChin to chest; common in MSA
Posterior tiltRetrocollisRetrocaputHead extension; common in PSP
Lateral shiftLateral shift (Col)Sagittal shift (Cap)Head shifted laterally/forward-backward without rotation

Col = movement of cervical column (C2-C7 level); Cap = movement of atlanto-axial/occipital joints (head only)

TWSTRS (Toronto Western Spasmodic Torticollis Rating Scale)
DomainMax scoreWhat it measures
Severity subscale35Head posture (maximal deviation, duration, effect of sensory trick), shoulder elevation, ROM, duration factor
Disability subscale20Activities of daily living impact (work, reading, driving, social)
Pain subscale20Pain severity (0–10 NRS), duration, disability from pain
Total TWSTRS75Higher score = more severe

3.7.3 Botulinum Toxin (BoNT) Treatment for Cervical Dystonia

BoNT Preparations — Comparison
ProductSerotypeBrandMolecular weightTarget proteinActive neurotoxin
OnabotulinumtoxinAABotox®900 kDa complexSNAP-250.90 ng/100U
AbobotulinumtoxinAADysport®500–900 kDaSNAP-252.69 ng/500U (highest active neurotoxin per approved dose)
IncobotulinumtoxinAAXeomin®150 kDa (naked, no complexing proteins)SNAP-250.40 ng/100U (lowest antibody potential)
RimabotulinumtoxinBBMyobloc®700 kDaSynaptobrevin (VAMP)—

Field et al., Toxins 2018: Dysport 500U contains 2.69 ng of active 150kDa neurotoxin vs Botox 100U = 0.90 ng — greater amounts of active neurotoxin per FDA-approved dose may explain longer duration of action reported with Dysport.

BoNT Clinical Evidence for CD

First-line treatment for cervical dystonia (Level A evidence)

  • Dysport (AboBoNTA) 500U RCT: Significant TWSTRS-Total reduction vs placebo at week 4; maintained to week 12; 72.6–81.5% patients did NOT require retreatment before week 16 (Esquenai A et al. Front Neurol 2020)
  • Dysport QoL Study (Mordin M et al. BMJ Open 2014): Significantly greater improvements in all SF-36 Physical Functioning, Role Physical, Bodily Pain, Role Emotional vs placebo (p≤0.03)
  • INTEREST IN CD2 long-term study (Colosimo C et al. JNS 2020): 466 patients over 3 years; TWSTRS continuously decreased; mean −7.4 ± 11.9 reduction from baseline to 36 months; patient satisfaction at peak effect: 84.1–91.2%; AEs: dysphagia 6.8%, muscle weakness 3.7%
  • Dysport vs Botox crossover RCT (Ranoux D et al. JNNP 2002; 54 patients): Dysport 1:3 and 1:4 more efficient than Botox on Tsui score and TWSTRS pain (p=0.01–0.04); dysphagia in 3% (Botox), 15.6% (Dysport 1:3), 17.3% (Dysport 1:4)
Thai FDA BoNT-A Guidelines for CD

Thai FDA indications (as per Appendix 3, Phokaewvarangkul):

  • Cervical dystonia (iCD) — allowed cervical area only; NOT face, mouth, or trunk
  • Hemifacial spasm (idiopathic)
  • Spasmodic dysphonia

Eligibility for BoNT-A treatment:

  • Must have failed conservative treatment >3 months
  • Frequency at least every 3 months (to reduce antibody formation risk)
  • Severity criteria for insurance coverage:
    • Mild (<20 units per session): ≥20% abnormal posture, impaired ADL
    • Moderate (20–50 units): 20–50% abnormal posture, impaired ADL, TWSTRS >5
    • Severe (>50 units): >50% abnormal posture, unable to stop, TWSTRS >5

Starting doses:

  • Botox: 50–150 units per session
  • Dysport: 250–500 units per session

Dose titration principle: Lower dose first session; increase by 30% if inadequate at follow-up; reduce by 30% if adverse effects. Key muscles: SCM, Splenius capitis, Semispinalis capitis, Trapezius, Levator scapulae (based on clinical pattern).

Muscle selection for CD by subtype:
  • Torticollis (chin toward right): Right sternocleidomastoid (contralateral rotation) + Left splenius capitis (ipsilateral rotation)
  • Laterocollis (right ear toward right shoulder): Right splenius capitis + Right SCM + Right levator scapulae
  • Retrocollis: Bilateral splenius capitis + semispinalis capitis
  • Antecollis: Bilateral SCM (for head flexion) — warning: this is the most dangerous injection (dysphagia, respiratory risk); start very low dose
  • Shoulder elevation: Ipsilateral trapezius
Use ultrasound or EMG guidance for deep muscles. Wait at least 3 months between sessions (antibody prevention).
BoNT Injection Technique — "2MIN" Checklist
  • M — Muscles to avoid at eye area: levator palpebrae superioris and frontalis muscles (risk of ptosis)
  • M — Makeup must be removed before injection
  • I — Injection types: intradermal, intramuscular, subcutaneous (choose per target)
  • I — Identifying target side (clinical exam, EMG/ultrasound guidance)
  • N — Needle: 29 or 30 gauge only (fine gauge minimizes spread)
  • N — No rubbing at treated area (prevents toxin spread)

For blepharospasm/hemifacial spasm injection: 40 units per eye (Botox); 10U per injection point × 4 points (preseptal/orbital orbicularis oculi); upper lid: direct needle away from center (avoid levator palpebrae); bevel side up; max 120U per eye.

Oral Medications for Dystonia
DrugDoseUseEvidence
TrihexyphenidylHigh dose: up to 60 mg/day (tolerated in children); 6–12 mg in adultsGeneralized dystonia; especially DYT1Level C; better in children
LevodopaStart low: 1 mg/kg/dayDRD (DYT5) — dramatic response; ALL childhood dystonia warrant trialLevel A for DRD
Clonazepam0.5–6 mg/dayAdjunct for anxious patients; myoclonic dystoniaLevel C
Baclofen (oral/intrathecal)10–80 mg/day; intrathecal pump for severe generalizedGeneralized dystonia, especially spastic dystoniaLevel C; intrathecal for selected
Tetrabenazine25–75 mg/daySecondary dystonia; tardive dystoniaReduces involuntary movements; depression risk

DBS for dystonia: GPi target; best evidence for DYT1 generalized dystonia (Level A); focal dystonia refractory to BoNT; onset may take weeks-months (unlike PD); improvement continues up to 2 years.

References: Albanese A et al. EFNS/MDS-ES recommendations for the diagnosis of primary dystonias. Eur J Neurol 2011;18:5–18. Phokaewvarangkul O. Optimizing Cervical Dystonia Management. CMU lecture 2024. Field M et al. AbobotulinumtoxinA, OnabotulinumtoxinA, and IncobotulinumtoxinA neurotoxin content. Toxins 2018;10:535. Colosimo C et al. Cumulative effects of long-term abobotulinumtoxinA in cervical dystonia. JNS 2020;416:117015.
3.8 Chorea & Huntington's Disease

3.8.1 Chorea — Differential Diagnosis

HereditaryWilson's diseaseYoung-onset; KF rings; liver disease; copper
HereditaryNeuroacanthocytosis, DRPLA, SCA17, BenignHCRare; genetic testing
AutoimmuneSydenham's choreaPost-streptococcal; children; "milkmaid grip"; GABHS
AutoimmuneSLE, antiphospholipid syndromeYoung women; ANA, anti-dsDNA, antiphospholipid Ab
AutoimmuneAnti-NMDAR encephalitisOrofacial dyskinesia + psychiatric + autonomic

Chorea gravidarumPregnancyPrior Sydenham's or antiphospholipid; resolves post-delivery
InfectionsEncephalitis, HIV, CJD, toxoplasmosis (BG)Acute/subacute; fever, CSF changes

3.8.2 Huntington's Disease

Huntington's Disease — Key Facts

Genetics: Autosomal dominant; CAG repeat expansion in HTT gene (chromosome 4p16.3); normally <27 repeats; HD if ≥36 repeats; >40 = full penetrance; 36–39 = reduced penetrance; juvenile HD (<20y onset) has >60 repeats (usually paternal transmission due to anticipation)

Pathology: Selective loss of medium spiny neurons in caudate nucleus and putamen → striatal atrophy; "caudate atrophy" + enlarged frontal horns (boxcar ventricles) on MRI

Clinical triad:

  • Motor: Chorea (early) → hypokinesia and rigidity (late); dysphagia, dysarthria; oculomotor (saccade initiation delay, broken smooth pursuit)
  • Cognitive: Subcortical dementia (executive dysfunction, processing speed, memory); unlike Alzheimer's — relatively preserved language and naming early
  • Psychiatric: Depression (40%), irritability, apathy, OCD, psychosis; psychiatric symptoms often precede motor symptoms

Diagnosis: Clinical features + positive genetic testing (CAG ≥36). Predictive testing requires genetic counseling (pre-test and post-test). MRI: caudate atrophy, dilated frontal horns (bicaudate index elevated). PET/SPECT: decreased striatal metabolism.

HD Management
SymptomTreatmentNotes
ChoreaTetrabenazine 25–75 mg/day; Deutetrabenazine (preferred — fewer SE); ValbenazineVMAT2 inhibitors; depression risk; sedation; parkinsonism
Chorea (alternative)Haloperidol, aripiprazole, quetiapineAntipsychotics reduce chorea via D2 blockade; use atypical first
Chorea (alternative)Amantadine 200–400 mg/dayModest benefit; NMDA antagonism
DepressionSSRIs (sertraline, fluoxetine) preferredMirtazapine if insomnia comorbid
Psychosis/irritabilityQuetiapine, aripiprazoleAvoid haloperidol for long-term (worsens rigidity later)
CognitiveNo proven treatment; memantine or rivastigmine modestSupportive care; occupational therapy
DysphagiaSpeech therapy; modified diet; PEG late-stageMajor cause of aspiration pneumonia

No disease-modifying therapy approved (as of 2025). Investigational: antisense oligonucleotides (ASO) targeting HTT mRNA — Phase III trials ongoing.

Prognosis: Mean survival 15–20 years from onset; death typically from aspiration pneumonia, cardiovascular disease, suicide.

Tardive Dyskinesia (TD): Involuntary orobuccolingual-masticatory choreiform movements after chronic exposure to dopamine receptor blockers (DRBAs: antipsychotics, metoclopramide, prochlorperazine). Features: oro-buccal-lingual (smacking, chewing, tongue protrusion) + trunk rocking + finger piano-playing + dorsiflexion of toes. Appears after months-years of DRBA. Persists or worsens even after stopping. Treatment: (1) Reduce/stop offending drug; (2) Switch to clozapine/quetiapine (less D2 blockade); (3) VMAT2 inhibitors: valbenazine (40–80 mg/day; FDA-approved 2017) or deutetrabenazine (12–48 mg/day; FDA-approved 2017) — most effective treatments.
References: Ross CA et al. Huntington disease: natural history, biomarkers and prospects for therapeutics. Nat Rev Neurosci 2014;15:375–387. Bhidayasiri R et al. Evidence-based guideline: Treatment of tardive syndromes. Neurology 2013;81:463–469.
3.9 Wilson's Disease
Wilson's Disease — Key Facts (EASL Guidelines 2012)

Genetics: Autosomal recessive; ATP7B gene (chromosome 13q14.3) mutation → defective hepatic copper-transporting ATPase → copper accumulates in liver, brain, cornea, kidneys

Incidence: 1/30,000; gene frequency 1/90; consider in all patients <55 years with unexplained liver disease, neurological/psychiatric symptoms, or movement disorder

Clinical Manifestations
SystemFeatures
HepaticAcute hepatitis → fulminant hepatic failure (Coombs-negative hemolytic anemia); chronic hepatitis → cirrhosis; liver disease often precedes neurological symptoms
NeurologicalTremor (wing-beating/Holmes tremor most classic), dysarthria, dysphagia, ataxia, dystonia, chorea, rigidity, bradykinesia (Parkinsonism), gait abnormality
PsychiatricPersonality change (50%), depression, psychosis, mania, cognitive decline; often presenting complaint in young adults
OcularKayser-Fleischer (KF) rings — golden-brown ring at corneal periphery (Descemet's membrane); slit-lamp required; present in >95% of neurological Wilson's but only 50–60% of hepatic
OtherRenal tubular acidosis (Fanconi syndrome), premature osteoporosis, cardiomyopathy, sunflower cataracts
Wilson's Disease Diagnosis (Leipzig Score, EASL 2012)
FeatureScore
KF rings present+2
Neuropsychiatric symptoms typical of WD+2
Serum ceruloplasmin <0.1 g/L+2 (0.1–0.2 g/L = +1)
Coombs-negative hemolytic anemia (with high serum copper)+1
24-hour urine copper >2 μmol/day (in absence of acute hepatitis)+2
Liver copper >250 μg/g dry weight+2
Mutation analysis: two disease-causing mutations+4 (one mutation = +1)

Score ≥4 = likely WD; Score 3 = possible WD; Score ≤2 = WD unlikely

Essential investigations in suspected WD:

  • Serum ceruloplasmin (low <20 mg/dL in 95% of neurological WD; false negatives with inflammation/estrogen)
  • 24-hour urine copper (>100 μg/day in symptomatic WD; >40 μg/day = abnormal)
  • Slit-lamp examination for KF rings
  • LFTs, CBC (hemolytic anemia)
  • Brain MRI: "face of the giant panda" sign (midbrain T2); T2 hyperintensity putamen, caudate, thalamus, brainstem
  • ATP7B genetic testing (confirm diagnosis; screen siblings)
Wilson's Disease Treatment
DrugClassDoseIndicationKey issues
PenicillamineCopper chelator250 mg QID (+ pyridoxine 25 mg/day); titrate upFirst choice in hepatic WD; established for decadesNeurological worsening on initiation (25%); SLE-like, nephrotic syndrome, penicillin allergy cross-reactivity; pyridoxine deficiency
TrientineCopper chelator (different mechanism)250 mg TID–QID before mealsPreferred when neurological WD (less neurological worsening); penicillamine intoleranceIron deficiency; less SE than penicillamine; drug of choice in neurological WD
Zinc acetateBlocks intestinal copper absorption50 mg TID (elemental zinc)Maintenance after chelation; presymptomatic (siblings); pregnancyGastric irritation; less effective for hepatic decompensation; monitor LFTs
Liver transplantationCurative for hepatic—Acute liver failure; decompensated cirrhosis; neurological WD (controversial)Corrects hepatic metabolic defect; KF rings and neurological symptoms may improve
Wilson's disease in movement disorders: The single most important treatable cause of young-onset movement disorder. Wing-beating tremor (Holmes/rubral tremor = rest + postural + kinetic) is the CLASSIC movement disorder of Wilson's, but can also present as dysarthria, dystonia, chorea, or parkinsonism. Brain MRI: "face of giant panda" (midbrain) = pathognomonic. ALWAYS check in patients <50 with unexplained neurological + hepatic disease. Treatment reverses or stabilizes neurological symptoms in most patients.
3.10 Movement Disorder Emergencies

3.10.1 Neuroleptic Malignant Syndrome (NMS)

NMS — Diagnosis & Management

Definition: Life-threatening idiosyncratic reaction to dopamine receptor blockers (DRBAs) or rapid withdrawal of dopaminergic agents; characterized by hyperthermia + muscle rigidity + autonomic instability + altered consciousness.

Levenson Diagnostic Criteria (1985):

  • 3 Major criteria: Hyperthermia (>38°C), muscle rigidity, elevated CK (>2× ULN)
  • 9 Minor criteria: Tachycardia, BP fluctuation, tachypnea, altered consciousness, diaphoresis, leukocytosis, elevated LDH, abnormal LFTs, incontinence
  • Diagnosis: 3 major OR 2 major + 4 minor (after excluding other causes)

Pathophysiology: Central DA blockade (especially in striatum and hypothalamus) → impaired thermoregulation + muscle rigidity. Peripheral: direct muscle toxicity from continued contraction → rhabdomyolysis.

Treatment — Woodbury staging:

StageSeverityTreatment
IMild (rigidity, temp <38°C, CK ↑)Discontinue offending drug; IV fluids; cooling; benzos for agitation
IIModerate (temp 38–40°C, hemodynamics stable)Stage I + Bromocriptine 2.5–10 mg TID or Amantadine 100 mg BID–TID (DA agonism)
IIISevere (temp >40°C, CK >1000, renal failure)Stage II + Dantrolene 1–2.5 mg/kg IV q6h (max 10 mg/kg/day) — direct muscle relaxant (blocks RyR)
IVLife-threatening (rhabdomyolysis, respiratory failure)ICU; Dantrolene + Bromocriptine; dialysis if renal failure; intubation
VRefractoryElectroconvulsive therapy (ECT) as last resort

Mortality: 5–30% (historical); now ~5–10% with early recognition/treatment; death from respiratory failure, cardiovascular collapse, acute renal failure.

Wait 2 weeks before restarting antipsychotic after NMS resolution; switch to lower-potency agent; monitor closely.

NMS vs Serotonin Syndrome:
FeatureNMSSerotonin Syndrome
CauseDA receptor blockers; DA withdrawalSerotonergic agents (SSRIs, SNRIs, MAOIs, triptans, fentanyl, dextromethorphan)
OnsetDays to weeks (insidious)Hours (rapid — within 24 hrs)
RigidityLead-pipe rigidity (extrapyramidal)Clonus >> rigidity; hyperreflexia
PupilsNormalMydriasis
TremorRareYes (coarse, action)
TreatmentDantrolene + bromocriptineCyproheptadine (5-HT2 antagonist) 4–8 mg; benzodiazepines; cooling

3.10.2 Acute Dystonic Reaction

Cause: Acute exposure to dopamine receptor blockers (antipsychotics, metoclopramide, prochlorperazine). Occurs within hours to 5 days of starting or increasing dose.

Clinical features: Oculogyric crisis (eyes roll up/sideways), torticollis, trismus, laryngospasm (emergency!), opisthotonus, dystonic gait.

Treatment:

  • First-line: Benztropine (Cogentin) 1–2 mg IV/IM OR Diphenhydramine (Benadryl) 25–50 mg IV/IM — rapid reversal within minutes
  • Follow-up: Oral anticholinergic (benztropine 1–2 mg BID or trihexyphenidyl) for 4–7 days (to prevent recurrence)
  • Laryngospasm: Airway management; benztropine IV
  • If offending drug cannot be stopped: prophylactic low-dose anticholinergic

3.10.3 Status Dystonicus

Definition: Escalating, severe dystonic contractions causing rhabdomyolysis, hyperthermia, renal failure, respiratory failure; life-threatening emergency requiring ICU care.

Causes: Sudden withdrawal of baclofen/benzodiazepines; infection triggering dystonic storm; medication adjustment in dystonia patient; underlying metabolic/neurodegenerative disease.

Management:

  • ICU admission; airway management
  • IV benzodiazepines (high dose)
  • IV anticholinergics (trihexyphenidyl)
  • IV dantrolene (for hyperthermia/rigidity)
  • Propofol infusion (sedation)
  • Continuous intrathecal baclofen if pump available
  • Emergency DBS activation if patient has implant
  • Treat precipitating cause

3.10.4 Acute Chorea/Hemiballism Emergencies

Hyperglycemic hemichorea-hemiballism: Non-ketotic hyperosmolar state → hemichorea/hemiballism contralateral to T1-hyperintense (striatal) lesion on MRI. Treatment: glucose normalization; dopamine receptor blocker (haloperidol 0.5–2 mg BID) if severe; almost always resolves with metabolic correction.

Sydenham's chorea: Post-GAS streptococcal autoimmune; milkmaid grip; "Jack-in-the-box" tongue; often with carditis (rheumatic fever). Treatment: valproate first-line; penicillin prophylaxis (secondary prevention for RF); IVIG/steroids for severe cases.

References: Strawn JR et al. Neuroleptic malignant syndrome. Am J Psychiatry 2007;164:870–876. Woodbury MM. NMS staging. 1984. Frucht SJ. Treatment of movement disorder emergencies. Neurotherapeutics 2014;11:208–12. Bhidayasiri R, Truong DD. Chorea and related disorders. Postgrad Med J 2004;80:527–34.
Q: A 28-year-old man with schizophrenia received haloperidol 5 mg IM 2 days ago. He now presents with neck twisted to the right, difficulty swallowing, and eyes forced upward. Temperature is 37.2°C, BP 130/80, HR 90. What is the diagnosis and immediate treatment?
Acute Dystonic Reaction (ADR) to haloperidol — specifically: torticollis + oculogyric crisis. Immediate treatment: Benztropine 1–2 mg IV (or diphenhydramine 25–50 mg IV if benztropine unavailable) — reversal usually within 5–15 minutes. If no IV access: IM is equally effective. Follow-up: Oral benztropine 1–2 mg BID × 4–7 days (prevents recurrence as antipsychotic effect continues). If antipsychotic is needed long-term: switch to atypical (quetiapine, aripiprazole — less D2 blockade); consider prophylactic anticholinergic. Distinguished from NMS: No fever, no rigidity, no CK elevation, no autonomic instability → ADR not NMS. NMS is more insidious, systemic, and life-threatening.
Q: A 45-year-old man with PD is admitted for urgent surgery and becomes NPO. His usual medications (levodopa 300 mg TID + pramipexole 1 mg TID) are held. On day 2, he develops fever 39.5°C, generalized rigidity, confusion, and CK 5000 U/L. What is the diagnosis and treatment?
NMS-like syndrome from acute dopaminergic withdrawal (Parkinsonism-Hyperpyrexia Syndrome — PHS). Clinically identical to NMS but caused by abrupt discontinuation of dopaminergic drugs rather than DA receptor blockade. Treatment: (1) Restart dopaminergic therapy IMMEDIATELY — via NG tube, dispersible levodopa, or parenteral route if available; (2) Dantrolene 1–2.5 mg/kg IV q6h for rigidity/hyperthermia; (3) Bromocriptine 2.5–10 mg q8h via NG; (4) ICU monitoring; cooling; IV fluids (rhabdomyolysis prevention — target urine output 200–300 mL/hr); (5) Avoid typical antipsychotics. Prevention: Never make PD patients NPO without alternative dopaminergic route. Use dispersible levodopa via NG tube perioperatively. Alternative: rotigotine transdermal patch (continuous DA stimulation, no GI needed).
↑ Back to top

4. Headache Disorders

Headache disorders are the most common neurological complaints worldwide, with migraine ranking as the leading cause of disability in adults aged 15–49. This section covers the full ICHD-3 classification, pathophysiology (trigeminovascular pathway, CGRP), acute and preventive treatment algorithms, special populations, TACs, trigeminal neuralgia, and red flag evaluation at headache-specialist depth.

4.1 Pathophysiology & Classification of Headache
KEY PATHOPHYSIOLOGIC MECHANISMS

1. Cortical Spreading Depression (CSD): A self-propagating wave of neuronal and glial depolarization spreading at ~3–5 mm/min across cortex. The neurophysiologic substrate of migraine aura. CSD activates meningeal nociceptors via release of K⁺, H⁺, glutamate, and arachidonic acid metabolites, triggering trigeminovascular activation.

2. Trigeminovascular Pathway: Central to migraine pain generation. Trigeminal nerve (CN V1) innervates dural vessels → releases CGRP, substance P, PACAP → vasodilation + neurogenic inflammation → signals travel via trigeminal ganglion → Trigeminal Nucleus Caudalis (TNC, C1–C2) → thalamus → somatosensory cortex. Convergence with cervical afferents at TNC explains cervical pain and cutaneous allodynia.

3. CGRP (Calcitonin Gene-Related Peptide): The key neuropeptide in migraine. Released from trigeminal terminals during attacks → vasodilation + pain sensitization. CGRP levels elevated during attacks, normalize with triptan treatment. Target of all modern acute (gepants) and preventive (CGRP mAbs) therapies.

4. Central Sensitization: Repeated activation → sensitization of TNC neurons → cutaneous allodynia (late-attack marker). Sensitization of thalamic neurons → allodynia distant from head. Central sensitization indicates poor triptan response (treat early). Chronic sensitization → chronification of migraine.

5. Hypothalamic Involvement: "Migraine generator." Premonitory symptoms (yawning, fatigue, food craving) precede attacks by 24–48h, originating from hypothalamic activation. Also explains circadian patterns in cluster headache (suprachiasmatic nucleus).

6. Descending Modulation: Periaqueductal gray (PAG) and rostral ventromedial medulla (RVM) modulate pain descending to TNC. PAG dysfunction → loss of inhibitory control → headache facilitation. PAG iron deposition seen in chronic migraine MRI.

ICHD-3 Headache Classification Framework

CategoryMajor TypesKey Distinguishing Feature
Part 1: Primary HeadacheMigraine, TTH, TACs, Other primaryNo underlying structural cause
Part 2: Secondary HeadacheHead/neck trauma, Vascular, Non-vascular intracranial, Substance, Infection, Homeostasis, Cranial structure, PsychiatricCausally related to another condition
Part 3: Painful Cranial NeuropathiesTrigeminal neuralgia, Other cranial neuralgiasNerve-territory pain distribution
🧠 Specialist Pearl – Migraine Subtypes Spectrum: Migraine without aura (MwoA) is most common (70–75%). Migraine with aura (MwA) 25–30%; aura is fully reversible cortical/brainstem symptom. Chronic migraine (CM) ≥15 days/month ≥3 months with ≥8 migraine features. Status migrainosus: attack >72h. Medication overuse headache (MOH) complicates ≥50% of CM cases. Key: migraine disability is vastly underestimated — MIDAS scoring essential.

Migraine Epidemiology

Global prevalence ~15% (women 18–19%, men 6–7%). Peak reproductive years women age 25–55. F:M ratio 3:1 (hormonal influence). Point prevalence higher than diabetes + asthma + epilepsy combined. Leading cause of disability in 15–49 year age group (GBD 2019). Annual direct/indirect costs enormous (lost productivity, ED visits, medications). Thai data: prevalence ~12.6%.

ICHD-3 Classification Hierarchy: 1-digit code = group (e.g., 1 = Migraine), 2-digit = type (1.1 = MwoA), 3-digit = subtype (1.2.1 = Typical aura with headache), 4-digit = sub-subtype (1.2.1.1 = Migraine with typical aura). All codes must match minimum diagnostic criteria.
4.2 Migraine Without Aura (MwoA) — ICHD-3 1.1
ICHD-3 DIAGNOSTIC CRITERIA — MwoA (1.1) [MEMORIZE]

A. At least 5 attacks fulfilling criteria B–D

B. Headache lasting 4–72 hours (untreated or unsuccessfully treated)

C. Headache has ≥2 of 4 characteristics (mnemonic: MUPA):

  • Moderate–severe intensity (limits/prohibits daily activity)
  • Unilateral location
  • Pulsating quality
  • Aggravated by routine physical activity (walking, climbing stairs)

D. During headache, ≥1 of:

  • Nausea and/or vomiting
  • Photophobia AND phonophobia

E. Not better accounted for by another ICHD-3 diagnosis

Note: Bilateral location in ~40%; non-pulsating in ~50% — diagnosis still met if other criteria fulfilled

Clinical Features of MwoA

FeatureClassic PresentationFrequency (%)
LocationUnilateral (frontotemporal)~60%
QualityPulsating/throbbing~75%
SeverityModerate–severe~90%
NauseaPresent~80%
VomitingPresent~50%
PhotophobiaPresent~90%
PhonophobiaPresent~85%
OsmophobiaPresent (not in criteria)~40%
Cutaneous allodyniaScalp/face/arm tenderness~60%
PostdromeFatigue, cognitive fog~80%

Four Phases of Migraine Attack

PhaseTimingFeaturesNeurobiological Basis
Premonitory24–48h beforeYawning, fatigue, food craving (esp. sweet), mood change, neck stiffness, increased urinationHypothalamic activation; dopaminergic activation
Aura20–60 min before (if present)Visual, sensory, speech symptoms — fully reversible, march over ≥5 minCortical spreading depression
Headache4–72hMUPA + nausea/photo/phono; worsens with movementTrigeminovascular activation; CGRP release
PostdromeUp to 48h afterFatigue, cognitive slowing, mood change (low/high), scalp tendernessCortical hypoactivity; ongoing sensitization
🧠 ID Migraine Screen (Validated 3-Question Tool): (1) Has headache limited activity for ≥1 day in past 3 months? (2) Nausea/vomiting with headache? (3) Sensitivity to light? — Sensitivity 81%, Specificity 75% for diagnosing migraine in primary care. ≥2/3 positive = migraine likely. MIDAS score quantifies disability (Grade I–IV).

MIDAS (Migraine Disability Assessment) Score

GradeScoreDisability LevelClinical Action
I0–5Little/noneAcute-only treatment
II6–10MildAcute ± consider prevention
III11–20ModerateAcute + preventive treatment
IV≥21SevereAcute + aggressive prevention + multidisciplinary

HALT-90 Index: headache days in past 90 days — ≥10 days/90d = frequent episodic migraine; ≥15 = chronic migraine. Tracks frequency simply.

Q: A 32-year-old woman presents with 10-year history of headaches, unilateral throbbing, severity 8/10, with nausea and photophobia, lasting ~18 hours, occurring 5–6 times/year. What is the diagnosis and what first-line acute treatment is most appropriate?
Diagnosis: Migraine Without Aura (ICHD-3 1.1) — fulfills all criteria: ≥5 attacks, 4–72h duration, unilateral + pulsating + severe (≥2/4 MUPA) + nausea/photophobia (criterion D).
Severity stratification: Moderate–severe → Stratified care → Triptan as first-line acute specific therapy.
Choice: Sumatriptan 50–100 mg oral (most studied) or Eletriptan 40 mg (faster onset, higher efficacy). May combine with naproxen 500 mg for better sustained pain-free rate (POZEN study).
Frequency: 5–6 attacks/year → MIDAS Grade I–II; preventive not indicated. Headache diary + lifestyle triggers recommended.
4.3 Migraine With Aura & Subtypes (Hemiplegic, Brainstem, Retinal, Vestibular)
ICHD-3 DIAGNOSTIC CRITERIA — Migraine With Aura (1.2) [MEMORIZE]

A. At least 2 attacks fulfilling B–C

B. ≥1 of the following fully reversible aura symptoms:

  • Visual (most common: scintillating scotoma, fortification spectra)
  • Sensory (numbness, tingling — typically unilateral, hand-face)
  • Speech/language (dysphasia)
  • Motor (weakness) — only in Hemiplegic Migraine
  • Brainstem (dysarthria, vertigo, tinnitus, diplopia, ataxia, decreased consciousness)
  • Retinal (monocular visual symptoms)

C. ≥3 of 6 aura characteristics:

  • ≥1 aura spreads gradually over ≥5 minutes
  • ≥2 auras occur in succession
  • Each aura lasts 5–60 minutes
  • ≥1 aura is unilateral
  • ≥1 aura is positive (scintillating, tingling) — note visual negative OK too
  • Aura accompanied or followed within 60 minutes by headache

D. Not better accounted for by another ICHD-3 diagnosis; TIA excluded

Types of Aura — Visual Aura Details

Scintillating scotoma: Classic — expanding arc of shimmering zigzag lights (fortification spectrum) surrounding a blind spot, starting central, spreading peripherally over 20–30 min. Corresponds to CSD propagation across visual cortex (V1). Pathognomonic of migraine aura when typical.

Simple visual aura: Flashing lights, spots, or lines without fortification. Positive phenomena from CSD activation phase; negative phenomena (scotoma) from CSD suppression phase.

Typical aura duration: 5–60 minutes (key: if >60 min → suspect hemiplegic, stroke, or prolonged aura). Aura-to-headache interval up to 60 minutes allowed.

Migraine Subtypes — Key Differentiators

SubtypeICHD-3 CodeDefining FeatureKey Considerations
Typical aura with headache1.2.1.1Visual/sensory/speech aura only; migraine headache followsMost common aura subtype
Typical aura without headache1.2.1.2Same aura, no headacheOlder patients; DD: TIA (aura spreads gradually, TIA symptoms appear abruptly)
Hemiplegic migraine1.2.3Motor weakness (fully reversible) as part of auraMay mimic stroke — CT/MRI mandatory first episode; never use triptans/ergots acutely
Familial hemiplegic (FHM)1.2.3.1Hemiplegic + 1st/2nd degree relative sameFHM1 (CACNA1A), FHM2 (ATP1A2), FHM3 (SCN1A) — ion channel mutations
Sporadic hemiplegic (SHM)1.2.3.2Hemiplegic, no family historySame as FHM1 in severity; consider genetic testing
Migraine with brainstem aura1.2.2≥2 brainstem symptoms (dysarthria, vertigo, tinnitus, diplopia, ataxia, altered consciousness, bilateral paresthesia)Previously "basilar-type migraine"; motor weakness absent; if motor → hemiplegic
Retinal migraine1.2.4Monocular visual symptoms (blindness or positive phenomena) in one eye onlyMust examine eye during attack; ophthalmology referral; may cause permanent monocular vision loss; triptans relatively contraindicated
🧠 Aura vs. TIA — Critical Distinction:
  • Aura: Positive symptoms → negative symptoms; gradual spread (march) over ≥5 min; typically homonymous (hemianopia) not monocular; young woman; prior identical episodes
  • TIA: Sudden onset; negative symptoms predominate (weakness, numbness, aphasia); monocular visual loss (amaurosis fugax = ICA territory); older age + vascular risk factors; MRI DWI may show acute lesion
  • Key test: MRI brain DWI/ADC — acute ischemic changes = TIA/stroke; normal = favors aura
  • Migraine with aura ↑ stroke risk 2× (especially in women who smoke + use OCP — avoid OCP in MwA + smoking)

Vestibular Migraine — ICHD-3 A1.6.5 (Appendix)

VESTIBULAR MIGRAINE DIAGNOSTIC CRITERIA

A. At least 5 episodes fulfilling B–D

B. Current or past history of migraine without or with aura

C. Vestibular symptoms of moderate–severe intensity lasting 5 min–72h

  • Spontaneous vertigo (internal or external)
  • Positional vertigo (triggered by head movement)
  • Visually induced vertigo
  • Head motion-induced vertigo
  • Head motion-induced nausea

D. ≥50% of episodes associated with ≥1 migraine feature:

  • Headache with ≥2 migraine characteristics (MUPA)
  • Photophobia and phonophobia
  • Visual aura

E. Not better accounted for by another vestibular or ICHD-3 diagnosis

Vestibular Migraine — DDx Table

Q: A 28-year-old woman with known migraine with aura presents with sudden onset of right-sided weakness and aphasia during a headache. She has had 3 prior attacks with right arm tingling followed by left-sided headache. How do you differentiate hemiplegic migraine from stroke, and what acute treatment is contraindicated?
Differentiation:
  • Prior identical episodes (3 previous) with same weakness + young age favor hemiplegic migraine (HM)
  • But: cannot exclude stroke clinically → Urgent MRI DWI/ADC mandatory
  • HM: No acute DWI lesion; may show cortical swelling, perfusion asymmetry; EEG may show slow waves
  • Family history of same attacks → Familial HM (CACNA1A/ATP1A2/SCN1A mutation)
  • HM attacks may last hours to days; full recovery expected (though may take longer)
Contraindicated in Hemiplegic Migraine:
  • Triptans — vasoconstriction concern; theoretically may extend ischemia in affected hemisphere
  • Ergotamine — same vasoconstrictive mechanism; absolutely avoid
Safe acute treatment for HM: NSAIDs (naproxen, ibuprofen), paracetamol, dopamine antagonists (metoclopramide), IV magnesium, IV valproate. If stroke cannot be excluded: treat as stroke emergency (tPA if criteria met within window).
🧠 Migraine With Aura & Stroke Risk: MwA confers 2× increased ischemic stroke risk vs. non-migraineurs. Risk multiplies:
  • MwA + OCP: RR 8–13×
  • MwA + smoking: RR 9–10×
  • MwA + OCP + smoking: RR ~34× (absolute risk still low but unacceptable)
Guideline: Avoid combined OCP in MwA + smoking. Progestin-only OCP or non-hormonal contraception preferred. Assess cardiovascular risk at each visit for MwA patients.
4.4 Chronic Migraine & Medication Overuse Headache (MOH)
ICHD-3 DIAGNOSTIC CRITERIA — Chronic Migraine (1.3)

A. Headache (tension-type-like and/or migraine-like) on ≥15 days/month for >3 months, fulfilling criteria B and C

B. Occurring in a patient who has had at least 5 attacks fulfilling criteria for 1.1 (MwoA) and/or 1.2 (MwA)

C. On ≥8 days/month for >3 months, fulfilling any of:

  • Criteria C and D for Migraine without aura (1.1)
  • Criteria B and C for Migraine with aura (1.2)
  • Believed by the patient to be migraine at onset and relieved by triptan/ergot

D. Not better accounted for by another ICHD-3 diagnosis

Key concept: CM diagnosis can be made even when medication overuse is present — they are separate diagnoses that frequently coexist

Chronification Risk Factors (Migraine → Chronic Migraine)

CategoryRisk FactorsModifiable?
BehavioralMedication overuse (strongest), sleep disorder (insomnia/OSA), caffeine overuse, physical inactivity, obesity (BMI >30 → 5× risk)Yes
PsychiatricDepression, anxiety, PTSD, somatization — all independent risk factorsYes (treat comorbidities)
Social/EnvironmentalLife events, stressors, socioeconomic status, trauma historyPartially
BiologicalHigh attack frequency (≥5 attacks/mo), female sex, allodynia (central sensitization marker), family history of CMNo
MedicalHead injury, snoring/OSA, hypothyroidism, asthma (↑ inflammation)Yes
ICHD-3 — MEDICATION OVERUSE HEADACHE (MOH) Criteria (8.2)

A. Headache on ≥15 days/month

B. Regular overuse for >3 months of ≥1 drug taken as acute/symptomatic headache treatment:

Drug ClassOveruse ThresholdICHD-3 Code
Ergotamine≥10 days/month8.2.1
Triptans≥10 days/month8.2.2
Non-opioid analgesics (NSAIDs, paracetamol)≥15 days/month8.2.3
Opioids≥10 days/month8.2.4
Combination analgesics≥10 days/month8.2.5
Multiple drug classes without individual overuse≥10 days/month combined8.2.6

C. Not better accounted for by another ICHD-3 diagnosis

MOH Pathophysiology

Chronic exposure to acute medications → downregulation of endogenous pain modulation (serotonergic descending inhibition reduced), upregulation of CGRP expression, cortical hypersensitivity. Analgesic-specific: opioids cause receptor desensitization and paradoxical hyperalgesia. Triptans: 5-HT1B/D desensitization → reduced efficacy + rebound. Reversal requires withdrawal; full recovery may take 2–3 months.

🧠 MOH Management Strategy — 4-Step Approach:
  1. Diagnose and Educate: Explain MOH mechanism non-judgmentally; written information; headache diary. Many patients initially deny overuse — review diary together.
  2. Withdraw the overused medication: Abrupt discontinuation preferred for most (triptans, NSAIDs, paracetamol). Gradual taper for opioids and barbiturates (seizure/withdrawal risk). Expect withdrawal headache for 2–10 days (reassure patient this is expected and will improve).
  3. Bridging therapy during withdrawal: IV/IM dexamethasone 8 mg (reduces withdrawal symptoms per RCT); naproxen 500 mg bid if not the overused drug; amitriptyline 10 mg; prednisolone 60 mg × 5 days taper (evidence mixed).
  4. Start preventive therapy: Topiramate (most RCT evidence in MOH), valproate, amitriptyline. CGRP mAbs: erenumab effective even without withdrawal first (PROMISE-1/2 subgroup analyses). BoNT-A PREEMPT protocol approved for CM ± MOH.

Resistant vs. Refractory Migraine — EHF Consensus Definitions

TermDefinition (EHF 2020)
Resistant MigraineFailed ≥3 preventive drug classes (adequate dose, ≥3 months each) AND ≥4 migraine days/month with significant disability despite treatment. One of the failed drugs should be topiramate or valproate.
Refractory MigraineResistant migraine PLUS failed all CGRP mAbs (or gepants as preventives) AND BoNT-A. "End-stage" — requires tertiary headache center referral; neuromodulation devices, CGRP-nasal delivery, combination strategies.
Aspiration Goals for CM Treatment (IHS Guidelines):
Primary goal: ≥50% reduction in headache days/month
Secondary goals: ≥50% reduction in acute medication intake; ≥30% improvement in MIDAS/HIT-6 score; reduction in allodynia; improvement in sleep; return to work/social function
Q: A 45-year-old woman has headache on 22 days/month for 6 months. She uses ibuprofen 600 mg on 18 days/month. She has history of episodic migraine. Which diagnoses are present and what is the management priority?
Diagnoses (both present simultaneously):
  1. Chronic Migraine (ICHD-3 1.3): ≥15 days/month >3 months with migraine history ✓
  2. Medication Overuse Headache (ICHD-3 8.2.3): NSAIDs ≥15 days/month >3 months ✓ (threshold for non-opioid analgesics = 15 days)
Management priority:
  1. First: NSAID withdrawal (abrupt OK for NSAIDs) + patient education
  2. Bridging: Dexamethasone 8 mg IM/IV or prednisolone taper to manage withdrawal headache
  3. Start prevention simultaneously: Topiramate 25 mg → 100 mg (Level A evidence in CM/MOH) or consider BoNT-A if frequent enough
  4. Rescue acute: Limit to ≤10 days/month; switch class (if was using NSAID → use triptan as needed, limit <10d/mo)
  5. Follow-up: 4–6 weeks; if no improvement after withdrawal → reassess MOH diagnosis
4.5 Acute Treatment of Migraine — Stratified Care & IV Rescue Protocols
STRATIFIED CARE ALGORITHM — ACUTE MIGRAINE

Step-care across attacks (preferred): Assess severity + disability → select treatment tier accordingly:

SeverityMIDASFirst-Line AgentDose
Mild–Moderate (no disability)Grade INSAIDs: Naproxen, Ibuprofen, AspirinNaproxen 500–1000 mg; Ibuprofen 400–600 mg; Aspirin 900–1000 mg + Metoclopramide 10 mg
Mild–ModerateGrade I–IIParacetamol (if NSAID contraindicated)1000 mg oral; less effective than NSAIDs alone
Moderate–Severe (with disability)Grade II–IVTriptans (5-HT1B/1D agonists)See triptan table below
Triptan-refractory or CIAnyGepants (CGRP antagonists)Rimegepant 75 mg ODT (oral dissolving tablet)
Very severe/vomiting (ED)StatusIV/IM rescue protocolSee IV rescue table below

Triptan Comparison Table

DrugDoseRouteOnset2h Pain-Free RateKey Notes
Sumatriptan50–100 mgOral30–60 min~29%Most studied; also SC 6 mg (fastest, highest efficacy), nasal spray 20 mg
Sumatriptan SC6 mgSC injection10–15 min~55%Fastest onset; best for rapid severe attacks; auto-injector available
Eletriptan20–40 mgOral30 min~38%Highest oral efficacy; more lipophilic; avoid if hepatic impairment (CYP3A4)
Rizatriptan10 mg (5 mg with propranolol)Oral (ODT available)30 min~37%Reduce dose to 5 mg if on propranolol (MAO-A interaction)
Zolmitriptan2.5–5 mgOral / nasal spray30–45 min~32%Nasal spray 5 mg useful if vomiting
Almotriptan12.5 mgOral30–45 min~32%Favorable tolerability; less chest symptoms
Frovatriptan2.5 mgOral60–90 min~26%Longest T½ (~26h); useful for long attacks, menstrual migraine prophylaxis (mini-prophylaxis)
Naratriptan2.5 mgOral60–90 min~24%Slow onset; long T½; lower recurrence; fewer side effects; menstrual migraine
🧠 Triptan Prescribing Pearls:
  • Take early: Efficacy highest when taken at pain onset (mild phase), before central sensitization develops. Once allodynia present → triptan may not work.
  • Triptan + NSAID combination: Superior to either alone — Sumatriptan 85 mg + Naproxen 500 mg (Treximet) shows better sustained pain-free rates (POZEN studies)
  • Triptan failure ≠ class failure: Try 2–3 different triptans before concluding class failure (different receptor affinities, pharmacokinetics)
  • CI to triptans: Uncontrolled hypertension, ischemic heart disease, stroke/TIA, hemiplegic migraine, basilar migraine (relative), pregnancy (Category C), severe hepatic impairment, MAO-A inhibitors (sumatriptan/rizatriptan)
  • Chest tightness (triptan-associated): Common (up to 15%); not due to coronary vasospasm in most patients (myalgia, esophageal spasm). Rule out cardiac cause if risk factors present.

Gepants — CGRP Receptor Antagonists (Acute Use)

DrugDoseIndicationEvidenceKey Advantage
Rimegepant75 mg ODTAcute migraine ± preventionSPARTAN, BHV3000-301: 2h pain-free 19% vs placebo 12%No MOH potential; dual acute + preventive dosing option (QOD for prevention); safe in CVD
Ubrogepant50–100 mgAcute migraineACHIEVE I/II: 2h pain-free ~19% vs 12%No vasoconstriction; safe with CV risk
Zavegepant10 mg nasal sprayAcute migrainePhase 3: Rapid onset (15 min)First nasal gepant; fastest gepant onset

Gepant 2h pain-free rates (~19%) lower than triptans (~29–38%) but useful when triptans contraindicated or overused. No serotonin syndrome risk. No rebound headache risk.

IV Rescue Therapy — Emergency Department / Inpatient Protocol

Indication: Status migrainosus (>72h), severe migraine unresponsive to oral therapy, migraine with vomiting/dehydration, hyperemesis preventing oral medications, rebound after triptan failure.

DrugDoseRouteEvidence LevelNotes
Metoclopramide10–20 mg IV over 15 minIVLevel AAnti-dopaminergic; antiemetic + analgesic; watch for akathisia (treat with diphenhydramine 25 mg IV)
Prochlorperazine10 mg IVIVLevel AMore sedating; akathisia risk; superior to metoclopramide in some trials
Chlorpromazine0.1 mg/kg IV (max 25 mg)IV slowLevel AOrthostatic hypotension — prehydrate with 500 mL NS; QTc monitoring
Droperidol2.5 mg IVIVLevel AQTc prolongation; highly effective; ECG monitoring required
Ketorolac30 mg IV / 60 mg IMIV or IMLevel ANSAID; useful when dopamine antagonists CI or failed; avoid in renal impairment
Valproate sodium500–1000 mg in 100 mL NS over 30 minIVLevel BEffective; avoid in women of childbearing age (teratogen); hepatotoxicity monitor
Magnesium sulfate1–2 g in 100 mL NS over 15–30 minIVLevel BMost evidence in MwA; blocks NMDA receptors; safe in pregnancy (up to 48h use); flush sensation common
Dexamethasone8–10 mg IV (single dose)IVLevel BReduces headache recurrence at 24–72h; does NOT treat index headache acutely; use as add-on
Dihydroergotamine (DHE)0.5–1 mg IV over 3 min; may repeat q8h × 3 dosesIV (with antiemetic pre-treatment)Level AHighly effective for status migrainosus; pre-treat with metoclopramide 10 mg; CI: recent triptan use (24h), cardiovascular disease, pregnancy
Sumatriptan SC6 mg SCSCLevel AIf no previous triptan; fastest-onset triptan
Lasmiditan100–200 mg oralOralLevel A5-HT1F agonist (dittan); no vasoconstriction; CNS dizziness/sedation; do not drive 8h after
🧠 IV Protocol for Status Migrainosus (Siriraj Protocol):
  1. IV fluid hydration (NS 500–1000 mL)
  2. Metoclopramide 10 mg IV slowly (antiemetic first)
  3. Wait 15 min → if partial response: add Ketorolac 30 mg IV
  4. If no response: Valproate 500 mg IV over 30 min OR Droperidol 2.5 mg IV
  5. Dexamethasone 8 mg IV to prevent recurrence (add-on at any point)
  6. If all fail: DHE IV protocol with antiemetic pre-treatment
  7. Consider admission if >72h, dehydrated, or uncontrolled pain
Discharge: Limit opioids (MOH risk, not first-line per guidelines). If discharged with headache: naproxen 500 mg + methylprednisolone 32 mg to take at home.

Greater Occipital Nerve (GON) Block

Indication: Acute migraine rescue (ED or office), cluster headache prophylaxis, frequent migraine requiring rapid bridge to preventives, MOH withdrawal.

Technique: Patient prone or sitting. Landmark: 1/3 distance from occipital protuberance to mastoid tip (along superior nuchal line). Inject 2–3 mL of 0.5% bupivacaine + 20 mg methylprednisolone. Bilateral injection for bilateral headache. Effect within 15–30 min; may last 4–8 weeks.

Evidence: RCT evidence for acute migraine and cluster headache. Multiple small RCTs show superior to placebo for headache days. Duration of benefit varies (1–4 weeks).

Q: A 38-year-old woman with known migraine presents to the ED with severe throbbing headache 9/10, vomiting ×4, lasted 80 hours. She used sumatriptan 100 mg ×2 at home without relief. What is the diagnosis and what IV treatment do you initiate?
Diagnosis: Status Migrainosus (ICHD-3 1.4.1) — migraine attack lasting >72 hours (80h), with characteristic features and triptan failure (triptan-refractory at home). May also have component of dehydration (vomiting ×4).

IV Treatment Protocol:
  1. Hydration: NS 500–1000 mL IV (dehydration from vomiting)
  2. Antiemetic first: Metoclopramide 10 mg IV over 15 min → reduces both vomiting and headache (dopamine antagonist antiemetic + analgesic)
  3. NSAID add-on: Ketorolac 30 mg IV (different mechanism; additive to dopamine antagonist)
  4. Recurrence prevention: Dexamethasone 8 mg IV × 1 dose (reduces headache recurrence at 24–72h)
  5. If no response to above: DHE 0.5–1 mg IV (after pre-treatment with antiemetic) — highly effective for status; or IV valproate 500 mg over 30 min
Avoid: Opioids (↑MOH risk, not superior to dopamine antagonists, addictive potential). Another triptan (already failed ×2; also CI within 24h of DHE).
Before discharge: Discuss preventive therapy (MIDAS Grade IV if frequent attacks); bridge with naproxen; follow-up neurology.
4.6 Preventive Treatment — Oral Agents, BoNT-A, CGRP mAbs
INDICATIONS FOR PREVENTIVE THERAPY

Initiate prevention when ANY of the following present:

  • ≥4 migraine days/month with significant disability (MIDAS ≥11, Grade III–IV)
  • ≥8 headache days/month (risk of chronification)
  • Frequency: ≥3 attacks/month that significantly impair function despite optimal acute treatment
  • Acute medication overuse or contraindication to acute drugs
  • Chronic migraine (≥15 days/month)
  • Rare but devastating subtypes: Hemiplegic migraine, migraine with prolonged aura, basilar migraine
  • Patient preference (even with lower frequency if attacks are debilitating)

Goal: ≥50% reduction in migraine days/month. Must trial ≥3 months before declaring failure (adequate dose, adequate duration).

Oral Preventive Agents — Evidence Table

DrugClassStarting DoseTarget DoseEvidenceKey Side Effects / Notes
TopiramateAnticonvulsant25 mg nightly50–100 mg/day (divided)Level A — AAN 2012; effective in CM/MOHCognitive slowing ("dopamax"), weight loss, paresthesias, kidney stones, glaucoma. Teratogen (cleft lip/palate). Monitor carbonic anhydrase. Target 100 mg (50 mg bid)
Valproate / DivalproexAnticonvulsant250 mg bid500–1500 mg/dayLevel A — AAN 2012Teratogen (NTD) — ABSOLUTE CI in women of childbearing age without effective contraception; weight gain, tremor, alopecia, hepatotoxicity, pancreatitis; LFT + CBC at baseline

🧠 Preventive Drug Selection — Comorbidity-Guided (Match Treatment to Patient Profile):
ComorbidityPreferred AgentAvoid
HypertensionPropranolol, metoprolol, candesartan, lisinoprilAmitriptyline (if severe HTN)
Depression/AnxietyAmitriptyline, venlafaxineTopiramate (cognitive SE), beta-blockers (depression)
EpilepsyValproate, topiramate, lamotrigineBeta-blockers (seizure threshold effect)
ObesityTopiramate (weight loss), zonisamideAmitriptyline, valproate, flunarizine (weight gain)
Asthma/COPDTopiramate, valproate, amitriptyline, candesartanBeta-blockers (bronchospasm)

Raynaud's / Cold extremitiesAmitriptyline, topiramate, candesartanBeta-blockers (vasoconstriction)
InsomniaAmitriptyline (given at night)Topiramate (can disturb sleep)

OnabotulinumtoxinA (BoNT-A) — PREEMPT Protocol

PREEMPT PROTOCOL — FDA/TGA APPROVED FOR CHRONIC MIGRAINE

Indication: Chronic migraine (≥15 days/month, ≥8 migraine features) — ≥3 months duration; failed ≥2 oral preventives; also effective in CM with MOH

Total dose: 155–195 units per treatment session (31–39 injection sites)

Injection sites (31 fixed + 8 flexible "follow the pain"):

Muscle GroupUnitsSites
Frontalis20 U (10U/side)4 sites (2/side)
Corrugator supercilii10 U (5U/side)2 sites (1/side)
Procerus5 U1 site (midline)
Occipitalis30 U (15U/side)6 sites (3/side)
Temporalis40 U (20U/side)8 sites (4/side)
Trapezius30 U (15U/side)6 sites (3/side)
Cervical paraspinal20 U (10U/side)4 sites (2/side)
Flexible additional sitesup to 40 Uup to 8 sites

Frequency: Every 12 weeks (3 months). Effect onset: 2–4 weeks. Full effect by session 2–3.

Key trials: PREEMPT 1 and PREEMPT 2 — ~56% reduction in headache days vs placebo (29%) at 24 weeks; ≥50% responder rate ~47% vs 35% placebo

SE: Neck pain, injection site pain, brow ptosis (avoid <1 cm above brow), head/neck weakness (temporary). Rare: dysphagia (if trapezius injected too deep)

CGRP Monoclonal Antibodies — Comparative Table

DrugTargetDose / Route / FrequencyKey Phase III TrialEfficacy (≥50% responder)Notes
Erenumab (Aimovig)CGRP receptor70 mg or 140 mg SC monthlyARISE, STRIVE~43% (EM), ~40% (CM) at 140 mgFirst approved CGRP mAb; constipation notable AE (unique to receptor blockade); auto-injector; some patients may need dose escalation to 140 mg
Galcanezumab (Emgality)CGRP ligand120 mg SC monthly (240 mg loading × 1)EVOLVE-1, EVOLVE-2 (EM); REGAIN (CM)~61% (EM) at 3 monthsAlso approved for episodic cluster headache (300 mg SC monthly × 3 doses); loading dose may give faster onset; injection site reactions
Fremanezumab (Ajovy)CGRP ligand225 mg SC monthly OR 675 mg SC quarterlyHALO EM, HALO CM~44% (EM), ~41% (CM)Unique quarterly dosing option (convenience); effective in MOH subgroup; auto-injector
Eptinezumab (Vyepti)CGRP ligand100 mg IV q3 months (may increase to 300 mg)PROMISE-1 (EM); PROMISE-2 (CM)~50% (EM 100 mg), ~57% (EM 300 mg)IV infusion only (30 min); fastest onset (within 24h of first infusion); useful when guaranteed adherence needed; superior efficacy at higher dose
🧠 CGRP mAb Clinical Pearls:
  • Safety: No liver toxicity, no CNS side effects, no teratogenicity data (avoid in pregnancy — limited data). Avoid in: active cardiovascular disease (CGRP is vasodilatory; uncertain CV implications), pregnancy, known hypersensitivity.
  • Constipation (erenumab): Dose-dependent; CGRP receptor blockade in gut. Manage with dietary fiber, osmotic laxatives. May switch to ligand-targeting mAb if intolerable.
  • Responder rate expectations: ~50% of patients achieve ≥50% reduction in migraine days (super-responders: ≥75% reduction). Assess after 3 months (3 injections). Give up to 6 months before declaring failure.
  • CGRP mAbs vs BoNT-A: No head-to-head RCTs. Both approved for CM. BoNT-A requires in-office procedure every 12 weeks; mAbs self-injected. May combine BoNT-A + mAb in refractory CM (off-label combination used in practice).
  • Rimegepant as preventive: 75 mg ODT every other day — approved as preventive (QOD dosing) in patients who also use it acutely; useful for low-frequency migraine prevention or those refusing injections.
  • Insurance/Access (Thailand): CGRP mAbs not yet in NHSO formulary (as of 2025); significant cost barrier; compassionate access programs available for some.
