Standard preventives if >3 menstrual cycles/year affected.Pure menstrual migraine (ICHD-3 1.4.1.1): attack exclusively D−2 to D+3; 60% are MwoA. Perimenstrual estrogen drop = trigger. Transdermal estradiol patch (100 μg) during perimenstrual period (avoid oral estrogen — thrombosis risk).
Q: A 35-year-old woman with chronic migraine (18 days/month, 10 migraine features) failed topiramate 100 mg/day × 4 months and amitriptyline 50 mg × 3 months. She has no cardiovascular disease. What are the next management options and evidence base?
Assessment: Failed 2 adequate oral preventive trials (≥3 months each at therapeutic dose). Meets criteria for escalated prevention.

Options with evidence:
  1. OnabotulinumtoxinA (BoNT-A) PREEMPT: FDA-approved for CM; 155–195 U every 12 weeks. PREEMPT 1 & 2: significant reduction in headache days. First choice if insurance covers / available.
  2. CGRP monoclonal antibody:
    • Erenumab 70–140 mg SC monthly (STRIVE: 50% responder rate 40%)
    • Fremanezumab 225 mg monthly or 675 mg quarterly (HALO CM)
    • Galcanezumab 120 mg monthly (REGAIN)
    • Eptinezumab 100–300 mg IV q3 months (PROMISE-2: fastest onset)
  3. Combination: If one class fails, may try BoNT-A + CGRP mAb (off-label; real-world evidence positive). Consider whether MOH also present → treat simultaneously.
  4. Valproate: Level A evidence — but check reproductive status (absolute CI if pregnancy possible without effective contraception)
Additional: Assess and address MOH if acute medication ≥10–15 days/month; multidisciplinary approach (CBT for migraine, physiotherapy, sleep hygiene, trigger management).
4.7 Tension-Type Headache (TTH)
ICHD-3 TTH DIAGNOSTIC CRITERIA (2.1–2.3)

A. ≥10 episodes on <15 days/month (episodic) or ≥15 days/month >3 months (chronic TTH 2.3)

B. Lasting 30 minutes to 7 days

C. ≥2 of 4 characteristics (mnemonic: BEND):

  • Bilateral location
  • Equals pressing/tightening quality (non-pulsating)
  • Not aggravated by routine physical activity
  • Dull/mild-to-moderate severity

D. BOTH of:

  • No nausea or vomiting
  • No more than one of: photophobia OR phonophobia (may have one but NOT both)

E. Not better accounted for by another ICHD-3 diagnosis

Key differentiator from migraine: TTH = bilateral + non-pulsating + not aggravated by activity + no nausea/vomiting + only one of photo/phono. Migraine = opposite pattern.

TTH Subtypes

SubtypeFrequencyICHD-3 CodeClinical Note
Infrequent episodic TTH<1 day/month (<12 days/year)2.1Usually no preventive needed; OTC as needed
Frequent episodic TTH1–14 days/month for ≥3 months2.2Consider prevention if disability; pericranial tenderness key finding
Chronic TTH≥15 days/month >3 months2.3Often comorbid with depression/anxiety; MOH common; amitriptyline first-line

TTH Pathophysiology

Peripheral mechanism (episodic TTH): activation and sensitization of pericranial myofascial nociceptors → sensitization of spinal dorsal horn neurons. Pericranial tenderness on manual palpation (Total Tenderness Score) correlates with frequency. Central mechanism (chronic TTH): central sensitization of trigeminal nucleus caudalis + reduced pain inhibition (decreased serotonin, endorphins).

TTH Treatment — Abortive

DrugDoseEvidence LevelNotes
Aspirin500–1000 mgLevel AMost evidence; GI side effects
Ibuprofen400–800 mgLevel AFastest onset of NSAIDs; superior to paracetamol in some studies
Naproxen375–550 mgLevel ALonger duration; useful for prolonged TTH episodes
Paracetamol1000 mgLevel ASafe; useful when NSAIDs CI; may be less effective than NSAIDs
Caffeine + analgesic65 mg caffeine + NSAIDLevel ACombination superior to analgesic alone; limit caffeine (withdrawal headache)
Triptans—Not effective for TTHTriptans work in migraine but NOT TTH — use to differentiate if uncertain diagnosis

TTH Prophylaxis

DrugDoseEvidence Level (EAN/EFNS)
Amitriptyline30–75 mg nightly (start 10 mg)Level A — most evidence for chronic TTH
Mirtazapine15–30 mg nightlyLevel B
Venlafaxine75–150 mg/dayLevel B
Clomipramine75–150 mg/dayLevel B
Maprotiline75 mg/dayLevel B
Mianserin30–60 mg/dayLevel B

Non-pharmacological for TTH (high evidence): Biofeedback (EMG), relaxation therapy, cognitive-behavioral therapy, physiotherapy (cervical manipulation, trigger point therapy). Often superior long-term to pharmacotherapy alone for chronic TTH.

4.8 Trigeminal Autonomic Cephalalgias — Cluster, PH, SUNCT/SUNA, Hemicrania Continua
TAC OVERVIEW — KEY DIFFERENTIATORS
FeatureCluster HeadacheParoxysmal Hemicrania (PH)SUNCT / SUNAHemicrania Continua (HC)
Duration/attack15–180 min2–30 min1–600 sec (SUNCT); 1 sec–10 min (SUNA)Continuous (background) + exacerbations
Frequency1–8/day (in bouts)≥5/day (>50% time)≥1/day (>50% time); up to 200/dayContinuous (no pain-free periods)
Autonomic featuresYes (ipsilateral)Yes (ipsilateral)Yes (ipsilateral); conjunctival injection + lacrimation prominentYes (ipsilateral, milder)
Sex predominanceM:F = 3:1F:M = 2.5:1M:F = 1.5:1F:M = 2:1
Indomethacin responseNoYES — absoluteNoYES — absolute
Key treatmentO2 + sumatriptan SC; verapamil preventiveIndomethacin (diagnostic + therapeutic)Lamotrigine; IV lidocaineIndomethacin (diagnostic + therapeutic)
ICHD-3 Code3.13.23.3/3.43.4

Cluster Headache — ICHD-3 3.1

CLUSTER HEADACHE DIAGNOSTIC CRITERIA

A. At least 5 attacks fulfilling B–D

B. Severe/very severe unilateral orbital, supraorbital, and/or temporal pain lasting 15–180 min (untreated)

C. Either or both:

  • ≥1 ipsilateral cranial autonomic symptom: conjunctival injection, lacrimation, nasal congestion, rhinorrhea, eyelid edema, facial sweating/flushing, miosis/ptosis (partial Horner), ear fullness
  • Sense of restlessness or agitation

D. Attacks have circadian periodicity (same time daily, nocturnal peak 1–2 AM) and/or circannual pattern (bout same season yearly)

E. Episodic CH (3.1.1): clusters of 7 days–1 year separated by ≥3-month pain-free periods. Chronic CH (3.1.2): no remission or remission <3 months for >1 year.

Cluster Headache Pathophysiology

Hypothalamic activation (posterior hypothalamus — suprachiasmatic nucleus dysfunction → circadian pattern). Trigeminovascular activation → pain (V1 distribution). Parasympathetic activation via sphenopalatine ganglion → autonomic features (lacrimation, rhinorrhea, conjunctival injection). Sympathetic deficiency → partial Horner (ptosis + miosis but NOT anhidrosis — only superior cervical ganglion sympathetics affected). Unique feature: patient paces, cannot lie still (contrast with migraine → lies in dark quiet room).

Cluster Headache Treatment

PhaseDrugDoseEvidenceNotes
Acute AttackOxygen (100%)12–15 L/min via non-rebreather mask × 15–20 minLevel A — AANMost effective, safest; must use non-rebreather mask (not nasal cannula); response within 15 min; no systemic SE
Sumatriptan SC6 mg SCLevel AFastest pharmacologic option; limit 2 injections/24h; high responder rate (~74%)
Sumatriptan nasal / Zolmitriptan nasalSuma nasal 20 mg; Zolmi nasal 5 mgLevel AAlternative if SC injection not tolerated; slower than SC
Preventive (episodic bout)Verapamil240–960 mg/day (divided tid)Level A — First-line preventiveCardiac monitoring: ECG baseline + q2 weeks with dose increase (PR prolongation, AV block); titrate up by 80 mg/week; 360–480 mg/day usually effective target
Lithium600–1200 mg/day (levels 0.4–0.8 mEq/L)Level B — especially chronic CHMonitor levels, renal, thyroid; narrow therapeutic window; interactions with NSAIDs, diuretics
Topiramate100–200 mg/dayLevel BAlternative when verapamil CI/ineffective
Galcanezumab300 mg SC monthly × 3 monthsLevel A (CONQUER trial)FDA-approved for episodic CH; only CGRP mAb with CH indication; given monthly × 3 doses in bout
Prednisone bridge60–100 mg/day × 5 days then taperLevel BTransitional prevention while verapamil takes effect (2–3 weeks); GON block (methylprednisolone) alternative bridge
🧠 Cluster Headache vs. Migraine — Key Clinical Differences:
  • Patient behavior: CH patient paces, rocks, cannot stay still (agitation); migraine patient lies still in dark quiet room (photophobia → movement worsens)
  • Timing: CH attacks like clockwork — same time daily (circadian), often 1–3 AM (nocturnal hypothalamic activation); migraine irregular timing
  • Duration: CH = 15–180 min (usually peaks in 30–45 min); migraine = 4–72h
  • Autonomic features: CH = prominent ipsilateral Horner, lacrimation, rhinorrhea; migraine = no autonomic features (or bilateral photophobia/phonophobia)
  • Oxygen therapy: Highly effective in CH (NNT ~2); NOT effective in migraine
  • Verapamil monitoring: HIGH-DOSE verapamil (up to 960 mg/day) for CH — ECG mandatory at each dose increment; heart block and PR prolongation real risks

Paroxysmal Hemicrania (PH) — ICHD-3 3.2

Diagnostic criteria: ≥20 attacks of severe unilateral orbital/supraorbital/temporal pain lasting 2–30 min + ipsilateral autonomic features (same as CH criteria C) + frequency >5/day for >50% time + absolute response to indomethacin.

Indomethacin test (diagnostic + therapeutic): 25 mg tid × 3 days; if partial response → 50 mg tid × 3 days; if partial → 75 mg tid × 3 days. Complete response = PH confirmed. Maintenance: lowest effective dose (often 25–50 mg tid); use with PPI (GI protection).

SUNCT / SUNA — ICHD-3 3.3/3.4

SUNCT (Short-lasting Unilateral Neuralgiform headache with Conjunctival injection and Tearing): attacks 1–600 sec, conjunctival injection + lacrimation prominent, frequency up to 200/day. SUNA (Short-lasting Unilateral Neuralgiform headache with cranial Autonomic symptoms): same but only one or no conjunctival/lacrimation feature.

Treatment: No acute treatment effective (attacks too short). Prevention: Lamotrigine 25–300 mg/day (Level A); Topiramate 50–300 mg/day; IV Lidocaine (acute hospital prevention in refractory); Gabapentin, carbamazepine (variable response). Important: must rule out secondary cause (posterior fossa lesion — MRI pituitary, posterior fossa mandatory).

Hemicrania Continua (HC) — ICHD-3 3.4

Criteria: Unilateral headache for >3 months (continuous, no pain-free periods) + moderate baseline + exacerbations of severe pain + ipsilateral autonomic features during exacerbations + absolute indomethacin response. Treatment: Same as PH — indomethacin is diagnostic + therapeutic (absolute response required for diagnosis). Celecoxib alternative if indomethacin not tolerated. Secondary HC-like: pituitary adenoma, cavernous sinus lesion — MRI essential.

Q: A 45-year-old man presents with 3-week history of daily severe right orbital pain, lasting 45 minutes, occurring at 11 PM nightly. Associated with right eye tearing and ptosis. He paces around during attacks. Temp/BP/neuro exam normal. What is the diagnosis and immediate management?
Diagnosis: Episodic Cluster Headache (ICHD-3 3.1.1)
Criteria met: Severe unilateral orbital pain ✓; 45 min duration (15–180 min) ✓; ipsilateral autonomic (lacrimation + ptosis/miosis = partial Horner) ✓; restlessness/agitation (pacing) ✓; circadian (11 PM nightly) ✓; 3-week bout duration ✓

Workup: MRI brain with pituitary protocol (exclude secondary CH — cavernous sinus, pituitary, orbital lesion) particularly given this is first presentation. ECG before starting verapamil.

Immediate Management:
  1. Acute attacks: Oxygen 100% 12–15 L/min via non-rebreather mask × 15–20 min (Level A; most effective first-line); prescribe home oxygen tank. PLUS/OR Sumatriptan SC 6 mg auto-injector (Level A; limit 2/day).
  2. Transitional prevention (bridge): Prednisone 60 mg × 5 days then taper over 2 weeks — rapid relief while verapamil takes effect. OR GON block (methylprednisolone ipsilateral).
  3. Preventive (maintenance): Verapamil 240 mg/day (80 mg tid) → titrate to 360–480 mg/day. ECG monitoring before each dose increase (q2 weeks). Continue through bout duration (typically 6–12 weeks then taper).
Avoid: Oral triptans/sumatriptan nasal (slower onset; less effective than SC for CH). Opioids (not indicated; MOH risk; ineffective).
4.9 Trigeminal Neuralgia & Cranial Neuralgias
TRIGEMINAL NEURALGIA — ICHD-3 13.1 / Classic TN

A. Recurrent paroxysms of unilateral facial pain in the distribution(s) of one or more divisions of the trigeminal nerve with no radiation beyond, fulfilling B and C

B. Pain has all of the following characteristics:

  • Lasting from a fraction of a second to 2 minutes
  • Severe intensity
  • Electric shock-like, shooting, stabbing, or sharp in quality

C. Precipitated by innocuous stimuli within the affected trigeminal distribution (trigger: touch face, eating, talking, cold air, brushing teeth)

D. Not better accounted for by another ICHD-3 diagnosis

Distribution: V2 (maxillary) most common (37%), V3 (mandibular) 34%, V2+V3 combined 29%, V1 (ophthalmic) rare (<5%). Right side more common (60%). Bilateral <3% (rule out MS).

TN Classification

TypeICHD-3CauseMRI FindingImplication
Classic TN13.1.1Neurovascular compression (superior cerebellar artery most common)MRI FIESTA/CISS: contact between vessel and trigeminal nerve root entry zone (REZ)Best surgical candidate (MVD highly effective)
Secondary TN13.1.2Structural lesion (MS plaque, tumor, AVM, meningioma)MRI shows causative lesionTreat underlying cause; if MS → carbamazepine still first-line medical
Idiopathic TN13.1.3No vascular compression or structural lesionNormal MRIMedical treatment; may have partial surgical response

TN Treatment Algorithm

TreatmentDoseEvidenceNotes
Medical (First-Line)
Carbamazepine100 mg bid → 400–800 mg bid (target 200–400 mg tid)Level A — most evidence; NNT ~2Sodium channel blocker; reduce pain paroxysms in 70–80%. Monitor CBC + LFT + Na (hyponatremia). HLA-B*1502 screening before use in Asian patients (risk SJS/TEN). Drug interactions (CYP3A4 inducer). Titrate slowly: 100 mg bid → increase weekly. Therapeutic level: 4–12 μg/mL.
Oxcarbazepine150 mg bid → 600–900 mg bidLevel B — fewer drug interactions, better tolerated than CBZBetter tolerability than CBZ. Monitor Na (SIADH risk). Same HLA-B*1502 concern. Pro-drug of CBZ active metabolite. Preferred in elderly, polypharmacy.
Lamotrigine25 mg → 200–400 mg/day (very slow titration)Level C — add-onAdd-on to CBZ/OXC; Stevens-Johnson risk if titrated too fast (must titrate slowly over 6–8 weeks)
Baclofen10–80 mg/day (divided)Level C — add-onGABA-B agonist; add-on or if CBZ/OXC CI/failed; withdrawal seizures if stopped abruptly
Gabapentin900–3600 mg/dayLevel C — less evidence than CBZUseful add-on; better in TN with continuous background pain component
Surgical (When Medical Fails)
Microvascular Decompression (MVD)—Level A — most durablePosterior fossa craniotomy; Teflon pad between vessel and nerve. Best outcomes (80–90% pain-free immediately; 70% at 10 years). Indicated for classic TN with NVC on MRI. Not for elderly high surgical risk.
Gamma Knife Radiosurgery—Level AFocused radiation to REZ; no general anesthesia needed; response delayed 1–6 months; useful in elderly/high surgical risk; may cause numbness
Percutaneous rhizotomy (balloon, glycerol, RF)—Level BImmediate effect; high recurrence rate (<MVD); numbness/corneal anesthesia risk
🧠 TN vs. Other Facial Pain:
ConditionDurationTriggerKey Feature
Trigeminal NeuralgiaSec (fraction to 2 min)Light touchElectric shock; paroxysmal; no autonomic features
SUNCT/SUNA1 sec–10 minTouch (often orbital)Autonomic features (conjunctival injection); high frequency
Dental painContinuousCold, bitingThrobbing; localized to tooth; dental exam abnormal

Atypical facial painHours–continuousPsychological stressCrossing dermatomal lines; psychiatric comorbidity

Post-Herpetic Neuralgia (PHN)

Definition: Pain persisting >3 months after acute herpes zoster rash in same dermatomal distribution. Risk: elderly, immunocompromised, ophthalmic zoster (HZO — V1), inadequately treated acute zoster.

Prevention: Early antiviral treatment within 72h of rash onset (acyclovir/valacyclovir) reduces PHN risk. Shingrix vaccine (adjuvanted recombinant) >90% effective prevention of HZ and PHN in ≥50 years.

Treatment:

DrugDoseEvidence
Gabapentin300 mg tid → 900–3600 mg/dayLevel A; first-line
Pregabalin75 mg bid → 150–300 mg bidLevel A; faster titration, better adherence
Tricyclic antidepressants (amitriptyline, nortriptyline)10–75 mg nightlyLevel A; NNT ~3
Duloxetine / Venlafaxine30–60 mg/day / 75–150 mg/dayLevel B
Topical lidocaine patch (5%)Apply to affected area ×12h/dayLevel A; minimal systemic absorption
Topical capsaicin patch (8%)Apply 60 min by specialistLevel A; single application lasts 3 months
Opioids (tramadol, oxycodone)VariableLevel A; use as 3rd-line (risk: dependence, MOH)
4.10 Secondary Headache — Red Flags, SNOOP4, Neuroimaging Criteria
SNOOP4 RED FLAGS — INDICATIONS FOR URGENT NEUROIMAGING
LetterRed FlagDifferential Diagnosis
SSystemic symptoms or signs: fever, weight loss, jaw claudication, scalp tendernessMeningitis, encephalitis, GCA (ESR, CRP, temporal artery biopsy), systemic malignancy
SSecondary risk factors: HIV/immunocompromised, malignancy, pregnancy, anticoagulation, OSA (ICH risk)CNS opportunistic infection (toxo, crypto), brain metastases, cerebral venous thrombosis
NNeurological symptoms or signs: focal deficits, altered consciousness, papilledema, meningismusSOL, ICH, meningitis, SAH, CVST, hypertensive encephalopathy, carotid/vertebral dissection
OOnset sudden / thunderclap (≤1 min to maximal intensity)SAH (MUST exclude with CT/LP), CVST, RCVS, vertebral dissection, pituitary apoplexy
OOlder age: new headache onset >50 years (especially >65)GCA, SOL (neoplasm, SDH), cerebrovascular disease
PProgressive headache: new/changing headache worsening over weeksSOL (primary/metastatic brain tumor), chronic SDH, IIH, abscess
PPositional: worse lying flat (↑ ICP) or upright (CSF leak)IIH (worse lying flat at night, improves upright), CSF leak (orthostatic headache worse upright)
PPrecipitated by Valsalva (cough, sneeze, straining) or exertionArnold-Chiari malformation, posterior fossa tumor, cardiac cause, Chiari I
PPapilledema on fundoscopyIIH, SOL with raised ICP, CVST, malignant hypertension — urgent LP/MRI/ophthalmology

Thunderclap Headache — Differential Diagnosis Algorithm

Definition: Reaches maximal severity within 1 minute; most severe headache of life. ALWAYS a red flag until proven otherwise.

PriorityDiagnosisWorkupKey Feature
1 (MUST EXCLUDE)Subarachnoid Hemorrhage (SAH)CT head non-contrast first; if negative → LP (xanthochromia 12h–2 weeks; RBC count); CTA if CT negative + LP positive/equivocal → detect aneurysm"Worst headache of life"; meningismus; may have brief LOC; sentinel headache in 20%
2Cerebral Venous Sinus Thrombosis (CVST)MRI + MRV brainSubacute onset; focal deficits; seizures; OCP/pregnancy/hypercoagulable state risk
3Reversible Cerebral Vasoconstriction Syndrome (RCVS)CTA/MRA (beaded arteries); MRI FLAIR (cortical SAH)Recurrent thunderclap headaches over days–weeks; young women; drug trigger (sympathomimetics, SSRIs, triptans overuse); RCVS resolves in 1–3 months
4Vertebral/Carotid Artery DissectionMRI + MRA neck (fat-sat sequences)Neck pain ± Horner; posterior circulation sx (dissection); pulsatile tinnitus (carotid); often traumatic/chiropractic trigger
5Pituitary ApoplexyMRI pituitary (hemorrhage/infarction into adenoma)Bitemporal hemianopia; sudden onset; known pituitary adenoma; III/VI nerve palsy; steroids urgently if adrenal crisis
6Hypertensive Emergency/PRESBP >180/120 + MRI FLAIR (posterior white matter edema)Seizures; visual disturbance; BP crisis
7Primary Thunderclap Headache (benign)Diagnosis of exclusion — all secondary causes excludedSingle episode; no recurrence; normal CT/LP/MRA; no trigger
🧠 Ottawa SAH Rule (for CT-negative thunderclap headache): If CT negative and any of these present → LP mandatory:
  • Age ≥40
  • Neck pain or stiffness
  • Witnessed LOC
  • Onset with exertion
  • Thunderclap headache (maximal at onset)
  • Limited neck flexion on exam
CT sensitivity for SAH: 98% within 6h of onset, decreasing to 85–90% at 24h. LP remains necessary in appropriate clinical context even if CT negative. CTA can detect aneurysm ≥3 mm (sensitivity ~98%) and may replace conventional angiography in many centers.

Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension (IIH) — Pseudotumor Cerebri

IIH MODIFIED DANDY CRITERIA

A. Symptoms/signs of ↑ICP: headache (worsened lying, improves upright), pulsatile tinnitus, visual obscurations, diplopia (VI palsy)

B. Papilledema (not always present — "IIH without papilledema" recognized)

C. Normal neurological exam except VI nerve palsy

D. Normal neuroimaging (MRI: empty sella, flattening of posterior sclera, distension of optic nerve sheath, tortuosity of optic nerve — all indirect signs)

E. LP opening pressure >25 cmH₂O (in non-obese adults); CSF composition normal

F. No other cause identified

Risk factors: Obese women of childbearing age (BMI >30), tetracycline, vitamin A toxicity, steroids withdrawal, contraceptives

IIH Treatment: Weight loss (primary — even 5–10% reduces ICP significantly); Acetazolamide 250 mg–4 g/day (carbonic anhydrase inhibitor; reduces CSF production); serial LP (temporal relief); optic nerve sheath fenestration (if vision threatened); VP shunt or lumboperitoneal shunt (refractory); topiramate (carbonic anhydrase + weight loss effect).

Monitoring: Regular visual field testing (Goldmann/automated perimetry) — vision loss is the feared complication.

Giant Cell Arteritis (GCA) — Headache in Elderly

Must consider in ALL new-onset headache >50 years. Granulomatous vasculitis of medium/large vessels (temporal artery, ophthalmic artery). Features: unilateral temple headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication (pathognomonic), diplopia, amaurosis fugax (optic artery ischemia), ESR >50 mm/h, CRP elevated, temporal artery biopsy (skip lesions — take long segment ≥2 cm). Treatment BEFORE biopsy: Prednisolone 1 mg/kg/day (up to 60 mg) if high suspicion — do not wait for biopsy; start steroids immediately to prevent permanent vision loss (biopsy remains positive for 2 weeks after steroid start).

Q: A 55-year-old obese woman (BMI 32) presents with 3-month history of daily headache worse in the morning and at night, pulsatile tinnitus, and two episodes of brief visual blurring. Fundoscopy shows bilateral papilledema. MRI brain is normal. What is the diagnosis, and what is the management?
Diagnosis: Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension (IIH) — Modified Dandy criteria met: symptoms of ↑ICP (morning headache, pulsatile tinnitus, visual obscurations) + papilledema + normal MRI + obese woman of reproductive age (classic demographic).

Urgent next step: LP — measure opening pressure (>25 cmH₂O confirms) + CSF analysis (normal composition). Also: MRI/MRV to exclude CVST (secondary cause of raised ICP).

Management:
  1. Weight loss: Primary treatment — 5–10% weight reduction significantly lowers ICP; dietary counseling; referral to bariatric program if BMI ≥35
  2. Acetazolamide: 250 mg bid → titrate to 1–4 g/day as tolerated. Evidence: IIHTT trial — improved visual field + perimetry + reduced LP pressure. SE: paresthesias (common), GI upset, hyponatremia, teratogenicity (avoid in pregnancy)
  3. Serial LP: Symptomatic relief; not primary treatment (recurrence immediate)
  4. Ophthalmology urgent: Visual field testing (Goldmann perimetry) — monitor for vision loss (optic atrophy). If rapidly declining vision → surgical intervention (optic nerve sheath fenestration or CSF shunt)
  5. Avoid precipitants: Tetracyclines, vitamin A supplements, high-dose steroids withdrawal

5. Neuromuscular Disorders

Neuromuscular disorders encompass diseases of the motor neuron, peripheral nerve, neuromuscular junction (NMJ), and muscle. This section covers MG (with Thai guideline), GBS/CIDP, ALS, and inflammatory myopathies at subspecialist depth.

5.1 Myasthenia Gravis (MG)
FeatureAChRAb MG (85%)MUSKAb MG (5-8%)
Antibody classIgG1/IgG3 (complement-fixing → MAC destruction of AChR)IgG4 (non-complement; disrupts MuSK-LRP4 → impaired AChR clustering)
Clinical patternOcular ± generalized; thymoma 10-15%Bulbar-predominant; facial/tongue atrophy; respiratory crisis more common; NO thymoma
RNS (3 Hz)Decrement ≥10% in 50-70%Variable; may need proximal/facial muscles
Tensilon/Ice testEdrophonium +80%; Ice pack test: +2mm ptosis improvementLess reliable; ice pack still valid for ptosis
ThymusHyperplasia 65%; thymoma 10-15%Normal thymus
PLEX responseGoodExcellent (preferred over IVIG)
Rituximab responseModerateExcellent — often 2nd-line for MUSKAb
💊 Thai MG Treatment Guideline (Siriraj/SNAC Protocol)
  • Step 1 — Symptomatic: Pyridostigmine 30-60 mg q6-8h → titrate to 60-120 mg q3-8h (max 480 mg/day); MUSKAb: use lower doses
  • Step 2 — Prednisolone escalation: Week 1: Pred 10 mg + AzA 50 mg → Week 2: Pred 20 mg → Week 3: Pred 30 mg → Week 4: Pred 40 mg; maintain 0.75-1 mg/kg/day × 2mo → taper 5 mg q2-4wk
  • Step 3 — Steroid-sparing: Azathioprine 2-3 mg/kg/day (TPMT testing before; CBC+LFT q4wk); Cyclosporine 3-6 mg/kg/day BID; MMF 1500-2000 mg/day; Tacrolimus 3 mg/day
  • Rescue: IVIG 2g/kg over 5d OR PLEX 5 exchanges over 10-14d; PLEX preferred for MUSKAb/crisis/pre-op
  • Refractory/3rd line: Rituximab 375 mg/m² weekly ×4 (especially MUSKAb); Cyclophosphamide 500 mg/m² monthly
Targeted TherapyTargetTrialNotes
EculizumabAnti-C5 complement → blocks MACREGAIN 2017AChRAb+ refractory generalized MG; IV q2wk; meningococcal vaccine required
RavulizumabAnti-C5 (long-acting)CHAMPION-MG 2022Non-inferior to eculizumab; q8wk dosing — more convenient
ZilucoplanAnti-C5 peptide (SQ)RAISE 2023Self-administered SQ daily; AChRAb+ generalized MG

🔑 Myasthenic Crisis Management: Intubate if FVC <15 mL/kg or NIF <−25 cmH₂O (20-30-40 rule). RSI: avoid succinylcholine; rocuronium + sugammadex. Temporarily HOLD pyridostigmine (↓ secretions). PLEX preferred (faster onset 3-5d). Identify trigger (infection #1). Drugs to avoid: aminoglycosides, fluoroquinolones, macrolides, beta-blockers, calcium channel blockers, magnesium, contrast (use with caution).
5.2 Guillain-Barré Syndrome (GBS) & CIDP
GBS VariantAntibodyNCS PatternClinical
AIDP (demyelinating; 90% Western)Anti-GQ1b (30%)↑DL, ↓CV, conduction block, absent F-wavesAscending weakness + sensory; areflexia; most common
AMAN (motor axonal; Asia common)Anti-GM1/GD1a↓CMAP; normal SNAPPure motor; post-Campylobacter; good recovery
AMSAN (motor-sensory axonal)Anti-GM1/GD1a↓CMAP + ↓SNAPSevere; slower recovery
Miller Fisher SyndromeAnti-GQ1b (90-95%)Often normalClassic triad: Ophthalmoplegia + Ataxia + Areflexia (OAA); self-limiting
💊 GBS Treatment
  • IVIg 2g/kg over 5d (preferred) OR PLEX 5 exchanges/10-14d — equivalent efficacy; NOT additive (no PLEX after IVIG)
  • Corticosteroids: NOT indicated — multiple RCTs showed no benefit; oral steroids may worsen
  • Respiratory: FVC q4-8h; intubate if FVC <15 mL/kg (20-30-40 rule); dysautonomia: continuous cardiac monitoring
  • DVT prophylaxis; pain management (gabapentin, carbamazepine); early rehabilitation
  • Poor prognosis: Age >60, Campylobacter, rapid onset, AMSAN, CMAP <20% → use EGOS (Erasmus GBS Outcome Score)
CIDP FeatureDetails
DefinitionAcquired demyelinating polyneuropathy; onset >8 weeks; relapsing or progressive; proximal + distal weakness + sensory
NCS criteria (EFNS/PNS 2021)≥1 of: ↑DL, ↓CV (<75% LLN), conduction block, temporal dispersion, absent F-waves in ≥2 nerves
VariantsDADS (distal, anti-MAG IgM); MADSAM (multifocal = Lewis-Sumner; asymmetric); sensory CIDP; pure motor
TreatmentIVIg 2g/kg loading → 1g/kg q3-4wk (PATH trial 2017); SCIG self-administered; prednisolone; PLEX; efgartigimod (ADHERE trial 2023 — FDA approved for CIDP)
5.3 ALS & Motor Neuron Disease
🔑 Gold Coast Criteria 2020: ALS diagnosis if: (1) LMN signs (clinical or EMG) + UMN signs in ≥1 body region; OR (2) LMN signs in ≥2 body regions — AND symptoms are spreading, other diagnoses excluded. Fasciculation + atrophy + preserved/brisk reflexes = ALS until proven otherwise. Replaced El Escorial/Awaji — improved sensitivity.
DrugMechanismBenefitNotes
RiluzoleAnti-glutamatergic (↓ glutamate release, Na channel block)+2-3 months survival; 50 mg BIDMonitor LFT q3mo; nausea; FDA approved 1995
EdaravoneFree radical scavenger (ROS)↓ ALSFRS-R decline 33% in selected patients (FVC ≥80%, <2yr, ALSFRS-R ≥2 all items)IV monthly cycles; oral form 2022; headache
TofersenASO silences SOD1 mRNA → ↓ SOD1 proteinVALOR trial: ↓ SOD1 protein, ↓ NfL; FDA accelerated approval 2023SOD1+ ALS only; IT injection every 28d
AMX0035 (Relyvrio)Phenylbutyrate + TUDCA (ER stress + mitochondria)CENTAUR 2020: ↓ ALSFRS-R declineWithdrawn April 2024 after confirmatory PHOENIX trial failed
🫁 ALS Symptomatic Management — Key Points
  • NIV (BiPAP): FVC <50% or overnight SaO₂ <95%; improves survival ~7 months; tracheostomy = patient/family decision
  • PEG: Insert when dysphagia worsens; safer when FVC >50%; RIG if FVC <50%
  • Pseudobulbar affect: Dextromethorphan/quinidine (Nuedexta — approved); amitriptyline; SSRIs
  • Sialorrhea: Glycopyrrolate; amitriptyline; BoNT-A to salivary glands
  • Spasticity: Baclofen; tizanidine; intrathecal baclofen
  • Multidisciplinary clinic: Improves survival and QoL — neurology, respiratory, nutrition, PT/OT/SLP, palliative
5.4 Inflammatory Myopathies
SubtypeKey FeaturesAntibodyBiopsy
Dermatomyositis (DM)Proximal weakness + heliotrope rash + Gottron's papules/sign; ↑CK; malignancy 15-20%Anti-Mi-2 (classic); anti-MDA5 (amyopathic DM + ILD — rapidly progressive); anti-TIF1γ (cancer-associated); anti-NXP2 (calcinosis)Perifascicular atrophy; perivascular inflammation; MHC-I upregulation
IMNMMarkedly ↑↑CK (5,000-50,000); severe proximal weakness; statin-associated or idiopathicAnti-SRP (severe, statin-naive); Anti-HMGCR (statin-associated — persists after stopping)Necrosis + regeneration; minimal inflammation; MHC-I+
Antisynthetase SyndromeMyositis + ILD + arthritis + mechanic's hands + Raynaud's + feverAnti-Jo-1 (most common); anti-PL-7, PL-12, EJ, OJ, KSPerimysial inflammation; perifascicular
Inclusion Body Myositis (IBM)Age >50; distal + proximal (quadriceps + finger flexors asymmetric); dysphagia; refractory to immunotherapyAnti-cN1A (50%)Rimmed vacuoles; TDP-43+ inclusions; endomysial inflammation
💊 IIM Treatment
  • 1st line: Prednisolone 1 mg/kg/day × 4-8wk → taper; start steroid-sparing EARLY: azathioprine 2-3 mg/kg/day or methotrexate 15-25 mg/week
  • ILD: MMF 2-3 g/day preferred; cyclosporine/tacrolimus for rapidly progressive ILD; cyclophosphamide for crisis
  • Refractory: IVIG 2g/kg q1-3mo (ProDERM trial 2021 — DM); rituximab (MAVI trial)
  • Malignancy screening (DM, especially anti-TIF1γ): CT chest/abdomen/pelvis + PET-CT; colonoscopy; repeat at 3yr
  • IBM: No proven immunotherapy; physiotherapy essential; bimagrumab (anti-activin receptor) — Phase 3 ongoing

📚 Key Guidelines — Neuromuscular

  • [G1] Thai MG Guideline — SNAC/Siriraj protocol (Kumutpongpanich T, 2025)
  • [G2] EFNS/PNS CIDP Guidelines 2021
  • [T1] ESETT 2019 — GBS: IVIg = PLEX; steroids NOT beneficial
  • [T2] MGTX Trial 2016 — Thymectomy + pred vs pred alone → better QMG, less azathioprine use (Wolfe GI et al., NEJM)

6. Demyelinating Diseases

This section covers multiple sclerosis (McDonald 2017 criteria, DMT ladder), NMOSD (Wingerchuk 2015 criteria, targeted biologics), MOGAD, and autoimmune encephalitis — the most rapidly evolving areas of clinical neurology.

6.1 Multiple Sclerosis — McDonald 2017 Criteria
🎯 McDonald 2017 — Dissemination in Space (DIS) & Time (DIT)
  • DIS: ≥1 T2 lesion in ≥2 of 4 CNS areas: (1) Periventricular (≥1; touching ventricle); (2) Cortical/juxtacortical; (3) Infratentorial; (4) Spinal cord
  • DIT: Any of: new T2/Gd+ lesion on follow-up MRI; simultaneous Gd+ AND non-enhancing T2 lesions; CSF-specific oligoclonal bands (NEW in 2017) — allows DIT diagnosis without repeat MRI
  • "Dawson fingers": Periventricular demyelination plaques perpendicular to corpus callosum on sagittal FLAIR — pathognomonic
MS DMT TierDrugARR ReductionKey SE / Monitoring
Platform / Moderate-efficacyIFN-β (1a/1b)~30%Flu-like injection site; ↑LFT; thyroid; neutralizing antibodies
Teriflunomide~31-36%Hair thinning; ↑LFT; teratogenic (washout with cholestyramine); HBP
Dimethyl fumarate (DMF)~49%Flushing; GI; lymphopenia (ALC q3-6mo); PML risk if ALC <500 sustained
High-efficacyNatalizumab~68%PML (JCV Ab index stratification); rebound upon withdrawal; 4-weekly IV
Ocrelizumab~46-47% RRMS; ↓CDP 24% PPMSInfusion reactions; PML (rare); HBV reactivation; hold vaccines 6mo; breast cancer signal (monitor). Only DMT with Level A for PPMS (ORATORIO trial 2016)
Alemtuzumab~49-69% vs IFNSecondary autoimmunity: thyroid (30%), ITP (1%), anti-GBM (0.3%) — monthly urinalysis + TFTs ×4yr; lymphocyte count recovery
Induction/ReconstitutionCladribine~58%Lymphopenia; herpes; teratogenic; PML risk; 2-year cycle only → durable remission
🔑 MS Symptom Management: Spasticity → baclofen, tizanidine, cannabis derivatives, intrathecal baclofen pump. Fatigue → amantadine 100 mg BID, modafinil 200 mg. Bladder (urgency) → oxybutynin, solifenacin; (retention) → ISC. Lhermitte's → carbamazepine. Walking speed → fampridine (potassium channel blocker) 10 mg BID. Acute relapse → IVMP 1g/day ×3-5d (speeds recovery; does NOT change final outcome). Serum NfL — biomarker of disease activity and treatment response.
6.2 NMOSD — Wingerchuk 2015 Criteria & Treatment
🎯 NMOSD 6 Core Clinical Characteristics
  • 1. Optic neuritis (bilateral; severe; chiasm involved)
  • 2. Acute myelitis (LETM: ≥3 vertebral segments; central T2; AQP4+ cells)
  • 3. Area postrema syndrome (intractable hiccups/nausea/vomiting >48h — pathognomonic)
  • 4. Acute brainstem syndrome
  • 5. Symptomatic narcolepsy with NMOSD-compatible diencephalic MRI lesion
  • 6. Symptomatic cerebral syndrome with NMOSD-compatible brain lesion

AQP4+ seropositive NMOSD: ≥1 core feature + positive AQP4-IgG (cell-based assay). Seronegative NMOSD: ≥2 core features + MRI criteria.

⚠️ MS DMTs that can WORSEN NMOSD: Natalizumab, fingolimod, alemtuzumab — can trigger NMOSD attacks. NEVER use without confirmed diagnosis.

DrugTargetTrialKey Notes
Eculizumab (Soliris)Anti-C5 → blocks complement MACPREVENT 2019: 94.2% risk reduction vs placeboAQP4+ NMOSD; IV q2wk; meningococcal vaccine mandatory; FDA 2019
Inebilizumab (Uplizna)Anti-CD19 → B-cell + plasmablast depletionN-MOmentum 2019: ↓ relapse 73%IV q6mo; broader CD19 vs CD20; FDA 2020; AQP4+ best response
Satralizumab (Enspryng)Anti-IL-6R recycling antibodySAkuraSky/SAkuraStar 2020: ↓ relapse 74%SQ q4wk; FDA 2020; safe in pregnancy (limited data)
RituximabAnti-CD20 → B-cell depletionMultiple observational; no RCT375 mg/m² q6mo or 1g ×2; widely used in resource-limited settings; off-label
6.3 Autoimmune Encephalitis — Antibodies & Treatment
AntibodyLocationSyndromeTumorKey Feature
Anti-NMDARSynaptic (NR1)Psychiatric prodrome → seizures → orofacial dyskinesia → autonomic instability → comaOvarian teratoma (50% women >18y)Most common AE; EEG "extreme delta brush"; 75% good outcome; treat tumor
Anti-LGI1SynapticLimbic encephalitis + faciobrachial dystonic seizures (FBDS) — brief unilateral face+arm dystonia <3s, very frequentThymoma (5-10%)FBDS precedes LE; hyponatremia (SIADH); IgG4; excellent steroid response
Anti-CASPR2SynapticLE + neuromyotonia; Morvan's syndrome (LE + neuromyotonia + insomnia + dysautonomia)Thymoma (20-30%)Older men; IgG4
Anti-GABA-BSynapticLE + prominent refractory seizures; cerebellar ataxiaSCLC (50%)Severe seizures; IgG1
Anti-Hu (ANNA-1)IntracellularParaneoplastic sensory neuropathy + LE + cerebellar ataxiaSCLC >90%T-cell mediated → poor IS response; find and treat tumor
Anti-Yo (PCA-1)IntracellularParaneoplastic cerebellar degeneration (rapid, severe, irreversible)Breast, ovaryIntracellular → minimal IS benefit
💊 AE Treatment Protocol (Graus 2016)
  • 1st line (acute — start empirically): IVMP 1g/day ×5d + IVIG 2g/kg over 5d; PLEX 5-7 exchanges for severe/rapid deterioration
  • 2nd line (insufficient response at 2-4wk): Rituximab 375 mg/m² ×4 OR cyclophosphamide 750 mg/m² monthly
  • Maintenance (high relapse risk): MMF or azathioprine + low-dose pred ×2yr (anti-AMPAR, GABA-B, CASPR2 with tumor)
  • Seizure control: Levetiracetam, lacosamide, valproate; AEDs alone insufficient — IS essential; AVOID carbamazepine for LGI1 FBDS (use IS primarily)
  • Tumor search: CT chest/abdomen/pelvis + testicular US (men) + pelvic MRI (women); PET-CT if CT negative; repeat at 6-12mo

7. CNS Infections

CNS infections are neurological emergencies where delayed treatment dramatically worsens outcomes. This section covers bacterial meningitis, HSV encephalitis, TB meningitis, cryptococcal meningitis, and neurocysticercosis — with emphasis on empirical treatment timing and diagnostic algorithms.

7.1 Bacterial Meningitis — Empirical Treatment
🔑 TIME IS BRAIN: Every 1-hour delay in antibiotics increases mortality by 30%. DO NOT delay antibiotics for LP or CT. Draw 2 blood cultures → give antibiotics immediately if LP will be delayed >30 minutes.
CSF ParameterNormalBacterialViralTBCryptococcal
WBC<51,000-10,000 (PMN)100-1,000 (lymph)100-500 (lymph)20-200 (lymph)
Protein (mg/dL)<45>100 (very high)50-100>100 (very high)50-200
Glucose ratio>0.6<0.4 (low)Normal<0.5 (low)Low
Opening pressure<20 cmH₂OElevatedNormal/mild ↑ElevatedVery high (>25)
Special test—Gram stain (60-90%); culture; PCR multiplex (FilmArray)PCR (HSV, VZV, EV)AFB smear 10-40%; Xpert MTB/RIF (80%); ADA >10 U/LIndia ink (50%); CrAg (94%); culture
Above + Listeria monocytogenes
Age / SituationLikely OrganismsEmpirical Regimen
Adults <50y, communityS. pneumoniae (#1), N. meningitidisCeftriaxone 2g IV q12h + Vancomycin 15-20 mg/kg q8h + Dexamethasone 0.15 mg/kg q6h ×4d (BEFORE or WITH 1st antibiotic dose)
Age >50y, alcoholic, immunocompromisedAdd Ampicillin 2g IV q4h to above regimen (Listeria coverage; cephalosporins inadequate)
Post-neurosurgery / nosocomialS. aureus, GNB, AcinetobacterVancomycin + Cefepime or Meropenem
7.2 HSV Encephalitis & Viral CNS Infections
🎯 HSV Encephalitis — High-Yield Points
  • Classic triad: Fever + behavioral/personality change + temporal lobe features (olfactory hallucinations, déjà vu, complex partial seizures)
  • MRI: T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in medial temporal lobe ± insula ± cingulate cortex (bilateral 40%; unilateral more common early)
  • CSF HSV PCR: Sensitivity 98%, specificity 94%; may be false-negative in first 24-72h → repeat if negative with high suspicion
  • EEG: PLEDs (periodic lateralized epileptiform discharges) in temporal region
  • Start Acyclovir 10 mg/kg IV q8h EMPIRICALLY — do NOT wait for PCR results if clinically suspected
  • Duration: 14-21 days (21d if immunocompromised); adjust for renal function (CrCl-based)
  • Post-HSV autoimmune encephalitis: Anti-NMDAR encephalitis 2-8 weeks after HSV — if patient deteriorates post-treatment, check AE antibodies
VirusClinical FeaturesTreatment
VZV encephalitis/vasculopathyPost-dermatomal or no rash; elderly/immunocomp; stroke from vasculopathy (VZV vasculitis)Acyclovir 10-15 mg/kg q8h; prednisone for vasculopathy
CMV (immunocompromised)HIV CD4 <50; ventriculoencephalitis; polyradiculomyelitis; retinitisGanciclovir + foscarnet; valganciclovir maintenance
Japanese Encephalitis (JEV)Thailand endemic; Culex mosquito; PD-like features; bilateral thalamus + BG + SN on MRI; acute flaccid paralysisSupportive; JE vaccine (prevention)
RabiesAnimal bite → prodrome → furious (hydrophobia, aerophobia) or paralytic; 100% fatal once symptomaticPost-exposure: HRIG + vaccine series (day 0,3,7,14,28); Milwaukee protocol (experimental)
7.3 TB Meningitis & Cryptococcal Meningitis
💊 TBM Treatment (WHO Protocol)
  • Intensive phase (2 months): HRZE — Isoniazid 5 mg/kg + Rifampicin 10 mg/kg + Pyrazinamide 25 mg/kg + Ethambutol 15-20 mg/kg + Pyridoxine 25-50 mg/day
  • Continuation phase (7-10 months): HR — Total 9-12 months
  • Dexamethasone (essential): 0.4 mg/kg/day ×4wk → taper ×4wk; Thwaites 2004: mortality reduction in HIV-negative TBM
  • Hydrocephalus: communicating → acetazolamide; obstructive → EVD or VP shunt
  • Paradoxical reaction during treatment: ↑ lesion size → continue anti-TB + ↑ steroids
Cryptococcal Meningitis PhaseRegimenNotes
Induction (2 weeks)Amphotericin B deoxycholate 1 mg/kg/day IV + Flucytosine (5-FC) 100 mg/kg/day (4 divided doses)Prehydrate NS 1L before AmB; monitor K, Mg, Cr daily; 5-FC myelosuppressive → CBC; AMBITION trial 2022: single-dose liposomal AmB 10 mg/kg + fluconazole (resource-limited alternative)
Consolidation (8 weeks)Fluconazole 800 mg/day—
Maintenance (≥12mo)Fluconazole 200 mg/dayUntil CD4 >200 ×6mo in HIV
ICP management (critical)Daily therapeutic LPs: remove 20-30 mL until pressure <20 cmH₂O or symptoms resolve; VP shunt if chronicDO NOT use acetazolamide or corticosteroids for ICP in crypto meningitis — worsens outcome
7.4 Neurocysticercosis & CNS Parasites
NCC StageMRI FeaturesTreatment
Vesicular (viable cyst)Cystic lesion + scolex (dot sign); no edema; no enhancementAlbendazole 15 mg/kg/day ×8-30d (2 divided doses) + dexamethasone ± AED
Colloidal vesicular (dying)Ring enhancement + perilesional edema; scolex may be absentAlbendazole + dexamethasone (start 24h before antiparasitic) + AED
Granular nodularShrunken + ring enhancement (fading)Albendazole + dexamethasone + AED
Calcified nodular (inactive)Calcification; no enhancementNo antiparasitic needed; AED for seizure control; taper AED if 2yr seizure-free
Subarachnoid (racemose)Multilobulated grape-like cysts; basal cisterns/Sylvian fissure; hydrocephalusAlbendazole + praziquantel (combination); long course; dexamethasone; VP shunt for hydrocephalus
🔑 Cerebral Malaria (P. falciparum): Altered consciousness + seizures + coma; fever; thick blood smear. IV artesunate (preferred over quinine). Dexamethasone is CONTRAINDICATED in cerebral malaria — worsens outcome (increases coma duration, GI complications). Exchange transfusion for hyperparasitemia (>10%). Careful fluid management — pulmonary edema risk.

8. Dementia & Cognitive Disorders

Dementia affects 55 million people worldwide. This section covers Alzheimer's disease (ATN framework, anti-amyloid therapies), DLB McKeith 2017, FTD, NPH, and the reversible causes checklist — with emphasis on the rapidly changing treatment landscape.

8.1 Alzheimer's Disease — ATN Framework & Anti-Amyloid Therapy
🎯 NIA-AA 2018 ATN Biomarker Framework
  • A (Amyloid): CSF ↓ Aβ42/40 ratio; Amyloid PET (+); Plasma ↓ Aβ42/40 ratio
  • T (Tau — pathological): CSF ↑ p-tau181/p-tau217; Tau PET (+); Plasma ↑ p-tau217
  • N (Neurodegeneration): CSF ↑ total tau, NfL; FDG-PET hypometabolism (temporo-parietal); MRI hippocampal atrophy
  • Definitive biomarker diagnosis: A+T+ regardless of symptoms = Alzheimer's continuum
DrugClassIndicationTrialKey Notes
DonepezilAChEIMild-severe ADMultiple RCTs5→10 mg/day; 23 mg for moderate-severe; SE: GI, bradycardia, insomnia
RivastigmineAChEI (dual AChE+BuChE)Mild-moderate AD; DLB (preferred)RCTs including DLBOral or patch (less GI SE); 9.5→13.3 mg/24h patch
MemantineNMDA antagonistModerate-severe AD (MMSE 3-14)Multiple RCTs5→20 mg/day (BID); add-on to AChEI; dizziness, confusion
Lecanemab (Leqembi)Anti-Aβ protofibrils mAbMCI/mild AD + confirmed amyloid (CSF or PET)CLARITY-AD 2022: 27% slowing of CDR-SB decline at 18moIV biweekly; ARIA-E/H (MRI monitoring); APOE ε4/ε4 highest ARIA risk; FDA traditional approval Jan 2023
Donanemab (Kisunla)Anti-Aβ plaque (N3pG) mAbMCI/mild AD + amyloid confirmedTRAILBLAZER-ALZ2 2023: 35% slowing in low-intermediate tau groupMonthly IV; unique: stop dosing when amyloid cleared on PET; FDA approved July 2024; ARIA monitoring required
🔑 ARIA (Amyloid-Related Imaging Abnormalities): Major safety concern for anti-amyloid therapies. ARIA-E (edema/effusion — T2 FLAIR hyperintensity) and ARIA-H (microhemorrhages/siderosis — T2* or SWI). Most asymptomatic; symptomatic ARIA: headache, confusion, dizziness. Monitoring MRI at baseline, 2, 4, 7, 12 months. APOE ε4 homozygous: highest risk — requires enhanced monitoring and shared decision-making.
8.2 DLB, FTD & NPH
FeatureDLB (McKeith 2017)bvFTDNPH
Core features4 core (≥2 = probable): fluctuating cognition; recurrent VH (well-formed, vivid); RBD; ≥1 parkinsonism feature≥3 of 6: disinhibition; apathy; loss of empathy; perseverative/compulsive behavior; hyperorality; executive dysfunction (relative sparing of memory and visuospatial)Hakim-Adams triad: Wet + Wobbly + Wacky (urinary incontinence + magnetic gait + cognitive decline)
Key biomarkerDaTscan ↓ striatum; MIBG ↓ cardiac uptake; REM without atonia (PSG); FDG-PET occipital hypometabolismFrontal/temporal atrophy; FDG-PET frontotemporal hypometabolism; TDP-43/tau pathologyEvans' index >0.3; MRI: ventriculomegaly + tight high-convexity sulci + dilated Sylvian fissures

8.3 Reversible Dementias & Rapidly Progressive Dementia (RPD)
📋 Reversible Causes of Dementia — DEMENTIA mnemonic
  • Drugs (anticholinergics, BZD, opioids, AEDs, antihypertensives)
  • Electrolyte imbalance; Emotional (depression — pseudodementia)
  • Metabolic (hypothyroid, B12, folate, thiamine, niacin, copper deficiency)
  • Endocrine (Cushing's, Addison's, hypo/hyperparathyroid)
  • Neurological structural (NPH, SDH, brain tumor, PCNSL)
  • Toxic (heavy metals, alcohol, CO poisoning)
  • Infective (neurosyphilis, HIV, cryptococcal, TB, Whipple's, HSV)
  • Autoimmune (anti-NMDAR, LGI1, CASPR2, SREAT/Hashimoto encephalopathy — anti-TPO + steroid-responsive)
RPD CauseKey CluesDiagnostic Test
Prion disease (sCJD)Subacute decline; myoclonus; ataxia; visual symptoms; mean survival 4-6moMRI DWI: cortical ribboning + striatum (hockey stick); EEG: PSWCs at ~1Hz; CSF RT-QuIC (87%/100%); CSF 14-3-3 protein
Autoimmune encephalitisSubacute psychiatric + cognitive + seizures; any ageCSF + serum antibody panel (NMDAR, LGI1, Hu, CASPR2, etc.); MRI medial temporal T2 (LE)
CNS vasculitis/PACNSHeadache + multifocal strokes + cognitive decline; CSF pleocytosis; normal CRP/ESR sometimesMRI multifocal lesions; CSF lymphocytic pleocytosis; CTA/MRA beading; brain + leptomeningeal biopsy (gold standard)
Leptomeningeal carcinomatosisCancer history; multifocal CN palsies + radiculopathy + hydrocephalusCSF cytology (×3 LPs, 90% sensitivity); CSF cfDNA emerging; MRI nodular leptomeningeal enhancement

9. Sleep Disorders

Sleep disorders are prevalent and frequently under-recognized in neurological practice. This section covers OSA, narcolepsy (ICSD-3 criteria), RBD (as a synucleinopathy biomarker), insomnia (CBT-I first), and RLS — with specialist-depth pharmacotherapy.

9.1 OSA — Diagnosis & Treatment
FeatureDetails
ICSD-3 DiagnosisCriterion A (symptoms): EDS/non-restorative sleep/fatigue + PSG AHI ≥5/h; OR Criterion B: AHI ≥15/h (asymptomatic). Severity: Mild 5-14/h; Moderate 15-29/h; Severe ≥30/h
STOP-BANG screeningSnoring; Tired; Observed apnea; Pressure (HTN); BMI >35; Age >50; Neck >40cm; Male. Score ≥3 = high risk; ≥5 = very high risk for moderate-severe OSA
CPAP (1st line)AHI goal <5/h; adherence ≥4h/night ≥70% nights; Auto-CPAP (APAP) for most patients; BiPAP for COPD-OSA overlap or CPAP failure; telemedicine monitoring
AlternativesMandibular advancement device (mild-moderate; CPAP intolerant); Positional therapy (supine-predominant); Hypoglossal nerve stimulation (Inspire — AHI 15-65, no complete concentric collapse, CPAP intolerant); Weight loss; SURMOUNT-OSA 2024: tirzepatide significantly reduces AHI in obese OSA
9.2 Narcolepsy — ICSD-3 Criteria & Treatment
🎯 Narcolepsy Type 1 (NT1) Diagnostic Criteria
  • Daily EDS ≥3 months + ≥1 of:
  • (1) Cataplexy + MSLT: mean sleep latency ≤8 min + ≥2 SOREMPs (including nREM period <15 min)
  • (2) CSF orexin (hypocretin-1) ≤110 pg/mL (or <1/3 mean normal)
  • Pathophysiology: Autoimmune destruction of orexin neurons in lateral hypothalamus; post-H1N1 influenza/Pandemrix vaccine trigger; HLA-DQB1*06:02 >90% NT1
  • Cataplexy: Brief bilateral muscle weakness triggered by positive emotions (laughter, surprise); consciousness preserved — most specific for NT1
DrugTarget SymptomMechanism / Notes
Sodium oxybate (GHB)EDS + cataplexy (gold standard)GABA-B agonist; consolidates nighttime sleep; 2.25g BID QHS; REMS program; avoid alcohol; respiratory depression
ModafinilEDS (1st line)Weak DAT inhibitor; wake-promoting; 200-400 mg/day; no dependence; induces CYP3A4 → ↓ OCP efficacy
Pitolisant (Wakix)EDS + cataplexyH3 receptor inverse agonist → ↑ histamine release; non-scheduled; QTc monitoring; FDA approved
Venlafaxine / SSRIsCataplexy (off-label)REM-suppressing; venlafaxine 75-225 mg/day; NEVER stop abruptly — rebound cataplexy crisis
Sodium oxybate (lower-sodium, FT218)EDS + cataplexy + NT1/NT2Lumryz — once-nightly sodium oxybate; FDA 2023 for NT1 and NT2
9.3 RBD, Insomnia & RLS
🔑 REM Sleep Behavior Disorder (RBD) — Synucleinopathy Biomarker: ICSD-3: Repeated vocalization/complex motor behavior during REM sleep + PSG: REM without atonia (RSWA). Idiopathic RBD: 90% convert to PD, DLB, or MSA within 14 years (median ~10-12yr). DaTscan abnormal in 50-60% iRBD. Skin biopsy alpha-synuclein positive in 70-80%. Treatment: melatonin 3-12 mg QHS (safest); clonazepam 0.25-2 mg QHS (effective; caution elderly — falls). Bed safety essential (pad floor, remove sharp objects).
Condition1st LinePharmacotherapyKey Notes
Chronic InsomniaCBT-I (Cognitive Behavioral Therapy for Insomnia) — ALL guidelines recommend above pharmacotherapy; effects maintained at 2yr; digital CBT-I validatedOrexin antagonists (suvorexant/lemborexant) — preferred for chronic; Z-drugs (zolpidem) for short-term; doxepin 3-6 mg for sleep maintenance (elderly); ramelteon for sleep onset/circadian; Avoid chronic BZD (tolerance, dependence, falls, cognitive impairment)Components: Sleep restriction therapy (SRT) + stimulus control + sleep hygiene + cognitive restructuring + relaxation
RLS/Willis-EkbomIron if ferritin <75 μg/L (ferrous sulfate + vit C; IV ferric carboxymaltose if oral fails)Pramipexole 0.125→0.5 mg pre-bed (DA agonist; augmentation risk); Gabapentin/pregabalin (preferred if pain, insomnia, augmentation); Rotigotine patch (less augmentation)Augmentation (most important SE of DA agonists): symptoms earlier in day, spread to other limbs, more intense → switch to gabapentin or opioid; check iron

📚 Key Guidelines — Sleep

  • [G1] ICSD-3 — American Academy of Sleep Medicine 2014
  • [G2] ACP Clinical Guidelines: Chronic Insomnia — Recommend CBT-I as 1st-line pharmacotherapy (Annals IM 2016)
  • [G3] IRLSSG Consensus: RLS Diagnostic Criteria and Treatment 2022

10. Neuro-Oncology

Neuro-oncology integrates WHO 2021 molecular classification, standard-of-care treatment protocols, and rapidly evolving targeted therapies. This section covers primary brain tumors (gliomas, GBM, PCNSL, meningioma) and brain metastases at subspecialist depth.

10.1 WHO 2021 CNS Tumor Classification — Gliomas
🎯 WHO 2021 — Key Change: Integrated Histomolecular Diagnosis
  • Histology + molecular markers = integrated diagnosis: IDH status, 1p/19q codeletion, TERT promoter, EGFR, MGMT methylation, CDKN2A/B, H3 K27M/G34
  • IDH-wildtype with any of: EGFR amplification, +7/−10, TERT mutation → Grade 4 (GBM) regardless of histological grade
IDH mutation + 1p/19q codeletion + TERT mutation (triple-positive)
TumorWHO GradeMolecularMedian SurvivalTreatment
Astrocytoma, IDH-mutant2-4IDH1/2 mutation + ATRX loss + no 1p/19q; Grade 4 = CDKN2A/B homozygous deletionGrade 2: 10-15yr; Grade 4: 14-16moSurgery + RT + TMZ; vorasidenib (IDH1/2 inhibitor) — INDIGO trial 2023: ↑ PFS in grade 2 IDH-mutant glioma (FDA approved Aug 2024)
Oligodendroglioma, IDH-mutant, 1p/19q-codeleted2-3Grade 2: 12-15yr (best prognosis)Surgery + RT + PCV (procarbazine, CCNU, vincristine) — RTOG 9802/CATNON
Glioblastoma (GBM), IDH-wildtype4IDH-wildtype + EGFR amp / +7/−10 / TERT; MGMT methylation (30-40%) = treatment predictor15-18mo (MGMT+); 12-14mo (MGMT−)Stupp protocol: Surgery → RT 60Gy/30fr + TMZ 75mg/m²/day → TMZ 150-200mg/m² ×5/28d ×6 cycles. TTFields (Optune): ≥18h/day + TMZ → OS 20.9 vs 16mo (EF-14 trial 2015). Bevacizumab at recurrence (↑PFS; not OS)
10.2 PCNSL, Meningioma & Brain Metastases
TumorKey FeaturesTreatment
PCNSL (Primary CNS Lymphoma)DLBCL; periventricular; callosal; rapidly fatal untreated; immunocompromised or elderly; dramatic response to steroids (but confounds biopsy — defer steroids); MRI: homogeneous Gd+ ring in immunocompetent vs ring in immunocomp; CSF cytology, flow cytometry, IL-10NO surgical resection (biopsy only — stereotactic). HD-MTX 3.5 g/m² ± rituximab ± thiotepa ± ara-C (MATRix regimen); consolidation: HD-ASCT (eligible) or WBRT (elderly); steroids → dramatic but temporary response; restart after biopsy confirmed
MeningiomaWHO 1 (90%): benign, extra-axial, dural tail, MRI Gd+; WHO 2-3: aggressive, higher recurrence; molecular: NF2 (most common), TERT, H3K27me3, CDKN2A → WHO grade upgrading in 2021Observation (small, asymptomatic, elderly); surgery if symptomatic; RT for grade 2-3 or residual; no proven systemic therapy for grade 1-2; FAK inhibitor trials for refractory
Brain Metastases10× more common than primary brain tumors; most common: lung (40-50%) > breast (15-25%) > melanoma (5-20%); at gray-white junction; multiple1-3 lesions, good KPS: SRS ± surgery (Gamma Knife 15-24 Gy) — avoid WBRT if SRS feasible (QUARTZ trial: WBRT no benefit for NSCLC mets vs supportive care); WBRT 30Gy/10fr for multiple; hippocampal-sparing WBRT + memantine (cognitive preservation); targeted therapy for driver mutations: EGFR+ → osimertinib; ALK+ → alectinib/lorlatinib; BRAF V600E → dabrafenib+trametinib; HER2+ → T-DXd; Dexamethasone for symptomatic edema (4-8 mg q6-12h)

📚 Key Guidelines — Neuro-Oncology

  • [G1] WHO Classification of Tumors of the CNS 2021 — WHO Press
  • [T1] Stupp Trial 2005 — RT + TMZ for GBM. NEJM 2005;352:987
  • [T2] EF-14 Trial 2015 — TTFields + TMZ for GBM. JAMA 2015;314:2535
  • [T3] INDIGO Trial 2023 — Vorasidenib for IDH-mutant grade 2 glioma. NEJM 2023;389:589

11. Spinal Cord Disorders

Spinal cord disorders present as myelopathy — a combination of UMN and LMN features depending on the level. This section covers traumatic SCI (ASIA scale, acute management), non-traumatic myelopathies, and cauda equina syndrome.

11.1 SCI — ASIA Impairment Scale & Acute Management
ASIA GradeDescription
A — CompleteNo motor or sensory function preserved in sacral segments S4-5
B — Sensory incompleteSensory (not motor) preserved below NLI including S4-5
C — Motor incompleteMotor preserved below NLI; >50% key muscles below NLI have grade <3
D — Motor incompleteMotor preserved below NLI; ≥50% key muscles below NLI have grade ≥3
E — NormalMotor and sensory normal (may have hyperreflexia)
Below L1-L2; PNS
SCI SyndromeLocationClinical FeaturesPrognosis
Central cord (most common)Cervical cord; elderly + hyperextensionArms > legs weakness; bladder dysfunction; sensory below levelBest of incomplete syndromes; most recovery
Brown-Séquard (hemicord)Penetrating injury (ipsilateral)Ipsilateral motor + dorsal column loss; contralateral pain/temp lossBest functional prognosis
Anterior cordAnterior spinal artery (vascular)Motor + spinothalamic loss; preserved dorsal columns (vibration/proprioception)Worst prognosis; vascular etiology
Conus medullarisL1-L2 vertebralMixed UMN+LMN; saddle anesthesia; bladder/bowel/sexual dysfunctionVariable; mixed recovery
Cauda equinaPure LMN; asymmetric; saddle anesthesia; urinary retention → SURGICAL EMERGENCYGood if surgery within 24-48h
💊 Acute SCI Management
  • Hemodynamic target: MAP 85-90 mmHg ×7 days (avoid secondary cord ischemia); vasopressors for neurogenic shock (hypotension + bradycardia)
  • Respiratory: C3-C5 → phrenic nerve at risk; mandatory respiratory monitoring; NIV or early intubation for high injuries
  • Methylprednisolone: CONTROVERSIAL — NOT standard of care (ACS/AAN recommend against); individualized decision only if <8h; NASCIS protocol: 30 mg/kg bolus → 5.4 mg/kg/h ×23h
  • Surgical decompression <24h: STASCIS: earlier surgery → better neurological outcomes, especially incomplete SCI
  • Autonomic dysreflexia (chronic SCI >T6): BP surge >20-25 mmHg above baseline; trigger: bladder distension, pressure sores, bowel; Sit up; identify/remove trigger; nifedipine sublingual 10 mg or nitrates; prevent: scheduled CIC, bowel program
11.2 Non-Traumatic Myelopathies
CauseMRI FeaturesKey CluesTreatment
Cervical spondylotic myelopathyCord compression; T2 signal (myelomalacia) at compressed level; disc-osteophyte complexInsidious; neck pain + UMN legs + LMN arms; Lhermitte's; Hoffman sign; L'hermitte's with neck flexionSurgery (decompression) before severe cord damage; collar for mild
Subacute combined degeneration (B12)Posterior column T2 hyperintensity; may extend multiple segmentsLarge fiber neuropathy; Lhermitte's; B12 <200; ↑↑↑ MMA + homocysteine; history of malabsorption, veganism, nitrous oxideIM cyanocobalamin 1mg/day ×1wk → 1mg/wk ×4 → 1mg/mo; improvement expected in 3-6mo
Spinal dural AVFT2 hyperintensity with flow voids; perimedullary enhancement; MR angiography shows lesion; middle-aged men; lower thoracic/conusProgressive myelopathy; worsens with exercise/standing; no trauma historyEndovascular embolization or surgical ligation
HTLV-1 myelopathy (HAM/TSP)T2 thoracic cord; atrophy late; HTLV-1 serology in CSF and blood positiveEndemic: Thailand (Northeast), Japan, Caribbean; slowly progressive spastic paraparesis; bladder symptomsPrednisolone; IFN-α; symptomatic (baclofen for spasticity)
Transverse myelitisLETM (≥3 segments, central, AQP4+) vs partial TM (<50% cross-section, peripheral, MS-type)Acute; bilateral motor + sensory + autonomic; LETM → NMOSD until proven otherwiseIVMP 1g/day ×5d; PLEX if refractory; send AQP4-IgG, MOG-IgG, anti-MBP for diagnosis

📚 Key Guidelines — Spinal Cord

  • [G1] ASIA/ISCoS International Standards for Neurological Classification 2019
  • [G2] ACS/AAN Position on High-Dose MP for SCI — Against routine use (2013)
  • [T1] STASCIS Study 2012 — Early (<24h) surgical decompression improves neurological outcomes in cervical SCI. Fehlings MG et al., J Neurotrauma

12. Neurological Emergencies

Neurological emergencies require rapid recognition and time-critical intervention. This section integrates stroke (Section 1 cross-reference), status epilepticus (Section 2), myasthenic crisis (Section 5) with raised ICP management, brain death determination, and PRES.

12.1 Raised Intracranial Pressure — Management Algorithm
🔑 Cushing Triad = Imminent Herniation: Hypertension + bradycardia + irregular respiration. Normal ICP: 5-15 mmHg. Treat when ICP >20 mmHg. Uncal herniation: Ipsilateral CN III palsy (fixed dilated pupil) + contralateral hemiplegia → bilateral motor posturing — surgical emergency.
TierInterventionTarget / Details
Tier 0 (All patients)HOB 30°; avoid hypotension (MAP 70-90 mmHg); avoid hypoxia (SpO₂ ≥95%); normothermia; euglycemia; analgesia/sedation; treat seizures; avoid excessive PEEP—
Tier 1 (ICP >20 mmHg)Mannitol 20% 0.5-1 g/kg IV over 15-20 min (q4-6h; target serum osm <320; osmolar gap <20) OR Hypertonic saline (HTS) 23.4% 30-60 mL IV bolus (target Na 150-160 mEq/L); EVD (external ventricular drain) — ICP monitoring + CSF drainageHTS preferred for traumatic brain injury; mannitol for cerebral edema; choose based on patient factors; avoid combination without guidance
Tier 2 (Refractory to Tier 1)Mild hyperventilation PaCO₂ 30-35 mmHg (temporary bridge — avoid <30 → cerebral ischemia); NMB if agitation causing ICP spikes; temperature 35-36°CHyperventilation: onset within minutes; duration short (receptor upregulation); DO NOT use as primary long-term strategy
Tier 3 (Refractory)Pentobarbital coma: 5-20 mg/kg loading → 1-4 mg/kg/h (burst suppression on cEEG); Decompressive craniectomy (malignant MCA infarct <48h, age <60y — HAMLET/DECIMAL/DESTINY trials: ↓ mortality but ↑ severe disability; family discussion essential)Barb coma: risk hypotension + infection; craniectomy: discuss functional outcome vs survival with family
12.2 Brain Death Determination (AAN 2010)
🎯 Brain Death — 3 Clinical Requirements (All Required)
  • Prerequisites: Irreversible cause established; no CNS depressants; temp >36°C; normal BP; no NMB; no metabolic abnormality
  • 1. Coma: No purposeful response; no response to noxious stimulation above foramen magnum
  • 2. Absent brainstem reflexes (ALL): Pupils fixed (4-9 mm) + no corneal + no VOR (cold caloric: 50 mL ice water; no eye deviation) + no oculocephalic (Doll's eyes) + no gag/cough to ET suction + no grimace
  • 3. Apnea test: Preoxygenate → disconnect vent → O₂ via ET → observe 8-10 min → no respiratory effort + PaCO₂ ≥60 mmHg (or ↑20 mmHg from baseline) = positive
Ancillary TestFindingWhen Needed
EEGElectrocerebral silence (flat; no response to stimulation)Cannot complete clinical exam; drug interference
Tc-99m HMPAO SPECTNo cerebral perfusion ("hollow skull")Most reliable; gold standard ancillary
CTA / 4-vessel angiographyNo intracranial filling above carotid/vertebral entryRapidly available alternative
TCD (Transcranial Doppler)Reverberating flow or spike pattern bilaterallyBedside; depends on operator skill; bilateral required
12.3 PRES & Hypertensive Encephalopathy
FeaturePRES Details
ClinicalHeadache + visual disturbance (cortical blindness, homonymous hemianopia) + seizures + altered consciousness + hypertension; usually reversible if treated promptly
MRISymmetric T2/FLAIR vasogenic edema; posterior > anterior (occipital, parietal predominant); may be hemorrhagic; resolves 2-4 weeks with treatment; "FLAIR dot sign" for cytotoxic component (worse prognosis)
CausesSevere hypertension; eclampsia/preeclampsia (most common); calcineurin inhibitors (cyclosporine, tacrolimus); chemotherapy (cisplatin, bevacizumab, VEGF-TKIs); autoimmune disease; sepsis/MAHA; transplant
PathophysiologyFailed cerebrovascular autoregulation → breakthrough hyperperfusion → vasogenic edema (posterior circulation less sympathetic innervation = more vulnerable); OR endothelial toxicity → cytotoxic + vasogenic (calcineurin inhibitors)
TreatmentBP reduction: 15-25% in first hour; IV labetalol (20-40 mg q10-15 min or drip 2 mg/min) or nicardipine 5-15 mg/h; Eclampsia: IV magnesium sulfate 4g load → 1-2g/h + delivery; Stop offending drug; AEDs if seizures persist; 90% full neurological recovery if BP controlled promptly
EmergencyTime-Critical RecognitionImmediate Action
Acute LVO strokeSudden focal deficit; high NIHSStPA ±EVT (Section 1); door-to-needle <60 min; door-to-groin <90 min
SAH thunderclapWorst headache of life; maximal at onsetCT head; LP if CT negative at 6-12h; CTA; neurosurgical consult; nimodipine 60 mg q4h (vasospasm prevention)
Status epilepticusSeizure ≥5 min or recurrent without recoveryBenzodiazepine → 2nd-line ASM → anesthetic infusion (Section 2.5)
Bacterial meningitisFever + headache + neck stiffness + altered MSBlood cultures → antibiotics + dexamethasone IMMEDIATELY (Section 7.1)
Myasthenic crisisProgressive bulbar/respiratory failure in MGFVC monitoring; intubate if FVC <15 mL/kg; PLEX or IVIG (Section 5.1)
Spinal cord compressionBack pain + acute myelopathy signsUrgent MRI; dexamethasone 10 mg IV → 4 mg q6h; surgical consult; Cauda equina = EMERGENCY
Brain herniationDeclining GCS; fixed dilated pupil; posturingHOB 30°; mannitol/HTS; hyperventilation (temporary); EVD; neurosurgical decompression
